Qada — Judiciary
intermediate
6 min
Child's Status When Mother is Claimed: Evidence vs. Acknowledgment
Ruling on child's inclusion in ownership claims with evidence vs. acknowledgment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the status of a child when the mother is claimed with evidence versus acknowledgment, and explain the difference in retroactive effect of proof.

Core Idea
With evidence, the child follows the mother retroactively; with acknowledgment, the child does not.

This lesson examines a classical Hanafi ruling on a claim of ownership over a slave-girl who has given birth. The critical distinction is whether the claimant presents evidence (bayyinah) or merely acknowledges the current owner's possession. The ruling depends on the nature of proof: evidence establishes ownership from the beginning, while acknowledgment only from the moment of acknowledgment. This distinction also affects the buyer's recourse for the price.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Scenario and Ruling
Understand the hypothetical: a man buys a slave-girl, she gives birth, then a claimant appears. If he brings evidence, he takes both mother and child. If he acknowledges the buyer's ownership, he takes only the mother, not the child.
The child is an accessory (tabi') to the mother.
2
Analyze the Nature of Bayyinah vs. Iqrar
Evidence is an absolute proof (hujjah mutlaqah): it establishes ownership retroactively to the time of the original transaction. Acknowledgment is a restricted proof (hujjah qasirah): it only proves ownership from the moment of acknowledgment, not earlier.
This difference is why the child is taken in one case and not the other.
3
Apply the Principle of Retroactivity
Since bayyinah shows ownership from the origin (min al-asl), the child, who was attached to the mother at that time, belongs to the claimant. With iqrar, ownership is established only after the child's birth and separation, so the child is not included.
Hanafi usul: Al-bayyinah hujjah mutlaqah, al-iqrar hujjah qasirah.
4
Examine the Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Some Hanafi scholars say the child is automatically included in the judgment on the mother. Others (including Muhammad al-Shaybani) require the judge to explicitly rule on the child, especially if the judge is unaware of the child or the child is in another's hand.
The text cites Kamal al-Dirayah supporting the need for explicit judgment.
5
Determine the Mufta Bihi
The relied-upon position is that with bayyinah, the child is automatically taken as an accessory, but the judge should exercise caution. In practice, explicit judgment is recommended to avoid disputes.
The text weighs toward requiring explicit judgment per Muhammad al-Shaybani.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أفصل في الاستحقاق ( ويؤخذ الولد إن استحقت أمة ببينة، وإن أقربها لا)، صورتها : اشترى رجل جاريةً، فولدت عنده فاستحقها رجل ببينة، فإنَّه يأخذها وولدها، وإن أقر بها لا ؛ لأنَّ البينة حجة مطلقة فيظهر بها ملكه من الأصل ١ ] قوله : لأن ... الخ ؛ توضيحه : إن البينة حجّة مطلقة فيثبت بها الملك من الأصل، وبهذا يرجع المشتري على البائع بالثمن في الاستحقاق بالبينة، وقد كان الولد متصلاً بأمه يومئذ فيثبت بالبينة الاستحقاق فيها. والإقرار حجة قاصرة يثبت به الملك في المخبر به ضرورة صحة الخبر، وقد اندفعت الضرورة بإثباتِ الاستحقاق بعد انفصال الولد ، فلا يظهر الاستحقاق في الولد ؛ ولهذا لا يرجعُ المشتري على البائع بالثمن في الاستحقاق بالقرار. وذكر التَّمُرْتَاشِيُّ : أنَّ الولد إنّما لا يدخلُ في الإقرار إذا لم يدعه المقر له، أما إذا ادعاه فإنه يكون له، ثمّ في البينة يكون القضاء بالأم عند البعض ؛ لأن الولد تابع لها ، فيدخلُ في الحكم عليها. وقيل : لا بُدَّ من القضاء بالولد ، وإليه أشار محمد الله حيث قال : إذا قضى القاضي بالأصل المستحق ولم يعلم بالزَّوائدِ لم تدخل الزَّوائد تحت القضاء، وكذا إن كانت الزوائد في يد آخر، وهو غائب، لم تدخل تحت القضاء ؛ لانفصالها عن الأصل يوم القضاء، ذكره في «كمال الدراية). [٢] قوله : حجة مطلقة ؛ حتى تظهر في حق الكافّة؛ لأنَّ البينة تصير حجَّةً بالقضاء، وللقاضي ولاية عامة فيتعدّى إلى الكل. [٣]قوله : فيظهر بها ؛ فإنَّ الملك لا بدَّ له من زمان، وليس الزَّمانُ أولوية بالتعين، فيظهر الملك من الأصل، يعني من وقت الشراء لا في الحال ؛ لأنَّ الشهود لا يتمكنون من إثبات ما لم يكن ثابتاً، والولد كان متصلاً بالأم يوم الشراء فيأخذها. (۱) «كمال الدراية (ق٤١٨).
Chapter on Claim/Entitlement. (And the child is taken if his mother is claimed with evidence, but if he acknowledges her, then not.) Its scenario: A man bought a slave-girl, and she gave birth at his place, then a man claimed her with evidence; he takes her and her child. But if he acknowledged her, he does not take the child; because evidence is an absolute proof, and through it his ownership is established from the origin. [1] His saying: 'Because...etc.'; its clarification: Evidence is an absolute proof, so ownership is proven from the origin by it. By this, the buyer can recover the price from the seller in the case of claim by evidence. And the child was attached to its mother at that time, so the claim is established in her by evidence. Acknowledgment is a restricted proof; it establishes ownership in the thing acknowledged only out of necessity for the validity of the report, and the necessity is removed by establishing the claim after the separation of the child, so the claim is not established in the child. For this reason, the buyer does not recover the price from the seller in the case of claim by acknowledgment. Al-Tamurtashi mentioned that the child is not included in the acknowledgment only if the acknowledged does not claim it; but if he claims it, then it becomes his. Then, regarding evidence, some say the judgment is concerning the mother because the child follows her, so it enters under the judgment upon her. And it is said: The judgment must be upon the child explicitly, and Muhammad Allah indicated this when he said: If the judge gives judgment on the principal claimed and is not aware of the accessories, the accessories do not come under the judgment. Similarly, if the accessories are in the hand of another and that person is absent, they do not come under the judgment because they were separate from the principal on the day of judgment. Mentioned in 'Kamal al-Dirayah'. [2] His saying: 'absolute proof'; meaning it appears regarding everyone, because evidence becomes proof by the judge's ruling, and the judge has general authority, so it extends to all. [3] His saying: 'appears through it'; because ownership must have a time, and time has no priority for specification, so ownership appears from the origin, meaning from the time of purchase, not at present; because witnesses cannot prove what was not established, and the child was attached to the mother on the day of purchase, so he takes it. (1) 'Kamal al-Dirayah' (Q 418).
Source pages p. 233

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on what happens when a claimant proves ownership of a slave-girl with evidence (bayyinah) or acknowledges it (iqrar). If he brings evidence, the judge awards him the mother and her child, because evidence establishes ownership from the beginning (from the time of the original sale). The child was born during the buyer's possession, but since the mother was owned by the claimant from the start, the child is also his. On the other hand, if the claimant merely acknowledges that the current owner (the buyer) owns the slave-girl (i.e., he does not claim ownership but admits it), then he cannot take the child. Acknowledgment is a restricted proof: it only establishes ownership as of the moment of acknowledgment, not retroactively. Since the child was born after the mother came into the buyer's possession and before the acknowledgment, the child does not become the claimant's. The commentary also discusses a dispute: some say in the case of evidence, the judge only rules on the mother and the child is automatically included; others say the judge must explicitly rule on the child. The text sides with the latter view, citing a principle from Kamal al-Dirayah that accessories (like a child) are not included in a judgment on the principal unless the judge is aware of them and they are in the same hand. This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.

Key Terms
الاستحقاق (al-istihqaq): A claim of ownership or entitlement, usually in a court.
البينة (al-bayyinah): Legal evidence, typically two male witnesses, establishing a claim.
الإقرار (al-iqrar): Acknowledgment or confession, a restricted proof that does not affect third parties retroactively.
الولد (al-walad): Child of a slave-girl; follows the mother's status in ownership claims.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the child not follow the mother when ownership is established by acknowledgment (iqrar) as opposed to evidence (bayyinah)?
A) Because acknowledgment is not a valid proof in court.
B) Because acknowledgment only establishes ownership from the time of acknowledgment, not from the beginning, so the child born before is not included.
C) Because the child is unrelated to the ownership claim.
D) Because the judge must always rule separately on the child.

Key Takeaway
The difference between absolute proof (bayyinah) and restricted proof (iqrar) determines whether the child is included in a claim on the mother: bayyinah reaches back, iqrar does not.

The Linguistic and Technical Definition of Qada'
Defines qada' linguistically and legally

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the linguistic and technical definitions of qada' and state the Hanafi juristic meaning.

Core Idea
Qada' linguistically means many things; technically it means the binding settlement of disputes.

This passage opens the Book of Judicature (Kitab al-Qada) by justifying its placement after transactions and then delves into the word's rich linguistic heritage. Understanding these definitions is essential because the same term carries different meanings in everyday language, worship, and judicial contexts. We will walk through the lexical meanings, the technical distinction between ada' and qada' in worship, and the precise legal definition used by Hanafi jurists.

Guided Path
1
Identify the placement rationale
The author explains why Kitab al-Qada follows transactions: most disputes arise from sales and debts, and judgment cuts off those disputes. Note the purpose: to resolve conflicts.
2
Parse the linguistic meanings
From Al-Misbah and Al-Bahr, list the various meanings: judgment, fulfillment, performance, completion, destruction, etc. Recognize that qada' has a broad semantic range in Arabic.
The word is a verbal noun (masdar) from qada-y.
3
Distinguish technical usage in worship
The passage notes a fiqh convention: 'ada' is performing worship in its time, while 'qada' is performing it outside its time. This is a technical distinction, not the original linguistic meaning.
This distinction is unique to Islamic legal terminology.
4
Extract the legal (shar'i) definition
From Al-Muhit, Al-Fath, and Al-Bada'i': the legal meaning is 'the settlement of disputes and cutting off of contentions' or 'binding (ilzam)' or 'judging between people by the truth.' Identify which part is decisive or based on explicit text.
The definition in Al-Bada'i' begins to classify evidence but is cut off.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب القضاء [1] قوله : كتاب القضاء ؛ لما كان أكثر المنازعات تقع في البياعات والديون عقبها بما يقطعها، وهو قضاء القاضي، ولم يقل كتاب أدب القاضي، كما عنون به صاحبُ «الهداية» (۱) ؛ نظراً إلى أن بيان القضاء مقصود، وبيان الأدب متبوع. والقضاء أصله قضاي ؛ لأنه من قضيت إلا أن الياء لما جاءت بعد الألف همزت. وقال في «المصباح» : قضيتُ بين الخصمين وعليهما حكمت بينهما، وقضيت وطري : بَلَغْتُه ونلته ، وقضيتُ الحاجة كذلك ، وقضيتُ الحج والدين : أديته. قال الله : فَإِذَا قَضَيْتُم مَّنَسِكَكُمْ ) : أي أديتموها، فالقضاء هاهنا بمعنى الأداء ، كما في قوله له : فَإِذَا قَضَيْتُمُ الصَّلَوةَ ) : أي أديتم ، واستعمل القضاء في العبادة التي تفعلُ خارج وقتها المحدود شرعاً، والأداه إذا فعلت في الوقت المحدود، وهو مخالف للوضع اللغوي ، لكنَّه اصطلاحي للتميز بين الوقتين ، والقضاء مصدر في الكل. انتهى. وقال في «البحر ) : إنه يستعمل لغة بمعنى الحكم والفراغ والهلاكِ والأداء والإنهاء والمضي والصنع والتقدير. انتهى. وأما معناه الشرعي ففصلُ الخصومات وقطعُ المنازعات، نقله في «البحر» عن «المحيط». وعَرَّفَهُ في «الفتح») : بالإلزام. وفي «البدائع ) : بالحكم بين الناس بالحق وهو الثابت عند الله علة من حكم الحادثات : إما قطعاً بأن كان عليه دليل قطعي، وهو النص المفسر من الكتاب والسنَّةِ
The Book of Judicature. [1] His saying: The Book of Judicature. Since most disputes occur in sales and debts, it follows them with what cuts them off, which is the judge's judgment. He did not say 'The Book of the Etiquette of the Judge', as the author of Al-Hidayah titled it, considering that explaining judgment is the objective, and explaining etiquette is subordinate. The word qada' originally is 'qadāy' because it is from 'qadaytu', but the ya' when it comes after alif is hamzated. And he said in Al-Misbah: 'I judged between the two litigants and against them, meaning I ruled between them. 'Qadaytu wataran': I reached it and attained it. 'Qadaytu al-hājata': similarly. 'Qadaytu al-hajja wad-dayna': I performed/fulfilled it.' Allah said: 'When you have performed your rites' meaning fulfilled them. So qada here is in the sense of performance, as in His saying: 'When you have performed the prayer' meaning performed it. And qada is used for worship performed outside its legally specified time, while adā' is used for worship performed within its specified time. This differs from the linguistic usage but is a technical distinction to differentiate the two times. Qada is a verbal noun in all cases. End. And he said in Al-Bahr: It is used linguistically in the sense of judgment, completion, destruction, performance, termination, passage, making, and determination. End. As for its legal meaning: it is the settlement of disputes and cutting off of contentions, as quoted in Al-Bahr from Al-Muhīt. And he defined it in Al-Fath as 'binding (ilzām)'. And in Al-Badā'i': as 'judging between people by the truth, which is what is established with Allah, either decisively when there is decisive evidence, which is the explicit text from the Book and Sunnah,'
Source pages p. 413

Commentary
This passage opens the Book of Judicature (Kitab al-Qada) by explaining why it follows the book of transactions: because most disputes arise in sales and debts, and judgement cuts off those disputes. The author justifies using the title 'Kitab al-Qada' rather than 'Adab al-Qadi' (etiquette of the judge) because the primary purpose is to explain the rules of judgment, with etiquette being secondary. The word 'qada' is then parsed: its root is q-d-y, and the ya' is hamzated because it follows an alif. Linguistic meanings are enumerated from Al-Misbah: judgment, fulfillment, performance, etc. The Quranic verses (2:200, 4:103) are cited where qada means 'performed' (ada'). A technical distinction is noted: in fiqh, 'ada' means performing worship in its time, while 'qada' means performing it outside its time, though this is a legal convention not a linguistic one. From Al-Bahr, additional linguistic meanings: judgment, completion, destruction, performance, termination, passage, making, and determination. The legal (shar'i) meaning is given as 'the settlement of disputes and cutting off of contentions' (from Al-Muhit), or 'binding' (from Al-Fath), or 'judging between people by the truth, which is established before Allah...' (from Al-Bada'i'). The definition from Al-Bada'i' begins to classify the basis of judgment as either decisive (qat'i) based on explicit text, but the passage cuts off mid-sentence.

Key Terms
القضاء (al-qada'): Linguistically: judgment, fulfillment, performance, completion, etc. Technically in fiqh: the settlement of disputes and cutting off of contentions; also defined as binding (ilzam) or judging between people by the truth.
كتاب القضاء (Kitab al-Qada'): The section of fiqh literature dealing with the rules of judicial decision-making, judgeship, and court procedures.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why did the author of 'Umdat al-Ri'aya title this section 'Kitab al-Qada' instead of 'Adab al-Qadi'?
A) Because the book is about judges only.
B) Because explaining judgment itself is the primary objective, while etiquette is subordinate.
C) Because the author disliked the term 'Adab al-Qadi'.
D) Because the book is only for judges.

Key Takeaway
Qada' in Islamic law refers to the binding settlement of disputes, distinct from its broader linguistic meanings and its specific usage in worship.

Qada — Judiciary

8 min
The Definition of Qada: Linguistic and Legal
Defines qada linguistically and legally

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the linguistic and legal definitions of qada and distinguish between the Hanafi definitions from Al-Muhit, Al-Fath, Al-Bada'i, and Allamah Qasim.

Core Idea
Qada means imposing a binding ruling in ijtihadi matters to resolve disputes.

The Hanafi school begins the Book of Judiciary by defining its subject matter. Understanding the precise definition of qada is crucial because it determines the scope of judicial authority. This passage explores the linguistic roots and legal meanings, showing how different Hanafi authorities articulated the essence of adjudication.

Guided Path
1
Analyze Linguistic Meanings
Examine the root q-d-y and its various usages: judgment, performance, completion. Note how the jurists distinguish between qada (after time) and ada (within time).
The terminological distinction is a convention, not pure Arabic.
2
Identify Legal Definitions
Locate the legal definitions quoted from Al-Muhit ('deciding disputes'), Al-Fath ('imposition'), Al-Bada'i ('judging with truth'), and Allamah Qasim ('imposition of obligation in ijtihadi matters').
Hanafis emphasize ijtihadi matters and worldly disputes.
3
Compare the Definitions
Note that Al-Muhit focuses on process, Al-Fath on binding nature, Al-Bada'i on truth, and Qasim on limitation to ijtihad.
All definitions cohere around resolving conflicts authoritatively.
4
Understand the Ijtihad Condition
Allamah Qasim explicitly restricts qada to matters of ijtihad, excluding matters of consensus and purely devotional acts.
This highlights the judge's role in areas open to interpretation.

Book of Judiciary. Because most disputes occur in sales and debts, he followed them with what puts an end to them, which is the judgment of the judge. He did not say 'Book of Etiquette of the Judge' as the author of Al-Hidayah titled it, considering that explaining judgment is the objective and explaining etiquette is subordinate. The prophets judged by it' and He said: 'And judge between them by what Allah has revealed and do not follow their desires'. The judge is the deputy of Allah on His earth in giving justice to the oppressed against the oppressor, delivering rights to those entitled, and enjoining good and forbidding evil. Then, judiciary is of five types: 1. Obligatory when the post is specific to him and no one else is qualified; because if he does not do it, it leads to wasting judgment, so accepting it becomes enjoining good and forbidding evil and giving justice to the oppressed. 2. Recommended when someone else is qualified but he is more qualified and better at it. 3. Permissible when he and others are equal in qualification and execution. 4. Disliked when he is qualified for the judgeship but someone else is more qualified and better at it. 5. Source pages p. 413

Commentary
This passage from the commentary 'Umdat al-Riayah' serves as an introduction to the Book of Judiciary. It opens by explaining why the author of the base text placed the book of judiciary after transactions: because most disputes arise in sales and debts, and the judge's decision cuts those disputes. The author then presents a linguistic analysis of the word 'qada', citing Al-Misbah and Al-Bahr, which enumerate meanings including judgment, completion, performance, and determination. Distinction is made between 'qada' (performing an act after its time) and 'ada' (within its time); this is a terminological convention of the jurists, not pure Arabic. Legally, qada is defined in various ways by Hanafi authorities: Al-Bahr quotes Al-Muhit: 'deciding disputes and cutting conflicts'; Al-Fath defines it as 'imposition'; Al-Bada'i as 'judging between people with truth'. A more detailed definition by Allamah Qasim limits qada to matters of ijtihad concerning worldly disputes. The passage then extols qada as the best of acts, the affair of every prophet, and cites Quranic proofs (5:44, 5:49) to show its divine mandate. The judge is Allah's deputy for establishing justice. Finally, the passage lists five rulings for accepting the position of judge: wajib, mustahabb, mukhayyar, makruh, and haram. Each category is based on the presence and qualification of other suitable candidates. The judge's stipend is to be taken from the public treasury to avoid temptation. This careful classification shows that the Hanafi school does not view judicial office as uniformly obligatory or recommended; its ruling depends on circumstances.

Key Terms
القضاء (al-qadaa): The legal term for judiciary: deciding disputes and terminating conflicts, or imposing a ruling between people with truth.
فصل الخصومات (fasl al-khusumat): The legal definition of qada: deciding disputes and cutting conflicts.
إلزام (ilzam): Imposition, making a ruling binding on the parties.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which definition of qada explicitly limits it to matters of ijtihad?
A) Al-Muhit's definition
B) Al-Fath's definition
C) Al-Bada'i's definition
D) Allamah Qasim's definition

Key Takeaway
Qada is a binding imposition on ijtihadi matters to resolve disputes, with its ruling on accepting the office varying by circumstance.

Qada — Judiciary
beginner
6 min
The Five Rulings for Accepting Judgeship
Lists five categories of accepting judgeship

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the five categories of ruling (wajib, mustahabb, mukhayyar, makruh, haram) for accepting judgeship and state the condition for each.

Core Idea
Accepting judgeship is not always recommended; its ruling depends on qualifications and alternatives.

After defining qada, the passage classifies the hukm (ruling) of accepting the position of judge. This classification reflects the Hanafi school's nuanced approach: judicial office can be an obligation, a recommendation, a choice, a disliked act, or even forbidden, depending on the individual's circumstances and the presence of other qualified candidates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Five Categories
List the five rulings: wajib, mustahabb, mukhayyar, makruh, haram. Note the condition given for each.
These are based on personal capacity and availability of others.
2
Understand the Wajib Condition
Judgeship is wajib when the person is uniquely suited and no one else is qualified. Why? To prevent wasting judgment and injustice.
This is an exceptional obligation due to necessity.
3
Explore the Haram Condition
It is haram when the person knows of his inability or lack of inner fairness (following desire). Self-knowledge of incompetence is key.
This protects the public from an unjust judge.
4
Connect to Public Treasury
The judge's salary is taken from the public treasury to prevent corruption, as seen in the example of Umar.
This is a practical safeguard for judicial integrity.

Commentary
This passage from the commentary 'Umdat al-Riayah' serves as an introduction to the Book of Judiciary. It opens by explaining why the author of the base text placed the book of judiciary after transactions: because most disputes arise in sales and debts, and the judge's decision cuts those disputes. The author then presents a linguistic analysis of the word 'qada', citing Al-Misbah and Al-Bahr, which enumerate meanings including judgment, completion, performance, and determination. Distinction is made between 'qada' (performing an act after its time) and 'ada' (within its time); this is a terminological convention of the jurists, not pure Arabic. Legally, qada is defined in various ways by Hanafi authorities: Al-Bahr quotes Al-Muhit: 'deciding disputes and cutting conflicts'; Al-Fath defines it as 'imposition'; Al-Bada'i as 'judging between people with truth'. A more detailed definition by Allamah Qasim limits qada to matters of ijtihad concerning worldly disputes. The passage then extols qada as the best of acts, the affair of every prophet, and cites Quranic proofs (5:44, 5:49) to show its divine mandate. The judge is Allah's deputy for establishing justice. Finally, the passage lists five rulings for accepting the position of judge: wajib, mustahabb, mukhayyar, makruh, and haram. Each category is based on the presence and qualification of other suitable candidates. The judge's stipend is to be taken from the public treasury to avoid temptation. This careful classification shows that the Hanafi school does not view judicial office as uniformly obligatory or recommended; its ruling depends on circumstances.

Key Terms
واجب (waajib): In this context, accepting the judgeship is obligatory when the post is uniquely designated for the person and no one else is qualified.
مستحب (mustahabb): Recommended, when others are qualified but the person is more suitable.
مخير (mukhayyar): Permissible/option, when he and others are equal in qualification.
مكروه (makruh): Disliked, when he is qualified but others are more qualified.
حرام (haraam): Forbidden, when he knows of his own inability or lack of fairness.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Accepting judgeship carries a variable ruling from wajib to haram based on the individual's qualifications and alternatives.

Qada — Judiciary
intermediate
6 min
Five Categories of Accepting Judgeship (Qada) in Hanafi Fiqh
Defining qada, its virtue, and its five legal categories

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the five legal categories of accepting the position of judge and determine which category applies to a given scenario.

Core Idea
Accepting judgeship can be obligatory, recommended, permissible, disliked, or forbidden—depending on one's qualifications and alternatives.

In this passage, the author defines qada (judgeship) and extols its virtue as the deputy of Allah. He then categorizes the ruling for accepting the position into five distinct categories, based on the person's ability and the presence of others. This classification is central to the Hanafi view on the judiciary, ensuring that justice is served without personal harm.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition of qada
The passage defines qada as 'creating an obligation in closely related matters of ijtihad in which dispute occurs for worldly interests.' Note that it excludes matters of consensus and pure acts of worship, confining qada to ijtihadi disputes.
This definition sets the scope of judicial authority.
2
Recognize the virtue of qada
The author cites Quran 5:44 and 5:49 to show that judging by divine revelation is a prophetic duty, and states that the judge is Allah's deputy. This establishes the high rank and responsibility of the office.
The virtue motivates the detailed rulings on acceptance.
3
Identify the five categories
Read each category carefully: wajib (obligatory when uniquely needed), mustahabb (recommended when more capable), mubah (permissible when equal), makruh (disliked when less capable), haram (forbidden when unjust or incapable). Understand the condition that triggers each ruling.
The condition is the deciding factor.
4
Apply the categories to scenarios
Practice matching a person's situation to the correct ruling. For example: if a qualified judge is the only one available, accepting becomes wajib; if he knows he is biased, it is haram.
Concrete application solidifies the classification.
5
Note the salary ruling
The passage states that the judge's provision must come from the public treasury (Bayt al-Mal) because he is occupied for the public good, and lacking salary might lead to corruption. Historical examples from Umar and Ali are cited.
This ensures judicial independence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعرفه العلامة قاسم بأنه إنشاء إلزام في مسائل الاجتهاد المتقاربة فيما يقعُ فيه النزاع لمصالح الدُّنيا، فخرج القضاء على خلاف الإجماع، وما ليس بحادثة من العبادات، وعلى هذا القياس عرَّفوه بعبارات مختلفة، وإشارات مؤتلفة. وهو أفضل العبادات، وهم أمرُ كلِّ نبي ، قال الله له : إِنَّا أَنزَلْنَا التَّوْرَاةَ فِيهَا هُدًى وَنُورٌ يَحْكُمُ بِهَا النَّبِيُّونَ (١) ، وقال له : وَأَن أَحْكُم بَيْنَهُم بِمَا أَنزَلَ اللَّهُ وَلَا تَتَّبِعْ أَهْوَاءَهُمْ (٢) ، ولولا ذلك لفسد العباد، وخربت البلاد، وانتشر الظلم والفساد، والحاكم نائب الله في أرضه في إنصاف المظلوم من الظالم، وإيصال الحق إلى المستحق، والأمر بالمعروف والنهي عن المنكر. ثم القضاء على خمسة أوجه :
١. واجب : وهو أن يتعيّن له، ولا يوجد من يصلح له غيره ؛ لأنه إذا لم يفعل أدّى إلى تضييع الحكم، فيكون قبوله أمراً بالمعروف ونهياً عن المنكر، وإنصاف المظلوم من الظالم.
٢. ومستحب : وهو أن يوجد من يصلح له غيرُه ، لكن هو أصلح وأقوم به.
٣. ومخير فيه : وهو أن يستوي هو وغيره في الصلاحية والقيام به.
٤. ومكروه : وهو أن يكون صالحاً للقضاء لكن غيره أصلح وأقوم به.
٥. وحرام وهو أن يعلم من نفسه العجز وعدم الإنصاف فيه في باطنه من اتباع الهوى بما لا يعرفه. ورزقُ القاضي وكفايته وكفاية أهلِهِ وأعوانِهِ ومن يمولهم يكون من بيت المال ؛ لأنه محبوس لحق العامة، فلولا الكفالة ربَّما يطمع في أموال الناس ، وإن عمر له أعطى شريحاً كل شهر مئة درهم ، وأعطاه علي له كل شهر خمسمئة درهم. كذا في كتب السير والحديث.
And Allama Qasim defined it as: creating an obligation in closely related matters of ijtihad in which dispute occurs for worldly interests. So it excludes judgment contrary to consensus and what is not an incident from acts of worship. Based on this analogy, they defined it with various expressions and harmonious indications. It is the best of acts of worship, and it is the command of every prophet. Allah said to him: 'Indeed, We sent down the Torah, in which was guidance and light. The prophets judged by it' (5:44), and said to him: 'And judge between them by what Allah has revealed and do not follow their desires' (5:49). Were it not for that, the servants would become corrupt, the lands would be ruined, and oppression and corruption would spread. The ruler (judge) is the deputy of Allah in His earth in giving justice to the oppressed from the oppressor, delivering rights to those entitled, commanding good and forbidding evil. Then judgment is of five types:
1. Obligatory: when the position is specific to him and no one else is suitable, because if he does not act it leads to wasting judgment, so his acceptance becomes commanding good and forbidding evil and giving justice to the oppressed from the oppressor.
2. Recommended: when someone else is suitable, but he is more suitable and better at it.
3. Permissible/optional: when he and another are equal in suitability and capability.
4. Disliked: when he is suitable for judgment but someone else is more suitable and better.
5. Forbidden: when he knows within himself that he is incapable and lacks fairness due to following desires in ways he does not recognize. The judge's provision and sufficiency for himself, his family, his assistants, and those he supports is from the treasury (Bayt al-Mal), because he is detained for the right of the public, and without provision he might covet people's wealth. Indeed, Umar gave Shuraih one hundred dirhams each month, and Ali gave him five hundred dirhams each month. Thus in the books of Sirah and Hadith.
Source pages p. 414

Commentary
This passage begins by defining the term 'qada' (judgeship) according to Allama Qasim, a Hanafi scholar. It is described as 'creating an obligation' in matters of ijtihad related to worldly disputes, thereby excluding matters of consensus and pure acts of worship. The passage then extols the virtue of judgeship, citing Quranic verses (5:44 and 5:49) to show that judgment by divine law is the way of prophets and prevents corruption. The judge is Allah's deputy on earth. The core ruling is the classification of accepting the position of judge into five categories based on one's circumstances: obligatory (wajib) when one is uniquely qualified, recommended (mustahabb) when one is more suitable than others, permissible (mubah) when equally qualified with others, disliked (makruh) when less suitable than another, and forbidden (haram) when one knows oneself to be unjust or incapable. The passage also details that the judge's salary is to be paid from the public treasury (Bayt al-Mal), as the judge is detained for the public good, and provides historical examples from the caliphs Umar and Ali. The author follows the order of first defining the term, then stating its virtue, then listing the categories, and finally addressing the judge's remuneration.

Key Terms
قضاء (qada'): Judgeship; the office of adjudicating disputes according to Islamic law.
واجب (wajib): Obligatory; here it refers to the ruling that accepting judgeship becomes obligatory when the person is uniquely qualified and needed.
بيت المال (bayt al-mal): The public treasury from which the judge's salary and expenses are paid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In which situation is accepting the position of judge considered forbidden (haram) according to the Hanafi school?
A) When no one else is qualified
B) When one is equally qualified with others
C) When one knows oneself to be incapable and unjust
D) When one is less qualified than another

Key Takeaway
Accepting judicial office has five rulings based on the individual's qualifications and the availability of others; the condition of capability is paramount.

The Qiyas Linking Testimony and Judgeship Qualifications
Commentary proving equivalence via analogy with subsidiary application

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the qiyas jaliy that derives judgeship qualification conditions from testimony qualification conditions, and apply those conditions to a given scenario.

Core Idea
Testimony and judgeship share wilayah and ilzam, so their qualification conditions are identical.

This passage from a Hanafi commentary on al-Wiqayah uses a clear analogy to link two key institutions: testimony (shahadah) and judgeship (qada). The commentator explains that both involve authority over others and the power to bind, so the same personal qualifications apply. This lesson unpacks that qiyas and the specific conditions required.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main claim
The base text asserts: 'The one qualified for testimony is qualified for judgeship.' Identify this as the matn being explained.
2
Extract the qiyas structure
The commentator gives two reasons: both are wilayah (authority) and both are ilzam (binding). Recognize these as the effective cause (illa) for the analogy between testimony and judgeship.
Qiyas jaliy: asl = testimony, hukm asl = conditions, far' = judgeship, illa = wilayah & ilzam, hukm far' = same conditions.
3
List the conditions derived
The passage lists: Islam, intellect, puberty, freedom, not blind, not a convicted slanderer (qadhf). These conditions come from the requirements for valid testimony.
Some conditions (e.g., sight) may have nuances; the poem adds eloquent speech and appointment.
4
Apply the rule to a subsidiary case
The passage then applies the rule: an enemy's testimony is inadmissible (worldly enmity), so if such a person is appointed judge, his judgment is invalid. Note the fatwa and scholarly opinions cited.
This shows the practical consequence of the equivalence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأهل للشهادةِ أهل للقضاءِ ، وشرط أهليتها شرط أهليته لا
قوله : الأهلُ للشهادةِ أَهل للقضاء ؛ لأنَّ كلا منهما من باب الولاية ؛ تنفيذ القول على الغير ؛ ولأنَّ كلاً منهما إلزام ، إذ الشهادة ملزمة على القاضي ، والقضاء ملزم على الخصم، فما يشترط لأهليّة الشهادة يشترط لأهلية القضاء، والعدو لا تقبل شهادته على عدوه إذا كانت دنيوية، ولو قضى القاضي بها لا ينفذ، ذكره يعقوب باشا له.
فلا يصح قضاؤه عليه لما تقرَّرَ أنَّ أهلَه أهلُ الشهادة ، وبه أفتى مفتي مصر شيخُ الإسلام أمينُ الدِّين بن عبد العال، وكذا سجل العدو لا يقبلُ على عدوه، ثم نقل عن «شرح الوهبانية»: إنّه لم ير نقلها عندنا ، وينبغي النفاذ لو القاضي . عدلاً. وقال ابن وهبان له بحثاً : إن يعلمه لم يجز، وإن شهادة العدول بمحضر من الناس جاز، واعتمده القاضي محب الدين الله في «منظومته». كذا في «الدر المختار) ، و«منح الغفار»)، وغيرهما. [٢] قوله : وشرط أهليتها ؛ يعني شروط أهليّة الشهادة من الإسلام والعقل والبلوغ والحرية وعدم العمى والحدّ في قذف وغيرها، شروط لصحة تولية القضاء، وقد نظمها الحموي فقال : شروط القضاء تسع عليك بحفظها لِتُحْرزَ سبقاً في طلاك العُلا بلوغ وإسلام وعقل و منطق فصیح به فصل الخصومة قد حلا تولية حكماً دون سمع لدعوة وحرية سمع والأبصار قد تلا (٣) وفقدان حد القذف قد شرط واله كذا قال زين الدين في «البحر» (٣) مُجملا
The one qualified for testimony is qualified for judgeship, and the condition of its qualification is the condition of his qualification. No.
His saying: 'The one qualified for testimony is qualified for judgeship' because both come under the category of authority (wilayah): executing a statement upon another; and because both are binding (ilzam), since testimony is binding upon the judge, and judgment is binding upon the opponent. So what is required for the qualification of testimony is required for the qualification of judgeship. The testimony of an enemy is not accepted against his enemy if it is worldly, and if the judge passes judgment based on it, it is not executed. Ya'qub Pasha mentioned it.
Thus his judgment is not valid against him because it has been established that his qualification is the qualification of testimony. And the Mufti of Egypt, Shaykh al-Islam Amin al-Din ibn Abd al-Aal, issued a fatwa accordingly. Likewise, the record of an enemy is not accepted against his enemy. Then he narrated from 'Sharh al-Wahbaniyyah' that he did not see its transmission with us, and it should be executed if the judge is just. Ibn Wahban said, upon research: if he knows it, it is not permissible, but if testimony of upright people in the presence of people is given, it is permissible, and the judge Muhibb al-Din Allah relied on it in his poem. Thus in al-Durr al-Mukhtar, Manh al-Ghaffar, and others. His saying: 'And the condition of its qualification' means the conditions of qualification for testimony: Islam, intellect, puberty, freedom, not being blind, and absence of hadd for qadhf, and others. These are conditions for the validity of appointing a judge. Al-Hamawi has versified them: 'The conditions of judgeship are nine, memorize them / to gain precedence in attaining high status. / Puberty, Islam, intellect, and eloquent speech / by which the separation of disputes is sweet. / Appointment as a judge [without hearing?] and freedom, hearing and sight follow. / And absence of hadd for qadhf is a condition over them.' Thus said Zayn al-Din in al-Bahr, concisely.
Source pages p. 415

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn (base text) statement: 'The one qualified for testimony is qualified for judgeship, and the condition of its qualification is the condition of his qualification.' The commentator explains the reasoning: both testimony and judgeship involve authority (wilayah) and binding effect (ilzam). Therefore, the same conditions of qualification apply: Islam, intellect, puberty, freedom, not being blind, and not having been punished for false accusation (qadhf). The passage then discusses a subsidiary issue: the testimony of an enemy against his enemy in worldly matters is not accepted, and if a judge rules based on it, the ruling is not enforceable. Various Hanafi authorities are cited, including a fatwa from the Mufti of Egypt. The passage concludes with a poetic summary by al-Hamawi listing nine conditions for judgeship: puberty, Islam, intellect, articulate speech, appointment (possibly?), freedom, hearing, sight, and absence of hadd for qadhf. The commentary follows the order of the matn: first explaining the general rule, then the condition regarding enemies, then listing the conditions.

Key Terms
شهادة (shahadah): Testimony given before a judge to establish a right or claim.
عدو ('aduw): An enemy; someone with whom the witness has hostility in worldly matters, making testimony against them inadmissible.
ولاية (wilayah): Authority; the legal power to enforce a decision or obligation upon another.
إلزام (ilzam): Binding effect; the quality of an act that creates a legal obligation.
حد القذف (hadd al-qadhf): The prescribed punishment for false accusation of adultery.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the primary proof used in the passage to establish that the one qualified for testimony is also qualified for judgeship?
A) A direct Quranic verse
B) Consensus of the Companions
C) Analogy (qiyas) based on shared attribute of authority (wilayah) and binding effect (ilzam)
D) A statement of Imam Abu Hanifa directly

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school derives the qualifications for judgeship from those for testimony via qiyas jaliy based on the shared attributes of wilayah and ilzam, requiring the judge to meet the same conditions of Islam, intellect, puberty, freedom, not blind, and not a convicted slanderer, with the added disqualification of enmity.

Eligibility for Judiciary Requires Same Eligibility as Testimony
Ruling linking eligibility for testimony and judiciary with conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the nine conditions for a judge's appointment according to the Hanafi school and explain why they are derived from the conditions for testimony.

Core Idea
Eligibility for judiciary is the same as for testimony because both involve binding authority (wilaya wa ilzam).

This passage establishes a key principle in Hanafi judicial theory: the qualifications for a judge mirror those for a witness. The author provides legal reasoning (ta'lil) that both roles involve enforcement of statements over others (ilzam). The commentary then lists the specific conditions, confirming that the same nine requirements apply, and addresses the special case of enmity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling and its logical basis
The ruling states: 'The one eligible for testimony is eligible for judiciary.' The basis is qiyas: both testimony and judiciary involve authority (wilaya) and binding enforcement (ilzam).
The Hanafi school uses this qiyas to link the two, rather than requiring separate qualifications.
2
Examine the reasoning (ta'lil) in detail
Testimony binds the judge (the judge must accept or reject), and judiciary binds the litigant. Therefore, the same capacity is needed to perform both functions.
This is the core ta'lil: both involve 'ilzam' – making a statement binding on another.
3
Note the exception: enmity as a disqualifier
The text discusses that an enemy's testimony against his enemy is not accepted, and a judge who is an enemy cannot validly judge the other. If the judge is unaware of enmity, there is a difference of opinion about enforcement.
Enmity is treated as a preventer (mani') that disqualifies both testimony and judiciary.
4
List the nine conditions from the poem
Al-Hamawi's poem enumerates: puberty (bulugh), Islam, intellect ('aql), eloquent speech (fasahah), ability to adjudicate (knowledge of fiqh), freedom (hurriyyah), hearing (sam'), sight (basar), and absence of hadd for false accusation of zina (qadhf).
These are the shurut (conditions) for validity of appointment.
5
Understand the practical application (mufta bihi)
The listed conditions are the mufta bihi (relied-upon) for a judge's appointment. The ruling is taken from al-Durr al-Mukhtar and Manh al-Ghaffar. A judge lacking any condition cannot be validly appointed.
This is the standard Hanafi position from zahir al-riwayah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

فلا يصح قضاؤه عليه لما تقرَّرَ أنَّ أهلَه أهلُ الشهادة ، وبه أفتى مفتي مصر شيخُ الإسلام أمينُ الدِّين بن عبد العال، وكذا سجل العدو لا يقبلُ على عدوه، ثم نقل عن «شرح الوهبانية»: إنّه لم ير نقلها عندنا ، وينبغي النفاذ لو القاضي عدلاً. وقال ابن وهبان له بحثاً : إن يعلمه لم يجز، وإن شهادة العدول بمحضر من الناس جاز، واعتمده القاضي محب الدين الله في «منظومته». كذا في «الدر المختار) ، و«منح الغفار»)، وغيرهما. Book of Judiciary and Testimony. The one eligible for testimony is eligible for judiciary, and the condition of its eligibility is the condition of his eligibility. His saying: 'The one eligible for testimony is eligible for judiciary' because each of them is from the category of authority: enforcement of a statement against another; and because each of them is a binding, for testimony is binding on the judge, and judiciary is binding on the opponent. So what is required for the eligibility of testimony is required for the eligibility of judiciary. And the enemy's testimony against his enemy is not accepted if it is worldly, and if the judge passes judgment with it, it is not enforceable, mentioned by Ya'qub Pasha. So his judgment against him is not valid according to what has been established that his eligibility is the eligibility of testimony, and the Mufti of Egypt, Shaykh al-Islam Amin al-Din ibn Abd al-Aal, gave fatwa accordingly. Likewise, the enemy's record is not accepted against his enemy. Then he quoted from 'Sharh al-Wahbaniyya': 'He said he did not see its transmission with us, and it should be enforced if the judge is just.' And Ibn Wahban said in his research: if he knows him (to be an enemy), it is not permissible, but if the testimony of just people in the presence of the people (is accepted), it is permissible, and it was relied upon by the judge Muhibb al-Din Allah in his 'Manzumah'. This is in 'al-Durr al-Mukhtar', 'Manh al-Ghaffar', and others. Source pages p. 415

Commentary
This is a ruling from the matn on the relationship between testimony and judiciary. The commentator expands by stating the ruling: the person who is qualified to give testimony is similarly qualified to be a judge. The proof is that both are forms of authority (wilaya) involving the enforcement of a statement over others, and both are binding (ilzam): testimony binds the judge, and the judge's judgment binds the litigant. Therefore, the same conditions for testimony apply to being a judge. The text then addresses the specific case of an enemy: if there is enmity, the testimony is not accepted, and a judge who is an enemy cannot validly judge the other. It also discusses opinions on whether the judge's ignorance of enmity affects enforcement. The conditions for testimony are listed: Islam, intellect, puberty, freedom, not being blind, not having been punished for qadhf (false accusation of adultery), etc. These are summarized in the poetic lines by al-Hamawi, listing nine conditions: puberty, Islam, intellect, eloquence, ability to adjudicate without hearing a claim (i.e., knowledge), freedom, hearing, sight, and absence of hadd for qadhf. This is the Hanafi position as stated in the reliable sources.

Key Terms
أهلية (ahliyyah): Legal capacity; fitness to perform a legal act.
شهادة (shahada): Testimony given in court, which is binding on the judge.
قضاء (qada): Judiciary; the office of judging and issuing binding verdicts.
ولاية (wilaya): Authority; the power to enforce rulings over others.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Because of ijma'

Key Takeaway
Eligibility for judiciary is identical to eligibility for testimony, and the nine conditions for a judge are derived from that principle.

The Fasiq as Judge and the Just Judge Who Becomes Fasiq
Ruling on appointing a fasiq judge and intra-Hanafi dispute on removal of a judge who becomes fasiq

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the appointment of a fasiq as judge and explain the intra-madhhab dispute on whether a just judge who becomes fasiq is automatically removed.

Core Idea
Appointing a fasiq is valid but sinful; a judge who becomes fasiq deserves removal—but not all agree.

This lesson examines two related issues in Hanafi judicial theory: the eligibility of a sinner (fasiq) to be appointed as judge, and the fate of a judge who becomes sinful after appointment. The Hanafi school adopts a nuanced position: while a fasiq is legally capable of holding office, appointing him is a sin. More controversially, if a sitting judge falls into sin, the majority of Hanafi scholars hold that he must be removed, but a minority argues for automatic disqualification. Understanding this dispute sharpens your appreciation of how the school balances formal validity with moral integrity.

Guided Path
1
State the ruling on appointing a fasiq
Identify the two-part ruling: the appointment is valid (sahih), but it is sinful (haram) to appoint him.
This is the apparent school position.
2
Understand the analogy with testimony
The text compares the fasiq's appointment to his testimony: valid in capacity but not to be accepted. Trace the parallel: just as testimony of a fasiq is binding if accepted by a judge, so is his judicial appointment.
The analogy shows that eligibility exists despite sin.
3
Identify the dispute on the judge who becomes fasiq
Note the ikhtilaf: the majority (mashayikhuna) say he 'deserves removal' (istahaqq al-'azl); some scholars (Karkhi, Tahawi) say he is not automatically removed.
Intra-Hanafi dispute on whether fisq automatically invalidates the judgeship or requires a new decree.
4
Analyze the evidence for each side
The majority cite the principle that a fasiq cannot be trusted, similar to rejection of his testimony. The minority argue that removal requires a formal act; moral change does not undo past rulings.
The difference hinges on whether fisq is a permanent disqualification or a matter of trustworthiness that must be formally addressed.
5
Determine the mufta bihi and practical consequence
The relied-upon position is that removal is obligatory (wajib) upon the ruler. Thus, the fasiq judge's rulings after fisq are not valid until he is reappointed, unless the ruler delays removal.
The practical effect: the judge's actions become void if the ruler removes him; if not removed, his rulings are enforced but he is sinful.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

والتفصيل في المبسوطات.

وهذا يقتضي نفوذ أحكامه فيما ارتشى فيه، وفي غيره ما لم يعزل ، وإليه أشار
And the fasiq is eligible for it (judgeship), his appointment is valid, but he should not be appointed, just as his testimony is accepted (in terms of validity) but not accepted (in practice). And if a just person becomes fasiq, he deserves removal in the apparent school, and our scholars (say) this. And according to some scholars, he is not removed. [1] His saying: 'And the fasiq' means the Muslim who commits a major sin and persists in a minor sin. It is said in 'al-Ma'dan': Fasiq is the one who exits from the command of Allah by committing major sins such as listening to singing and dancing, taking bribes, etc. It will come in the Book of Testimony if Allah wills. 'Eligible for it' means for judgeship, so his appointment is valid, and it implies that the judgment of a person of concealed status (mastur) is valid without ugliness, mentioned by al-Quhistani quoting from 'al-Kashf'. 'And the fasiq should not be appointed' means it is obligatory that he not be appointed, as he is not trusted due to lack of concern because of his fisq. So if he is appointed, the appointer is sinful, just as his testimony is valid (in terms of capacity) because the basis of eligibility for testimony exists, but it is not accepted for the reason mentioned. So even if the judge accepts it and judges by it, he is sinful, but it is enforced. This is in 'al-Manh'. And it is said in 'al-Durar': This is if he (the judge) is strongly of the opinion that he (the fasiq) is truthful, and he is careful. End. The detail is in the extended works. [2] His saying: 'His appointment' – taqlid is placing a necklace around the neck, and legally: a ruling indicating that someone is a judge in a certain place. This is in 'Jami' al-Rumuz' and others. [3] His saying: 'And if the just judge becomes fasiq' means he becomes fasiq by taking bribery, adultery, drinking wine, etc. after being just, then the judge deserves removal necessarily: i.e., it is obligatory for the sultan to remove him. This is in 'al-Fusul al-Imadiyya'. And this implies the enforcement of his rulings in the matter he took a bribe for, and in other matters as long as he is not removed, and he indicated this.
Source pages p. 416

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling on a fasiq (sinner) as a judge and the case of a just judge who becomes fasiq. The text begins with a general statement: a fasiq is eligible (ahl) for judgeship, so his appointment (taqlid) is valid (sahih), but he should not be appointed (la yuqallad), meaning it is obligatory not to appoint him. If appointed, the appointing authority commits sin. This is analogous to testimony: a fasiq's testimony is valid in the sense that if accepted by a judge, it is binding, but it should not be accepted. The commentator explains that 'fasiq' here means a Muslim who commits major sins (like bribery, listening to music) or persists in minor sins. The word 'ahl' indicates that even a person of concealed status (mastur, whose inner state is unknown but outwardly Muslim) can validly act as judge. However, a fasiq should not be appointed because he cannot be trusted due to his lack of piety. If a judge does accept the testimony of a fasiq and issues a ruling, it is enforced even though the judge is sinful. This is the view in al-Manh. The second part addresses the scenario where a just judge becomes fasiq after appointment. According to the apparent school (zahir al-madhhab) and the majority of our scholars (mashayikhuna), he deserves removal (istahaqq al-'azl), and it is obligatory for the sultan to remove him. However, some scholars hold that he is not automatically removed. This difference is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf. The commentator notes that his rulings in matters he took bribes for are not enforced, but other rulings remain in force as long as he is not formally removed.

Key Terms
فاسق (fasiq): A Muslim who commits major sins or persists in minor sins.
تقليد (taqlid): Appointment of a judge; legally, a statement indicating that someone is a judge in a particular place.
عزل ('azl): Removal from office; here, removal of a judge who becomes fasiq.
عدل ('adl): A just person; one who avoids major sins and does not persist in minor sins, maintaining uprightness.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the majority Hanafi view (mashayikhuna), what is the status of a just judge who becomes fasiq?
A) He is automatically removed and all his rulings are void.
B) He deserves removal but his rulings are enforced until formally removed.
C) He is not removed and his rulings remain valid.
D) He is removed but his past rulings remain valid.

Key Takeaway
The fasiq is technically eligible for judgeship, but appointing him is sinful; while a just judge who becomes fasiq deserves removal, his rulings remain valid until formal removal—an intra-Hanafi nuance.

Appointing a Fasiq as Qadi and the Effect of Becoming Fasiq
Intra-Hanafi dispute on qadi removal upon fisq

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on appointing a fasiq as qadi and distinguish between the majority view (zahir al-riwayah) and the minority view (al-Tahawi, Ali al-Razi) regarding automatic removal upon fisq.

Core Idea
A fasiq can be a valid judge, but it is sinful to appoint him; a just judge who becomes fasiq is not automatically removed according to the majority.

This lesson examines a crucial issue in Hanafi qada (judgeship): the capacity (ahliyyah) of a fasiq (sinner) to serve as a judge, and what happens when a judge who was just becomes fasiq. The text from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah clarifies that appointment of a fasiq is valid but sinful, and then navigates an intra-Hanafi dispute on automatic removal versus deserving removal. Understanding this distinction is essential for grasping the hierarchy of rulings within the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core issue
Recognise that the passage addresses two sub-issues: (1) appointing a fasiq initially, and (2) a just judge who becomes fasiq. The first part is clear: he is qualified but must not be appointed; if appointed, it is sinful but his judgments are enforceable.
The analogy with testimony (shahadah) is used to explain the duality of validity and sin.
2
Understand the majority position (zahir al-riwayah)
The majority of Hanafi scholars (especially from Bukhara and Samarkand) hold that a qadi who becomes fasiq is not automatically removed (la yan'azilu) but rather deserves removal (yastaḥiqq al-ʿazl). The sultan must remove him actively.
This is supported by Imam al-Bazdawi and al-Zayla'i.
3
Understand the minority position (al-Tahawi and Ali al-Razi)
Some Hanafi scholars, notably al-Tahawi and Ali al-Razi, argue that the qadi is automatically removed upon becoming fasiq because the appointment was conditional on his justice, like a slave permitted to trade who loses permission if he runs away.
The text says 'wa alayhi al-fatwa' (the fatwa is upon this view), but later sources claim the madhhab is contrary.
4
Analyse the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf)
If automatic removal is accepted, all judgments after fisq are invalid. If not, judgments in matters unrelated to the bribe remain valid until the sultan removes the judge. This has serious implications for the enforceability of court decisions.
The passage also notes that if a judge takes a bribe to secure his appointment, he does not become a judge at all.
5
Evaluate the tarjih (preferred view)
The text presents both views but leans towards the majority (zahir al-riwayah) as the 'madhhab', despite noting that the fatwa is on automatic removal. Later commentators like al-Zayla'i confirm that the school does not accept automatic removal.
This demonstrates that fatwa may sometimes differ from the established school position due to practical considerations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والفاسق أهل له، يصح تقليده، ولا يقلد، كما صح قبول شهادتِهِ، ولا تُقْبَلُ. ولو فَسَقَ العدل استحق العزل في ظاهر المذهب، وعليه مشايخنا. وعند بعض المشايخ لا يَنْعَزل. قوله: والفاسق؛ أي المسلم الذي أقدم على كبيرة، وأصر على صغيرة، قال في «المعدن»: الفسقُ هو الخروج من أمر الله بارتكاب الكبيرة كاستماع الغناء والرقص، وأخذ الرشوة وغير ذلك. انتهى. وسيأتيك تحقيقه في «كتاب الشهادة» إن شاء الله. أهل له: أي للقضاء، فيصحُ تقليده، وفيه إشعار بأنَّ قضاء المستور صحيح بلا قبح. ولا يقلد الفاسق: أي يجب أن لا يقلد إذ لا يؤمن عليه؛ لقلة المبالاة بواسطة فسقه، حتى لو قلّد يأثم كما يصح قبول شهادته؛ لوجود أصل الأهلية للشهادة، ولا تقبل لما ذكر، حتى لو قبل القاضي وحكم بها كان آثما، لكنّه ينفذ. والتقليد جعلُ القلادة في العنق، وشرعاً: حكم دال بكون فلان قاضياً في موضع كذا. ولو فسق القاضي العدل؛ أي صار فاسقاً بأخذ الرشوة أو الزنا أو شرب الخمر أو غير ذلك بعد كونه عدلاً استحق القاضي للعزل وجوباً: أي يجب على السلطان عزله. وهذا يقتضي نفوذ أحكامه فيما ارتشى فيه، وفي غيره ما لم يعزل، وإليه أشار الإمامُ البَزْدَوِي. فاستحقاق العزل ثابت في ظاهر المذهب، وعليه مشايخنا البحاريون والسمرقنديون. وعند بعض المشايخ ينعزل؛ لأنَّ المقلد اعتمد عدالته، فيتقيد التقليد بحال عدالته، فصار كأنه علق بقاء قضاء القاضي بحال عدالته، فلما فسق لم يبق التقليد لارتفاع العدالة. وهو اختيار الطحاوي وعلي الرازي صاحب أبي يوسف، وعليه الفتوى. لكن قال في «البحر» بعد نقله: والمذهب خلافه. انتهى. وقال الزيلعي في «شرح الكنز»: ولو كان القاضي عادلاً ففسق بأخذ الرشوة لا ينعزل، ويستحق العزل، وإذا أخذ القضاء بالرشوة لا يصير قاضياً، وكذا القضى بالرشوة لا ينفذ قضاؤه فيما ارتشى. وقال بعض مشايخنا: إذا قلد الفاسق ابتداءً يصح، ولو قلد وهو عدل ينعزل بالفسق؛ لأنَّ المقلّد اعتمد عدالته فلم يكن راضياً دونه، كالعبد المأذون له في التجارة إذا أبق ينعزل، ولو أذن له وهو آبق جاز. وعن علمائنا الثلاثةِ في «النوادر»: إنّ الفاسق لا يصلح قاضياً، والظاهر هو الأول. ولا ينبغي أن يكون القاضي فظا غليظاً جباراً عنيداً، وينبغي أن يكون موثوقاً به في دينه، وعفافه وعقله، وصلاحه وفهمه وعلمه بالسنة والآثار ووجوه الفقه، وكذا المفتي.
And the fasiq is qualified for it, his appointment is valid, but he should not be appointed, just as his testimony is validly accepted, but it is not accepted. And if the just person becomes fasiq, he deserves removal in the apparent position of the school, and our scholars are upon this. And according to some scholars, he is not removed. His statement: 'and the fasiq' means the Muslim who commits a major sin and persists in a minor sin. He said in 'al-Ma'din': fisq is departing from the command of Allah by committing a major sin like listening to singing and dancing, taking bribes, and so on. End. Soon you will receive its verification in the Book of Testimony, Allah willing. 'Qualified for it' means for judgment; so his appointment is valid, and there is an indication that the judgment of one whose condition is hidden is valid without being ugly. 'And the fasiq should not be appointed' means it is obligatory not to appoint him, because one cannot be safe from him due to his lack of concern because of his fisq, to the extent that if he is appointed, he sins, just as his testimony is validly accepted due to the existence of the basic capacity for testimony, but it is not accepted for the reason mentioned, to the extent that if the judge accepts it and judges by it, he is sinful, but it is executed. And 'taqlid' is placing a necklace on the neck, and legally: a ruling indicating that so-and-so is a judge in such a place. And if the just judge becomes fasiq, i.e., becomes fasiq by taking a bribe or adultery or drinking wine or otherwise after being just, the judge deserves removal obligatorily: i.e., it is obligatory upon the sultan to remove him. This implies the enforcement of his rulings in the matter he took a bribe for, and in other matters as long as he is not removed, and Imam al-Bazdawi pointed to this. So deserving removal is established in the apparent position of the school, and our scholars from Bukhara and Samarkand are upon this. And according to some scholars, he is removed; because the appointor relied on his justice, so the appointment is tied to his state of justice, as if he conditioned the continuation of the judge's judgment on his state of justice, so when he became fasiq, the appointment no longer remained due to the removal of justice. This is the choice of al-Tahawi and Ali al-Razi, the companion of Abu Yusuf, and the fatwa is upon it. But he said in 'al-Bahr' after quoting it: the school is contrary to it. End. And al-Zayla'i said in 'Sharh al-Kanz': If the judge was just and then became fasiq by taking a bribe, he is not removed, and he deserves removal, and if he took the judgeship by bribe, he does not become a judge, and likewise, if he judges by bribe, his judgment does not execute in the matter he took a bribe for. And some of our scholars said: If a fasiq is appointed initially, it is valid, and if he was appointed while just, he is removed by fisq; because the appointor relied on his justice, so he was not satisfied without it, like a slave permitted to trade, if he runs away, he is removed, but if he is permitted while being a runaway, it is valid. And from our three scholars in 'al-Nawadir': That the fasiq is not suitable as a judge, and the apparent is the first. And the judge should not be harsh, rude, tyrannical, or stubborn, and he should be trustworthy in his religion, chastity, intellect, righteousness, understanding, knowledge of the Sunnah, traditions, and aspects of fiqh, and likewise the mufti.
Source pages p. 416

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah' is a commentary on 'Sharh al-Wiqayah', addressing the rulings related to appointing a fasiq (sinner) as a qadi (judge) and the effect of a qadi becoming fasiq. The author begins by stating that a fasiq is, in terms of basic capacity, eligible for judgeship, so his appointment is valid (sahih). However, it is obligatory (wajib) not to appoint him because his fisq makes him unreliable. If he is appointed, the appointing authority sins, but the appointment is effective and his judgments are enforceable. This parallels the rule for a fasiq's testimony (shahadah): he has the basic capacity to testify, and if a judge accepts it, it is executed, but the judge sins for accepting it. The passage then delves into an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning a qadi who was initially just (adil) and then becomes fasiq. According to the zahir al-riwayah (apparent transmission of the school), the qadi does not become automatically removed (la yan'azilu) but rather deserves removal (yastahiqqu al-'azl). The authority (sultan) must remove him. This is the position of the majority of Hanafi scholars, especially those from Bukhara and Samarkand. However, some Hanafi scholars, notably al-Tahawi and Ali al-Razi (a companion of Abu Yusuf), hold that the qadi is automatically removed upon becoming fasiq. Their reasoning is that the appointment was based on his justice; when that condition ceases, the appointment ceases, similar to a slave permitted to trade who loses permission if he runs away. The text notes that the fatwa (operative ruling) is upon the view of automatic removal, yet it also quotes al-Bahr al-Ra'iq stating that the school (madhhab) is contrary to that, i.e., the school holds the opposite view (that he is not automatically removed). The preferred view in the author's time appears to be the one that treats the appointment as valid unless the ruler actively removes him. The passage also clarifies that if a judge takes a bribe for his appointment, he does not become a valid judge at all, and judgments given for a bribe are unenforceable. Finally, the passage lists desirable qualities of a judge: he should not be harsh or tyrannical, and he must be trustworthy, chaste, intelligent, pious, and knowledgeable in fiqh. The mufti should likewise possess these qualities.

Key Terms
الفاسق (al-fāsiq): A Muslim who commits a major sin (kabīrah) or persists in a minor sin (ṣaghīrah)
التقليد (al-taqlīd): Legally: a ruling that declares someone a judge in a specific place; literally: placing a necklace on the neck
العزل (al-ʿazl): Removal from office; the qadi who becomes fasiq deserves removal (yastaḥiqq al-ʿazl) but is not automatically removed according to the majority.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the majority (zahir al-riwayah) in this passage, what is the status of a qadi who was just but then becomes fasiq?
A) He is automatically removed from office.
B) He is not automatically removed, but deserves removal by the sultan.
C) His judgments become invalid immediately.
D) He remains judge but stops receiving salary.

Key Takeaway
Appointing a fasiq as qadi is valid but sinful; a just judge who becomes fasiq is not automatically removed (majority view) but must be removed by the ruler.

Does a Corrupt Judge Lose His Office? An Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on automatic dismissal of a corrupt judge

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between automatic dismissal (yan'azil) and deserving dismissal (yastahiqq al-'azl) in Hanafi law, and state which view is the apparent madhhab position and which is given in fatwa.

Core Idea
A corrupt judge is not automatically dismissed but deserves dismissal — the appointment does not self-destruct.

This passage deals with a nuanced intra-Hanafi dispute: when a qadi who was upright becomes corrupt (fasiq), does his appointment automatically end? The answer affects the validity of his judgments. The established school position (zahir al-madhhab) holds that he is not dismissed automatically, but the authority must remove him. However, some later scholars favor automatic dismissal, and the fatwa is given accordingly. We will trace the arguments and see how Hanafi jurists navigate between principle and public interest.

Guided Path
1
State the issue
What happens when a judge becomes corrupt? The specific question is whether his appointment automatically terminates.
2
Identify the two positions
Position 1 (zahir al-madhhab): The judge is not automatically dismissed but deserves dismissal (yastahiqq al-‘azl). Position 2 (some scholars): He is automatically dismissed (yan‘azil).
3
Examine the reasoning for each
Position 2 argues that the appointment is conditioned on integrity, like a slave's permission to trade: if the slave runs away, permission is revoked. Position 1 maintains that the contract of appointment continues until actively revoked; the authority must dismiss him.
4
Note the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
If a corrupt judge issues a judgment after corruption, those who hold automatic dismissal consider it invalid; those who hold non-dismissal consider it valid unless tainted by the corruption (e.g., bribery).
5
Determine the mufta bihi
According to al-Durr al-Mukhtar, the fatwa is on automatic dismissal, but al-Bahr al-Ra'iq states that the madhhab is contrary. The apparent position (al-zahir) remains non-dismissal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فاستحقاق العزل ثابت في ظاهر المذهب، وعليه مشايخنا البحاريون والسمرقنديون. كذا في «الفتح». وعند بعض مشايخنا ينعزل؛ لأنَّ المقلد اعتمد عدالته، فيتقيد التقليد بحال عدالته، فصار كأنه علق بقاء قضاء القاضي بحال عدالته، فلما فسق لم يبق التقليد لارتفاع العدالة. كذا في «النهاية». وهو اختيار الطحاوي وعلي الرازي صاحب أبي يوسف كذا في «العناية»، وعليه الفتوى. كذا في «الدر المختار»، لكن قال في «البحر» بعد نقله والمذهب خلافه. انتهى. وهو غريب. وقال الزيلعي في «شرح الكنز»: ولو كان القاضي عادلاً ففسق بأخذ الرشوة لا ينعزل، ويستحق العزل، وإذا أخذ القضاء بالرشوة لا يصير قاضياً، وكذا لو قضى بالرشوة لا ينفذ قضاؤه فيما ارتشى. وقال بعض مشايخنا: إذا قلد الفاسق ابتداءً يصح، ولو قلد وهو عدل ينعزل بالفسق؛ لأنَّ المقلّد اعتمد عدالته فلم يكن راضياً بدونه، كالعبد المأذون له في التجارة إذا أبق ينعزل، ولو أذن له وهو آبق جاز. وعن علمائنا الثلاثةِ في «النوادر»: إنّ الفاسق لا يصلح قاضياً، والظاهر هو الأول. انتهى.
The entitlement to dismissal is established in the apparent madhhab, and upon it are our scholars of Bukhara and Samarkand. Thus in 'al-Fath'. And according to some of our scholars, he is dismissed, because the one who appointed him relied on his integrity, so the appointment is conditioned upon his state of integrity, so it is as if he made the continuation of the qadi's judgeship conditional upon his state of integrity, so when he became corrupt, the appointment no longer remains due to the loss of integrity. Thus in 'al-Nihaya'. And it is the choice of al-Tahawi and 'Ali al-Razi, the companion of Abu Yusuf. Thus in 'al-'Inaya', and upon it is the fatwa. Thus in 'al-Durr al-Mukhtar', but he said in 'al-Bahr' after quoting it that the madhhab is contrary to it. End. And it is strange. And al-Zayla'i said in 'Sharh al-Kanz': If a judge was just and then became corrupt by taking a bribe, he is not dismissed and deserves dismissal; and if he accepted the judgeship by bribe, he does not become a judge; and likewise, if he adjudicated by bribe, his judgment is not executed in the matter for which he took the bribe. And some of our scholars said: If a corrupt person is appointed initially, it is valid; but if he was appointed while just, he is dismissed by corruption, because the one who appointed him relied on his integrity and was not satisfied without it, like a slave who is permitted to trade—if he runs away, his permission is revoked; but if he was given permission while already a runaway, it is valid. And from our three imams in 'al-Nawadir': The corrupt person is not suitable as a judge. And the apparent position is the first. End.
Source pages p. 417

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the status of a judge who becomes corrupt (fasiq) after his appointment. The author opens by stating the established position (zahir al-madhhab): the judge is not automatically dismissed but deserves dismissal (yastahiqq al-'azl). This is the view of the Bukhari and Samarqandi scholars. Then he presents an opposing view held by some scholars, including al-Tahawi and 'Ali al-Razi, that the judge is automatically dismissed (yan'azil). Their reasoning is that the appointing authority (muqallid) relied on the judge's integrity ('adala), so the appointment is implicitly conditioned upon continued integrity; when he becomes corrupt, the condition fails, so the appointment ends. They compare this to a slave given permission to trade: if the slave runs away (abaqa), the permission is revoked (yan'azil), but if the slave was already a runaway when permission was given, it is valid. The author notes that the fatwa is given on this second view according to al-Durr al-Mukhtar, but al-Bahr al-Ra'iq states that the madhhab (the relied-upon school position) is contrary to it (i.e., the first view). Al-Zayla'i further clarifies: a corrupt judge is not dismissed but deserves dismissal; if he obtained judgeship by bribe, his appointment is invalid; if he judges in a case due to bribe, his judgment is not enforceable. The passage ends by citing the three imams (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) from al-Nawadir that a corrupt person is unfit to be a judge, but the apparent position (al-zahir) is the first (that he is not dismissed). The author thus leaves the matter with the dominant view being non-dismissal despite the fatwa preference for dismissal.

Key Terms
استحقاق العزل (istihqāq al-‘azl): The entitlement or deservedness of dismissal; the judge does not automatically lose his position but the authority is obliged to remove him.
المقلد (al-muqallid): The person who appoints or designates the judge; the authority that confers the office of qada'.
العدالة (al-‘adālah): Integrity, uprightness; the quality of being trustworthy and just, a prerequisite for a judge's appointment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the apparent position (zahir al-madhhab) in this passage, what is the status of a judge who becomes corrupt by taking bribes?
A) He is automatically dismissed (yan‘azil).
B) He is not automatically dismissed, but deserves dismissal (yastahiqq al-‘azl).
C) His judgments are all invalid.
D) He is fined and removed from office.

Key Takeaway
The apparent Hanafi position is that a corrupt judge is not automatically dismissed but deserves dismissal; the fatwa view of automatic dismissal is a valid minority opinion within the school.

Ideal Qualities of a Qadi and Mufti
Ruling on the desirable characteristics of a judge

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the prohibited and required qualities of a qadi and mufti according to the Hanafi school, and explain why each quality is essential.

Core Idea
A judge must combine moral integrity with deep legal knowledge; harshness or ignorance disqualifies.

This short passage from Multaqa al-Abhar encapsulates the Hanafi view on the essential qualities of a judge (qadi) and, by extension, a jurisconsult (mufti). It serves as a memorization anchor for students, combining ethical and intellectual prerequisites. Understanding these qualities helps us appreciate the high standard required for those who interpret and apply Islamic law.

Guided Path
1
Identify prohibited traits
Read the first part of the passage: 'ولا ينبغي أن يكون القاضي فظا غليظاً جباراً عنيداً'. List the four negative qualities: harshness, coarseness, tyranny, and obstinacy.
These traits hinder justice and undermine the judge's ability to listen and decide fairly.
2
Identify required traits
Read the second part: 'وينبغي أن يكون موثوقاً به في دينه، وعفافه وعقله، وصلاحه وفهمه وعلمه بالسنة والآثار ووجوه الفقه'. List the positive qualities: trustworthiness in religion, chastity, intellect, righteousness, understanding, knowledge of Sunnah, traditions, and aspects of fiqh.
Note the emphasis on both moral character ('din, 'iffa, 'aql, salah) and formal knowledge (Sunnah, athar, wujuh al-fiqh).
3
Note the extension to mufti
The passage ends with 'وكذا المفتي' — 'Likewise, the mufti.' Recognize that these same qualities apply to anyone giving legal verdicts.
The mufti's role is similar: he must be trustworthy and knowledgeable.
4
Reflect on the source authority
The passage is attributed to 'Multaqa al-Abhar', a Hanafi manual. Consider the weight of this text in the Hanafi tradition (zahir al-riwayah).
This ensures the ruling is relied upon (mufta bihi) in the school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا ينبغي أن يكون القاضي فظا غليظاً جباراً عنيداً، وينبغي أن يكون موثوقاً به في دينه، وعفافه وعقله، وصلاحه وفهمه وعلمه بالسنة والآثار ووجوه الفقه، وكذا المفتي. كذا في «ملتقى الأبحر».
And it is not befitting that the judge be harsh, coarse, tyrannical, or obstinate; and it is befitting that he be trustworthy in his religion, his chastity, his intellect, his righteousness, his understanding, and his knowledge of the Sunnah, the traditions, and the aspects of jurisprudence. Likewise, the mufti. Thus in 'Multaqa al-Abhar'.
Source pages p. 417

Commentary
This passage is a concise ruling (hukm) stating the ideal qualities of a qadi (judge) and, by extension, a mufti (jurisconsult). It lists negative traits to avoid: harshness (fazz), coarseness (ghaliz), tyranny (jabbar), and obstinacy ('anid). Positive qualities include trustworthiness in religion (thiqa fi dinihi), chastity ('iffa), intellect ('aql), righteousness (salah), understanding (fahm), and knowledge of the Sunnah, transmitted reports (athar), and facets of fiqh (wujuh al-fiqh). The emphasis is on moral uprightness and comprehensive legal knowledge. The source is Multaqa al-Abhar, a well-known Hanafi fiqh manual. This statement functions as a memorization anchor for students, encapsulating the ethical and educational prerequisites for judicial office.

Key Terms
فظا غليظاً (fazzan ghalīzan): Harsh and coarse; rough in manner, the opposite of gentleness required for judicial conduct.
موثوقاً به (mawthūqan bihi): Trustworthy; reliable in his personal conduct and judgment.
وجوه الفقه (wujūh al-fiqh): The various aspects and methodologies of fiqh; comprehensive understanding of legal reasoning.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT listed as a positive quality for a qadi in this passage?
A) Trustworthiness in religion
B) Deep knowledge of the Sunnah
C) Wealth and social status
D) Understanding (fahm)

Key Takeaway
A qadi must combine moral uprightness (din, iffah, 'aql) with comprehensive legal knowledge (Sunnah, athar, wujuh al-fiqh); lacking either quality compromises the office.
