Definition and Conditions of Ijtihad
Definition of ijtihad and prerequisites for a mujtahid

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of ijtihad and list the eight conditions required for a mujtahid mutlaq.

Core Idea
Ijtihad requires exerting utmost effort; the mujtahid must meet strict conditions including Islam, reason, puberty, and expertise in Arabic, Quran, hadith, and qiyas.

This passage opens with a definition of ijtihad from al-Misbah al-Munir, then enumerates the conditions for a mujtahid. Understanding these conditions is essential because they define who is qualified to derive rulings. The author also distinguishes between the absolute mujtahid and the partial mujtahid. We will focus on the core definition and the full list of conditions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
The passage defines 'ijtihad' (the act) and implicitly 'mujtahid' (the person). Note that ijtihad is the exertion, and the mujtahid is the one who exerts.
The definition is from al-Misbah al-Munir.
2
Extract the definition of ijtihad
Ijtihad is defined as بذل الوسع والطاقة (exertion of capacity and energy) in seeking the ruling until reaching the utmost limit.
This emphasizes maximal effort.
3
List the conditions for the absolute mujtahid
The passage lists eight conditions: Islam, reason, puberty, being faqih al-nafs (naturally sharp), knowledge of Arabic, encompassing the Quranic verses on rulings, knowledge of hadith (text, chain, abrogating/abrogated), and knowledge of qiyas.
These conditions ensure both sincerity and competence.
4
Note the exception for partial mujtahid
A mujtahid in a specific area only needs knowledge related to that area, not all conditions. For example, ijtihad in prayer does not require knowledge of marriage rulings.
This is from al-Talwih.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والاجتهاد. قال في «المصباح المنير»: اجتهد في الأمر بذل وسعه وطاقته في طلبه؛ ليبلغ مجهوده، ويصل إلى نهايته. انتهى. ومعنى بذل الطاقة: أن يحس من نفسه العجز عن المزيد عليه. وشرطه: الإسلام، والعقل، والبلوغ، وكونه فقيه النفس؛ أي شديد الفهم بالطبع، وعلمه باللغة العربية، وكونه حاوياً لكتاب الله تعالى فيما يتعلق بالأحكام، وعالماً بالحديث متناً وسنداً، وناسخاً ومنسوخاً، وبالقياس. وهذه الشرائط في المجتهد المطلق الذي يفتي في جميع الأحكام، وأما المجتهد في حكم دون حكم فعليه معرفة ما يتعلّق بذلك الحكم مثلاً كالاجتهاد في حكم متعلّق بالصلاة لا يتوقف على معرفة جميع ما يتعلق بالنكاح. كذا في «التلويح»، والمراد هاهنا المعنى الأول. صرح به في «النهر» وغيره. وقال في «الفصول العمادية»: المجتهد من يعلمُ الكتاب والسنة مقدار ما يتعلق به الأحكام دون المواعظ، وقيل: إذا كان صوابه أكثر من خطئه حل له الاجتهاد، والأول أصح. انتهى. وقال في «الهداية»: وفي حد الاجتهاد كلام عرف في أصول الفقه، حاصله: أن يكون صاحب حديث له معرفة بالفقه؛ ليعرف معاني الآثار، أو صاحب فقه له معرفةٌ بالحديث؛ لئلا يشتغل بالقياس في المنصوص عليه. انتهى. والفرق بين القولين: أنَّ على الأوّل نسبة إلى معرفة الحديث أكثر من معرفته بالفقه وفي الثاني: عكسه، وأنت تعلم أنّ المجتهد يحتاج إلى الأمرين جميعا، وهما
And ijtihad. He said in 'al-Misbah al-Munir': 'Ijtihad in a matter is the exertion of one's capacity and energy in seeking it, so that he reaches his utmost and arrives at its end.' End. And the meaning of exertion of energy is that he feels from himself incapacity to do more. Its conditions are: Islam, reason, puberty, and that he is a faqih al-nafs—i.e., sharp in understanding by nature—and his knowledge of the Arabic language, and that he encompasses the Book of Allah Almighty regarding rulings, and that he is knowledgeable of hadith in text and chain, of abrogating and abrogated, and of analogical reasoning. These conditions apply to the absolute mujtahid who gives fatwa in all rulings. As for the mujtahid in a specific ruling without another, he must know what relates to that ruling, for example, ijtihad in a ruling pertaining to prayer does not depend on knowing all that pertains to marriage. Thus in 'al-Talwih'. And the intended meaning here is the first. It is explicitly stated in 'al-Nahr' and others. And he said in 'al-Fusul al-'Imadiyya': The mujtahid is one who knows the Book and the Sunnah to the extent that rulings depend on them, not exhortations. And it is said: If his correct opinions are more than his errors, ijtihad is permissible for him. The first is more correct. End. And he said in 'al-Hidaya': In the definition of ijtihad there is discussion known in usul al-fiqh, the summary of which is: Let him be a master of hadith with knowledge of fiqh to know the meanings of traditions, or a master of fiqh with knowledge of hadith lest he engage in qiyas in a matter that is textually ruled. End. The difference between the two statements is that according to the first, he is attributed more to knowledge of hadith than to fiqh, and in the second, the opposite. And you know that the mujtahid needs both matters, and they are
Source pages p. 418

Commentary
This passage provides a definition of ijtihad and outlines the conditions for a mujtahid. The author begins by quoting al-Misbah al-Munir: ijtihad is exerting one's utmost effort in seeking a ruling until reaching the limit of capacity. The conditions for a mujtahid include: Islam, reason (aql), puberty (bulugh), being 'faqih al-nafs' (naturally sharp in understanding), knowledge of Arabic, comprehensive knowledge of the Quranic verses related to rulings (ayat al-ahkam), knowledge of hadith (text and chain, abrogating and abrogated), and knowledge of qiyas (analogical reasoning). These are for the absolute mujtahid (mujtahid mutlaq) who issues fatwa in all matters. For a partial mujtahid (mujtahid fi hukm), he only needs to know what relates to that specific ruling, e.g., ijtihad in salah does not require knowledge of nikah. The author then cites al-Fusul al-'Imadiyya, which defines the mujtahid as one who knows enough of Quran and Sunnah for rulings (not for exhortations), and mentions an alternative view: if his correct opinions exceed his errors, ijtihad is permissible, but the first definition is more correct. Finally, from al-Hidaya, the definition is summarized: the mujtahid must be either a hadith scholar with knowledge of fiqh or a fiqh scholar with knowledge of hadith, to avoid applying qiyas where a textual ruling exists. The author notes that the first definition emphasizes more hadith knowledge, the second more fiqh, but both are needed. The passage ends abruptly, but the central point is clear: ijtihad requires comprehensive expertise in both the revealed sources and legal reasoning.

Key Terms
اجتهاد (ijtihād): Exerting one's utmost effort in deriving legal rulings from the primary sources (Quran, Sunnah, consensus, analogy).
فقيه النفس (faqīh al-nafs): One who is naturally sharp and quick in understanding legal matters; innate intelligence in fiqh.
المجتهد المطلق (al-mujtahid al-muṭlaq): A jurist qualified to derive rulings in all areas of fiqh independently.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is NOT a condition for a mujtahid according to the passage?
A) Knowledge of Quranic verses on rulings
B) Knowledge of hadith chain
C) Knowledge of Arabic grammar
D) Knowledge of Persian etymology

Key Takeaway
Ijtihad requires strict conditions; without them, one cannot derive rulings authoritatively.

Absolute vs. Partial Ijtihad and the Knowledge Debate
Comparison of two definitions of mujtahid and the required knowledge balance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to differentiate between an absolute and a partial mujtahid, and explain the two views on the relative importance of hadith and fiqh knowledge for a mujtahid.

Core Idea
A mujtahid must master both hadith and fiqh; opinions vary on which expertise is primary, but both are essential.

After listing the conditions, the passage delves into two definitions from al-Fusul al-'Imadiyya and al-Hidayah. The first emphasizes knowing enough of Quran and Sunnah for rulings; the second stresses that the mujtahid must be either a hadith specialist with fiqh or a fiqh specialist with hadith. This shows that the balance of knowledge is crucial, and the absolute mujtahid needs both. We will unpack these views and their practical implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two definitions
From al-Fusul: The mujtahid knows Quran and Sunnah to the extent of rulings (not exhortations). From al-Hidayah: He must be a hadith expert with fiqh or a fiqh expert with hadith.
Both definitions aim to define the scope of required knowledge.
2
Compare the emphasis of each definition
Al-Fusul focuses on the scope of sources (Quran and Sunnah), while al-Hidayah focuses on the balance between two disciplines (hadith and fiqh).
The author notes the difference in terms of which knowledge is primary.
3
Apply the distinction: absolute vs. partial mujtahid
The absolute mujtahid must meet the full conditions; the partial mujtahid only needs knowledge related to his specific area, implying he may have a narrower expertise.
Partial ijtihad is permissible and practical.
4
Determine the preferred view and synthesize
The author states 'the first is more correct' (al-Fusul) but then affirms that both hadith and fiqh are needed, harmonizing the two.
The mufta bihi is that a mujtahid must have both, with sufficient knowledge of Quran and Sunnah for rulings.

Definition and Conditions of Ijtihad
Definition and conditions of ijtihad

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define ijtihad and list the conditions required for an absolute mujtahid according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Ijtihad is the exhaustive search for a probable ruling; its conditions ensure the mujtahid has the tools to derive directly from the sources.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah begins by defining ijtihad both linguistically and technically, then enumerates the rigorous conditions a jurist must meet to be considered an absolute mujtahid. Understanding these conditions is foundational for appreciating who is qualified to issue independent rulings and how the Hanafi school conceptualises legal authority.

Guided Path
1
Locate the definition of ijtihad
Find the passage where ijtihad is defined. The text gives both a linguistic definition (expending one's capacity) and a technical one (seeking a probable legal ruling from the proofs).
The definition is essential: ijtihad is an effort, not a certainty.
2
Identify each condition of the absolute mujtahid
List the conditions: Islam, intellect, puberty, being faqih al-nafs (natural juristic aptitude), proficiency in Arabic, comprehensive knowledge of Quranic legal verses, knowledge of hadith (text, chain, abrogation), knowledge of analogy, and knowledge of consensus and Companions' statements.
These conditions ensure the mujtahid can operate independently without taqlid.
3
Distinguish absolute vs. partial mujtahid
The text differentiates between a mujtahid mutlaq (who rules on all areas) and a mujtahid fi hukm (who only rules on a specific area, e.g., prayer). The partial mujtahid only needs knowledge relevant to that area.
This distinction is crucial for understanding who can issue fatwas in specialized fields.
4
Note the dispute over the relative importance of fiqh vs. hadith
Al-Hidayah presents two views: one requiring more hadith knowledge, the other more fiqh. The text concludes the mujtahid needs both, and the most correct definition is comprehensive.
This ikhtilaf shows the nuanced nature of defining qualifications.

Source Text

والاجتهاد ( الاجتهاد (11) زبدة النهاية لعمدة الرعاية (1) قوله : والاجتهاد...الخ ؛ قال في «المصباح المنير : اجتهد في الأمر بذل وسعه وطاقته في طلبه ؛ ليبلغ مجهوده ، ويصل إلى نهايته. انتهى. ومعنى بذل الطاقة : أن يحس من نفسه العجز عن المزيد عليه. وشرطه : الإسلام، والعقل، والبلوغ، وكونه فقيه النفس ؛ أي شديد الفهم بالطبع، وعمله باللغة العربية، وكونه حاوياً لكتاب الله تعالى فيما يتعلق بالأحكام، وعالماً بالحديث متناً وسنداً، وناسخاً ومنسوخاً، وبالقياس. وهذه الشرائط في المجتهد المطلق الذي يفتي في جميع الأحكام، وأما المجتهد في حكم دون حكم فعليه معرفة ما يتعلّق بذلك الحكم مثلاً كالاجتهاد في حكم متعلّق بالصلاة لا يتوقف على معرفة جميع ما يتعلق بالنكاح. كذا في «التلويح (3)، والمراد هاهنا المعنى الأول. صرح به في «النهر» وغيره. وقال في «الفصول العمادية»: المجتهد من يعلمُ الكتاب والسنة مقدار ما يتعلق به الأحكام دون المواعظ ، وقيل : إذا كان صوابه أكثر من خطئه حل له الاجتهاد، والأول أصح. انتهى. وقال في «الهداية») : وفي حد الاجتهاد كلام عرف في أصول الفقه، حاصله : أن يكون صاحب حديث له معرفة بالفقه ؛ ليعرف معاني الآثار، أو صاحب فقه له معرفةٌ بالحديث ؛ لئلا يشتغل بالقياس في المنصوص عليه. انتهى. والفرق بين القولين : أنَّ على الأوّل نسبة إلى معرفة الحديث أكثر من معرفته بالفقه وفي الثاني : عكسه، وأنت تعلم أنّ المجتهد يحتاج إلى الأمرين جميعا، وهما (1) وأصح ما قيل في حدّ المجتهد أن يكون قد حوى علم الكتاب ووجوه معانيه، وعلم السنة بطرقها ومتونها ووجوه معانيها وكذا علم الآثار المنقولة عن الصحابة وما أجمعوا عليه وما اختلفوا فيه، وان يكون عالماً بالقياس وعرف الناس. ينظر : فتح باب العناية»(3 : 107 - 108). (2) «المصباح المنير» (ص112). (3) «التلويح» (2 : 236). (4) الهداية (3 : 101).  كتاب القضاء الشهادة شرط للأولوية ، فلو قُلَّدَ جاهل تحرزانه عن القياس في معارضة النصّ، ومعرفة معاني الآثار ؛ ليتمكن من القياس ، فالوجه أن يقال : صاحب حديث وفقه ؛ ليعرف معاني الآثار، ويمتنع عن القياس. والحاصل أن يعلم بالكتاب والسنّة بأقسامهما من عبارتهما وإشارتهما ودلالتهما واقتضائهما وباقي الأقسام : ناسخها ومنسوخها ، ومناطات أحكامهما، ومشروط القياس، والمسائل المجمع عليها ؛ لئلا يقع في القياس في مقابلة الإجماع، وأقوال الصحابة، كذا في «الفتح» وقيل : أن يكون صاحب قريحة مع ذل ذلك : أي أحد الأمرين، يعرف بها عادات الناس ؛ لأنَّ من الأحكام ما يبتني عليها. كذا في «الهداية»(2). فهذا القيد لا بُدّ منه في المجتهد، فمن اتقن هذه الجملة فهو أهل الاجتهاد، فيجب عليه أن يُعْمِلَ اجتهاده : وهو أن يبذل جهده في طلب الظن بحكم شرعي عن هذه الأدلة، ولا يقلّد أحداً. كذا في «الفتح» (3) ، وزيادة التفصيل في مطولات الأصول ومبسوطات الفقهاء الفحول [1] قوله : شرط للأولوية ؛ لا للجواز، وهو الصحيح تيسيراً وتسهيلاً، خلافاً للأئمة الثلاثة ؛ لأنه مأمور بالقضاء، والأمرُ به يستدعي القدرة عليه، ولا يثبت القدرة عليه إلا بالعلم ؛ لأنَّ الجاهل لا يميّز بين الحق والباطل، ولنا : أن المقصود من القضاء وهو إيصال الحق إلى مستحقه يحصل بفتوى غيره. [2] قوله : جاهل ؛ قال ابن الغرس : ليس مرادهم بالجاهل العامي المحض، بل لا بد من تأهل العلم والفهم ، وأقلُّهُ أن يحسن بعض الحوادث والمسائل الدقيقة، وأن يعرف طريق تحصيل الأحكام الشرعيّة من كتب المذهب وصدور المشايخ، وكيفية الإيراد والإصدار في الوقائع والدعاوى والحجج. انتهى. نقله العلامة الطحطاوي في «حاشيته على الدر المختار (4) عن العلامة الحموي.
And ijtihad... (Ijtihad) (11) Zubdat al-Nihaya li-‘Umdat al-Ri‘aya. (1) His statement: “And ijtihad...etc.” He said in al-Misbah al-Munir: “He exerted himself in a matter, i.e., he expended his capacity and effort in seeking it, to reach his utmost and attain its end.” End. And the meaning of expending capacity is that he feels from himself inability to do more. Its conditions: Islam, intellect, puberty, being a faqih al-nafs (i.e., naturally strong in understanding), his practice of the Arabic language, being fully aware of the Book of Allah regarding legal rulings, being knowledgeable of hadith in text and chain, abrogating and abrogated, and of analogy. These conditions are for the absolute mujtahid who gives fatwa in all rulings. As for the mujtahid in one ruling without another, he must know what relates to that ruling—for example, ijtihad in a ruling related to prayer does not depend on knowing everything related to marriage. So in al-Talwih (3), and the intended meaning here is the first one. He explicitly stated it in al-Nahr and others. And he said in al-Fusul al-‘Imadiyya: “The mujtahid is one who knows the Book and Sunnah to the extent relevant to rulings, not exhortations. And it was said: if his correct rulings are more than his errors, ijtihad is permissible for him. The first is more correct.” End. And he said in al-Hidaya: “Regarding the definition of ijtihad, there is discussion known in usul al-fiqh. Its summary: He should be a scholar of hadith with knowledge of fiqh to understand the meanings of athar, or a scholar of fiqh with knowledge of hadith to avoid engaging in analogy where there is a text.” End. The difference between the two statements: In the first, proportion of knowledge of hadith is more than knowledge of fiqh; in the second, the opposite. And you know that the mujtahid needs both together. And they are (1) “The most correct thing said concerning the definition of the mujtahid is that he should have encompassed the knowledge of the Book and its aspects of meanings, and the knowledge of the Sunnah with its chains and texts and aspects of meanings, likewise knowledge of the athar transmitted from the Companions, what they agreed upon and what they disagreed upon, and he should be knowledgeable of analogy and of the customs of the people.” Look: Fath Bab al-‘Inayah (3:107-108). (2) al-Misbah al-Munir (p.112). (3) al-Talwih (2:236). (4) al-Hidaya (3:101). [PAGE_419] Book of Judgments and Testimony. “A condition for preference. So if an ignorant person is appointed as judge...” ... to protect himself from analogy opposing the text, and knowledge of the meanings of athar so that he can perform analogy. So the correct view is to say: He should be a scholar of hadith and fiqh so that he understands the meanings of athar and refrains from analogy. In summary, he should know the Book and Sunnah in their categories: their explicit meaning, implication, indication, and requirement, and the remaining categories: their abrogating and abrogated, the effective causes of their rulings, the conditions of analogy, and the issues agreed upon by consensus, lest he fall into analogy opposing consensus and the statements of the Companions. So in al-Fath. And it was said: He should be of sound nature with that, i.e., one of the two things, by which he knows the customs of the people because some rulings are based on them. So in al-Hidaya (2). This condition is necessary for the mujtahid. Whoever masters these matters is qualified for ijtihad, and it is obligatory upon him to exercise his ijtihad: that he exerts his effort in seeking probability of a legal ruling from these proofs, and he does not imitate anyone. So in al-Fath (3). Further detail is in the lengthy books of usul and the comprehensive works of great jurists. [1] His statement: “A condition for preference,” not for permissibility. This is the correct view for ease and facilitation, contrary to the three imams. Because he is commanded to judge, and the command for it requires ability to do it, and ability is only established through knowledge, since the ignorant cannot distinguish truth from falsehood. Our evidence: The purpose of judgment, which is delivering rights to their rightful owners, is achieved by the fatwa of others. [2] His statement: “ignorant.” Ibn al-Ghars said: They do not mean by the ignorant a pure layman. Rather, he must be qualified with knowledge and understanding, the least of which is that he be adept at some incidents and intricate issues, and know the method of deriving legal rulings from the books of the madhhab and the teachings of the shaykhs, and the manner of handling cases, claims, and evidence. End. Transmitted by ‘Allamah al-Tahtawi in his Hashiyah on al-Durr al-Mukhtar (4) from ‘Allamah al-Hamawi.
Source pages p. 418

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah is a commentary on the conditions of ijtihad and its application to judgeship. It begins by defining ijtihad linguistically and technically, then lists the conditions for a mujtahid mutlaq (absolute mujtahid): Islam, intellect, puberty, natural juristic aptitude, proficiency in Arabic, comprehensive knowledge of Quranic legal verses, hadith (text and chain), abrogation, and analogy. It distinguishes between absolute and partial ijtihad. Several definitions from classical works are cited, emphasizing that a mujtahid must have both hadith and fiqh expertise. The text then addresses the specific context of a judge, stating that ijtihad is a condition of preference (first) not validity (second), which is the Hanafi position. The Hanafis argue that a non-mujtahid judge can fulfill the purpose of judgment by consulting a fatwa from a mujtahid. This is contrary to the three other imams who require ijtihad for validity. Finally, it clarifies that a 'jahil' judge is not a complete ignoramus but someone with basic competence in law and procedure, who can learn from books and scholars. This reflects the Hanafi concern for practicality and ease in judicial appointments.

Key Terms
الاجتهاد (al-ijtihād): Exerting one's maximum effort to arrive at a probable legal ruling from the textual proofs.
المجتهد المطلق (al-mujtahid al-muṭlaq): A jurist who can independently derive rulings in all areas of fiqh, meeting all conditions of ijtihad.
فقيه النفس (faqīh al-nafs): Natural juristic aptitude; a person who has a strong intuitive understanding of fiqh.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the minimum requirement for a partial mujtahid (mujtahid fi hukm)?
A) He must meet all conditions of an absolute mujtahid.
B) He must know only what relates to that specific ruling.
C) He must be a scholar of hadith only.
D) He must be chosen by a group of scholars.

Key Takeaway
Ijtihad requires a specific set of conditions; a partial mujtahid needs only knowledge relevant to his area, while an absolute mujtahid must master all the sources.

Qada — Judiciary
intermediate
8 min
The Judge's Requirement: Ijtihad as Preference vs. Validity
Ruling on ijtihad for a judge: preference vs. validity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on ijtihad as a condition for a judge, and explain why the Hanafis differ from the three imams.

Core Idea
A judge need not be a mujtahid; the purpose of judgment can be achieved through consulting scholars.

This section directly addresses the practical question: can a non-mujtahid serve as a judge? The Hanafi school takes a permissive stance, facilitating judicial appointments, while the other imams require ijtihad. The passage provides both sides and the Hanafi evidence, along with a crucial clarification that the 'ignorant' judge is not a layman but has basic competence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling: ijtihad is a condition of preference, not validity
The text states: 'shartun lil-awlawiyyah, la lil-jawaz'. This means ijtihad is preferred but not required for the judge's appointment to be valid.
This is the core Hanafi position.
2
Note the opposing view of the three imams
The three imams (Shafi'i, Maliki, Hanbali) hold that ijtihad is a condition for validity. The passage gives their proof: the command to judge (Quran) implies ability, which requires knowledge.
Know the opposing position to understand the dispute.
3
Analyze the Hanafi proof
The Hanafi proof is that the purpose of judgment (delivering rights) is achieved through the fatwa of others. A non-mujtahid can judge by consulting scholars.
This proof is based on convenience (taysiran wa tas-hilan).
4
Evaluate the Hanafi response to the opposing proof
The Hanafis respond that the command to judge does not specify ijtihad; the judge can learn from others. The ability required is the ability to seek knowledge, not to have it already.
The Hanafi response focuses on the attainability of the objective.
5
Understand the qualification of the 'jahil' judge
The passage clarifies that 'jahil' does not mean a complete layman. The judge must have basic competence: ability to handle some cases, knowledge of how to derive rulings from books and scholars.
This prevents the Hanafi position from being too lax.

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah is a commentary on the conditions of ijtihad and its application to judgeship. It begins by defining ijtihad linguistically and technically, then lists the conditions for a mujtahid mutlaq (absolute mujtahid): Islam, intellect, puberty, natural juristic aptitude, proficiency in Arabic, comprehensive knowledge of Quranic legal verses, hadith (text and chain), abrogation, and analogy. It distinguishes between absolute and partial ijtihad. Several definitions from classical works are cited, emphasizing that a mujtahid must have both hadith and fiqh expertise. The text then addresses the specific context of a judge, stating that ijtihad is a condition of preference (first) not validity (second), which is the Hanafi position. The Hanafis argue that a non-mujtahid judge can fulfill the purpose of judgment by consulting a fatwa from a mujtahid. This is contrary to the three other imams who require ijtihad for validity. Finally, it clarifies that a 'jahil' judge is not a complete ignoramus but someone with basic competence in law and procedure, who can learn from books and scholars. This reflects the Hanafi concern for practicality and ease in judicial appointments.

Key Terms
شرط للأولوية (sharṭun lil-awlawiyyah): A condition that is recommended but not obligatory; i.e., it makes the act better but its absence does not invalidate.
جاهل (jāhil): Literally 'ignorant', but in context, it refers to a judge who is not a mujtahid but has basic competence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Hanafi reason for allowing a non-mujtahid judge?
A) The Quran does not mention ijtihad.
B) The purpose of judgment is achieved through the fatwa of others.
C) The three imams are not correct.
D) Non-mujtahids are more just.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits a non-mujtahid judge based on the purpose of judgment being achievable through consultation, making ijtihad a condition of preference, not validity.

Knowledge for a Qadi: Condition for Preferability, Not Validity
States the Hanafi ruling on knowledge as a condition for preferability for a qadi.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the necessity of knowledge for a judge and explain why it is a condition for preferability, not validity.

Core Idea
A judge's appointment is valid without ijtihad; knowledge is only a condition for preferability to facilitate the judiciary.

This passage addresses a key dispute in Islamic jurisprudence: must a judge be a mujtahid? The Hanafi school takes a facilitative position: personal ijtihad is not required for the validity of a judge's appointment; it is only recommended. The ruling is based on the purpose of judgment being achievable through consultation with qualified scholars. We will explore the proof, the opposing views of the other three Sunni schools, and the precise definition of 'ignorant' in this context.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the appointment of a judge (qadi). The Hanafi ruling is that knowledge (ijtihad) is a condition for preferability (shart al-awlawiyya), not for validity (shart al-sihha).
This distinction is unique to Hanafi usul.
2
Locate the proof
The Hanafi proof is rational (qiyas khafi/istihsan): the purpose of judgment—delivering rights to their owners—can be achieved by the judge following the fatwa of another qualified scholar.
The proof is not a direct text but a consequence of the objective.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Since the objective is met without personal ijtihad, the requirement of knowledge is not essential for validity; it is only recommended to ensure better discernment.
This is an example of istidlal bi al-maqsad (reasoning by purpose).
4
Note the ikhtilaf
The other three schools (Shafi'i, Maliki, Hanbali) hold that knowledge is a condition for validity because the command to judge (فاحكم بينهم) implies capability, which requires knowledge.
Their proof is also rational but based on a different understanding of capability.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The Hanafi position is declared as 'al-sahih' (the correct view) for ease and facility. The mufta bihi is that the appointment of a non-mujtahid judge is valid.
Tarjih authority: the text itself states 'wa huwa al-sahih'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شرط للأولوية، فلو قُلد جاهل تحرزانه عن القياس في معارضة النصّ، ومعرفة معاني الآثار؛ ليتمكن من القياس، فالوجه أن يقال: صاحب حديث وفقه؛ ليعرف معاني الآثار، ويمتنع عن القياس. والحاصل أن يعلم بالكتاب والسنّة بأقسامهما من عبارتهما وإشارتهما ودلالتهما واقتضائهما وباقي الأقسام: ناسخها ومنسوخها، ومناطات أحكامهما، ومشروط القياس، والمسائل المجمع عليها؛ لئلا يقع في القياس في مقابلة الإجماع، وأقوال الصحابة، كذا في «الفتح» وقيل: أن يكون صاحب قريحة مع ذل ذلك: أي أحد الأمرين، يعرف بها عادات (1) الناس؛ لأنَّ من الأحكام ما يبتني عليها. كذا في «الهداية»(۲). فهذا القيد لا بُدّ منه في المجتهد، فمن اتقن هذه الجملة فهو أهل الاجتهاد، فيجب عليه أن يُعْمِلَ اجتهاده: وهو أن يبذل جهده في طلب الظن بحكم شرعي عن هذه الأدلة، ولا يقلّد أحداً. كذا في «الفتح» (۳) ، وزيادة التفصيل في مطولات الأصول ومبسوطات الفقهاء الفحول
[١] قوله: شرط للأولوية؛ لا للجواز، وهو الصحيح تيسيراً وتسهيلاً، خلافاً للأئمة الثلاثة؛ لأنه مأمور بالقضاء، والأمرُ به يستدعي القدرة عليه، ولا يثبت القدرة عليه إلا بالعلم؛ لأنَّ الجاهل لا يميّز بين الحق والباطل، ولنا: أن المقصود من القضاء وهو إيصال الحق إلى مستحقه يحصل بفتوى غيره.
[٢] قوله: جاهل؛ قال ابن الغرس: ليس مرادهم بالجاهل العامي المحض، بل لا بد من تأهل العلم والفهم، وأقلُّهُ أن يحسن بعض الحوادث والمسائل الدقيقة، وأن يعرف طريق تحصيل الأحكام الشرعيّة من كتب المذهب وصدور المشايخ، وكيفية الإيراد والإصدار في الوقائع والدعاوى والحجج. انتهى. نقله العلامة الطحطاوي في «حاشيته على الدر المختار»(4) عن العلامة الحموي.
A condition for preferability, so if an ignorant person is appointed... [he] abstains from analogical reasoning when it contradicts the textual evidence, and knows the meanings of traditions; so that he can perform analogical reasoning. The correct view is to say: a person of hadith and fiqh; so that he knows the meanings of traditions and refrains from analogical reasoning. In sum, he must know the Book and the Sunnah with their divisions: their expressions, indications, implications, and requisites, and the remaining divisions: the abrogating and abrogated, the effective causes of their rulings, the conditions of analogical reasoning, and the issues agreed upon by consensus; so that he does not fall into analogical reasoning against consensus and the sayings of the Companions. This is as in 'al-Fath'. And it is said: he should be a person of acute intelligence with that: i.e., one of the two matters, by which he knows the customs of the people, because some rulings are based on them. This is as in 'al-Hidayah'. This condition is necessary for the mujtahid, so whoever masters this summary is qualified for ijtihad, and it is obligatory upon him to exercise his ijtihad: which is to exert his effort in seeking speculative probability of a legal ruling from these evidences, and he should not imitate anyone. This is as in 'al-Fath'. And further detail is in the longer works of usul and the extensive works of the great jurists. [1] He said: 'A condition for preferability; not for permissibility.' This is the correct position, for ease and facility, contrary to the three imams; because he is commanded to judge, and the command necessitates capability for it, and capability is only established through knowledge; because the ignorant cannot distinguish between truth and falsehood. Our proof: the purpose of judgment—delivering the right to its owner—is achieved through the fatwa of others. [2] He said: 'Ignorant.' Ibn al-Ghars said: They do not mean by ignorant the completely uneducated layperson, rather he must have qualifications of knowledge and understanding. The minimum is that he be proficient in some incidents and precise issues, and know the method of acquiring legal rulings from the books of the madhhab and the mouths of the shaykhs, and the procedure of issuing and processing in cases, claims, and proofs. End quote. Al-Allamah al-Tahtawi transmitted it in his Hashiyah 'ala al-Durr al-Mukhtar from al-Allamah al-Hamawi.
Source pages p. 419

Commentary
This passage is a ruling-with-proof from the Hanafi school regarding the condition of knowledge for a judge (qadi). The matn states that knowledge is a condition for preferability (awlawiyya), not for validity (jawaz). This means that if an ignorant person is appointed as a judge, the appointment is still valid, but it is better to appoint a knowledgeable person. The commentary [1] clarifies that this is the correct Hanafi position, contrary to the three other Sunni schools (Shafi'i, Maliki, Hanbali) who argue that knowledge is necessary for the appointment to be valid because the judge is commanded to judge, and capability requires knowledge. The Hanafi proof is that the purpose of judgment—delivering rights to owners—can be achieved by the judge following the fatwa of a qualified scholar, so the appointment is valid even without personal ijtihad. The passage also describes the qualifications of a mujtahid: he must know the Quran and Sunnah, abrogation, effective causes, conditions of qiyas, consensus, and the customs of people. The commentary [2] explains that 'ignorant' here does not mean a complete layperson, but someone who still has some understanding of the madhhab and knows how to refer to scholars for rulings.

Key Terms
شرط الأولوية (sharṭ al-awlawiyya): A condition that is recommended but not required for validity.
جاهل (jāhil): In this context, not a complete layperson but one who has some knowledge of fiqh methodology and can refer to scholars.
مجتهد (mujtahid): One who can derive rulings from the primary sources directly.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, is knowledge (ijtihad) a condition for the validity of appointing a judge?
A) Yes, it is a condition for validity.
B) No, it is only a condition for preferability.
C) No, it is not required at all.
D) Yes, but only in criminal cases.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a judge's appointment is valid even if he is not a mujtahid; knowledge is only a condition for preferability.

Valid Appointment of a Muqallid Judge and Preference for the Best
Confirms validity of appointing a muqallid judge and recommends choosing the most capable.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on appointing a muqallid judge and explain the basis for recommending the most capable candidate.

Core Idea
Appointing a muqallid judge is valid; appointing a less capable one when a better candidate exists is a betrayal.

This passage from the commentary on al-Wiqayah addresses a key question in Islamic judiciary: can a non-mujtahid (muqallid) be appointed as a judge? The Hanafi school affirms it, grounding its position in the purpose of the judiciary and citing a prophetic hadith that warns against nepotism in appointments. This lesson unpacks the ruling and the underlying proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
Read the heading and commentary [1]. What is the initial ruling on appointing a muqallid? The text says 'sah'—it is valid.
2
Understand the reasoning
The commentary explains why it is valid: the purpose of the judiciary is to deliver rights, and this is achieved by acting on the fatwa of a qualified scholar, not necessarily by personal ijtihad.
3
Recognize the recommendation
Commentary [2] adds that the appointing authority should choose the most capable (aqdar) and preferable (awla) in knowledge, religion, and trustworthiness.
4
Analyze the proof
The hadith from al-Tabarani warns against appointing a less qualified person when a better one is available. This is used to support both the validity of appointing a qualified muqallid and the recommendation to select the best.
The hadith does not invalidate the appointment but condemns the negligence.
5
Connect to practice
Consider a modern context: a ruler appoints a judge who holds a degree in Hanafi fiqh but cannot perform independent ijtihad. The appointment is valid, but the ruler must ensure no more qualified candidate was bypassed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
صح، ويُختار الأقدر والأولى
[١] قوله: صح؛ التقليد لما مر من أن المقصود من القضاء إيصال الحق إلى مستحقه؛ وذلك يحصل بالعمل بفتوى غيره.
[٢] قوله: ويختار...الخ؛ يعني أن المناسب للمقلد أن يختار لمن هو الأقدر على القضاء، والأولى علماً وديناً وأمانة لما أخرجَ الطَّبَرَانِيُّ عن ابن عباس رضي الله عنهما قال: قال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم: «من تولى من أمر المسلمين شيئا فاستعمل عليهم رجلا وهو يعلم أن منهم من هو أولى بذلك، وأعلم منه بكتاب الله وسنة رسوله صلى الله عليه وسلم فقد خان الله وجماعة المسلمين»، ذكره ابن الهمام في «الفتح»(۳).
It is valid, and the most capable and preferable is chosen. [1] He said: 'It is valid,' because of what has passed: that the purpose of judgment is to deliver the right to its owner, and that is achieved by acting on the fatwa of another. [2] He said: 'And he chooses...etc.' It means that it is appropriate for the one appointing a muqallid to choose the one most capable of judgment, and the most preferable in knowledge, religion, and trustworthiness, due to what al-Tabarani narrated from Ibn 'Abbas (may Allah be pleased with them both) who said: The Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) said: 'Whoever assumes any matter of the Muslims and appoints over them a man while knowing that there is someone among them more deserving of that, and more knowledgeable of the Book of Allah and the Sunnah of His Messenger, he has betrayed Allah and the entire Muslim community.' Ibn al-Humam mentioned it in 'al-Fath'.
Source pages p. 420

Commentary
This passage provides the Hanafi ruling that appointing a judge who is not a mujtahid but follows a madhhab (muqallid) is valid (sah). The commentary [1] reiterates the proof: the purpose of the judiciary—delivering rights to owners—is achieved through the fatwa of a qualified scholar, so personal ijtihad is not required. The commentary [2] adds that the appointing authority should choose the person most capable (aqdar) and preferable (awla) in knowledge, religion, and trustworthiness. This is supported by a hadith narrated by al-Tabarani: the Prophet (peace be upon him) warned against appointing someone inferior when a more qualified person is available, deeming it a betrayal of Allah and the Muslims. This shows that while the appointment of a muqallid is valid, it is strongly recommended to select the best candidate to uphold justice and avoid treachery.

Key Terms
مقلد (muqallid): A judge who is not a mujtahid but follows the rulings of a madhhab.
الأقدر والأولى (al-aqdar wa al-awlā): The most capable and most preferable in terms of knowledge, religion, and trustworthiness.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It is invalid.
B) It is valid but makruh.
C) It is valid.
D) It is invalid unless he is also a mujtahid.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school validates the appointment of a muqallid judge because the goal of justice is achieved through reliance on qualified scholars, yet it strongly recommends choosing the most capable to avoid betrayal.

Dispute on Appointing a Sinful or Ignorant Judge
Records the Shafi'i position and Hanafi response on appointing unqualified judges.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi and Shafi'i positions on appointing a fasiq or jahil judge, explain the Hanafi response to the Shafi'i proof from hadith, and identify the thamarah al-khilaf.

Core Idea
The appointment of an ignorant judge is valid because the Prophet called him a judge.

In Islamic judiciary, qualifications of a judge are crucial. This passage records a dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools: can a sinful or ignorant person be appointed as a judge? While al-Shafi'i strictly requires knowledge and justice, the Hanafi school upholds validity based on a hadith and practical necessity.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
The Hanafi school holds that appointing a fasiq or jahil judge is valid; knowledge is a condition for preferability, not validity.
2
Identify the opponent's view
Al-Shafi'i holds that such an appointment is invalid; the judge must be knowledgeable, just, and trustworthy.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Al-Shafi'i cites the hadith of three judges: two in Hell (one ignorant, one unjust) and one in Paradise (knowledgeable and just). He argues this shows an ignorant judge is sinful and his appointment invalid.
Hadith: 'al-Qudatu thalathah...'
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafis counter that the hadith calls the ignorant person a judge; if the appointment were invalid, the Prophet would not have used the term 'qadi'. They also argue that requiring full ijtihad and 'adala would shut down the judiciary, causing greater harm.
Hanafi also reinterprets 'jahil' as one who has no fiqh knowledge at all.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
In practice, Hanafi allows muqallid judges; Shafi'i requires mujtahids and just witnesses. This affects the operation of courts in communities lacking highly qualified scholars.
Mufta bihi: Hanafi position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعند الشافعي لا يصح تقليد الفاسق والجاهل.
[٣] قوله: لا يصح تقليد الفاسق والجاهل؛ فعنده لا يجوز إلا أن يكون عالماً عدلاً مأموناً؛ لقوله: «القضاة ثلاثة: قاضيان في النار، وقاض في الجنة...» الحديث، ففسر القاضيين أحدهما جاهل يحكم بالجهل، والآخر عالم يحكم بالجور، والثالث العالم العادل يحكم بعلمه، وجوابنا عن هذا الاستدلال بأنه سماه قاضياً، ولولا أن التولية تصح لما سمّاه قاضياً.
[٤] قوله: لارتفع ... الخ؛ ولذا قال ابن الهمام في «الفتح»: والوجه تنفيذ قضاء ... واعلم أنه قد كان الاحتياط فيما قال الشافعي لكن بحسب الزمان، لو شرط العلم والعدالة لارتفع أمر القضاء بالكلية، ووقع الشر والفساد أعظم مما احترز عنه. لكن بالنظر إلى تعليلهم بقولهم: إن الأمر بالقضاء يستدعي القدرة، ولا قدرة بدون العلم، يحتمل أن يكون المراد بالجاهل: من لا يحفظ شيئاً من أقوال الفقهاء؛ لأنهم يقولون في التعليل بدون العلم، ولا يقولون بدون الاجتهاد.
According to al-Shafi'i, appointing a sinful or ignorant person is not valid. [3] He said: 'It is not valid to appoint a sinful or ignorant person.' According to him, it is not permissible unless he is knowledgeable, just, and trustworthy; due to his statement: 'The judges are three: two in the Fire, and one in Paradise...' hadith. He interpreted the two judges: one ignorant who judges with ignorance, and the other knowledgeable who judges with tyranny, and the third is the knowledgeable just judge who judges with his knowledge. Our response to this argument is that [the Prophet] called him a judge; if the appointment were not valid, he would not have called him a judge. [4] He said: 'It would cease... etc.' Therefore, Ibn al-Humam said in 'al-Fath': the correct view is to enforce his judgment... Know that precaution was in what al-Shafi'i said, but according to the time, if knowledge and justice were required, the matter of the judiciary would cease entirely, and evil and corruption would be greater than what he sought to avoid. However, considering their argument that the command to judge requires capability, and no capability without knowledge, it is possible that what is meant by 'ignorant' is one who does not memorize anything of the statements of the jurists; because they say in the rationale 'without knowledge' and do not say 'without ijtihad'.
Source pages p. 420

Commentary
This passage presents an inter-madhhab dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools. Al-Shafi'i holds that appointing a sinful (fasiq) or ignorant (jahil) person as a judge is invalid (la yasihh). He requires the judge to be knowledgeable, just, and trustworthy (amin). His proof is the hadith of 'three judges': two are in Hell—one ignorant who judges ignorantly, and one knowledgeable who judges unjustly—and one knowledgeable just judge is in Paradise. Al-Shafi'i argues that the ignorant judge's appointment is invalid because he is called a judge in the hadith, but that does not prove validity. The Hanafi response, given in commentary [3], is that the Prophet (peace be upon him) called him a judge, and unless the appointment was valid (sahih), he would not have been called a judge. The term 'qadi' is applied to him, implying that the appointment is valid, even if he is blameworthy. Commentary [4] adds a practical justification: if we required the judge to be both knowledgeable and just (as al-Shafi'i demands), the institution of judiciary would collapse entirely, especially in later times when finding fully qualified mujtahids is difficult. The resulting evil would be greater than the evil of appointing a less qualified judge. The Hanafi compromise is that the judge must have some knowledge (at least of madhhab rulings) to avoid being completely ignorant, but need not be a mujtahid. This is the Hanafi reliance on 'taysir' (facilitation) and 'maslaha' (public interest).

Key Terms
فاسق (fāsiq): A person who commits major sins or persists in minor sins, lacking justice ('adala).
تقليد (taqlīd): Appointing a judge who follows the rulings of a madhhab rather than exercising ijtihad.
مأمون (ma'mūn): Trustworthy, free from suspicion of partiality or corruption.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How does the Hanafi school counter al-Shafi'i's argument from the hadith 'al-Qudatu thalathah'?
A) By rejecting the hadith as weak.
B) By noting that the hadith calls the ignorant person a judge, implying the appointment is valid.
C) By arguing that the hadith only applies to criminal cases.
D) By stating that the ignorant judge is not actually a judge.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school upholds the validity of appointing a sinful or partially ignorant judge based on the hadith's terminology and the necessity of keeping the judiciary functional.

Qada — Judiciary

Validity of a Muqallid Judge and the Shafi'i Objection
Clarifies the permissibility of appointing a muqallid judge and refutes the Shafi'i condition of ijtihad.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on appointing a muqallid judge and evaluate the Shafi'i objection against it.

Core Idea
A muqallid judge is valid; necessity overrides the ideal of ijtihad.

This passage from the Hanafi commentary tackles a critical question: can a judge who is not a mujtahid be validly appointed? The Hanafi school answers yes, while the Shafi'i school insists on ijtihad and justice. The resolution relies on practical necessity (darurah) and the linguistic fact that the Prophet himself called ignorant and unjust judges 'judges'. Let's examine the arguments and the Hanafi response.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
The base text states 'Saḥḥa wa yukhtār al-aqdar' – it is valid and the most capable is chosen. The judge may be a muqallid; the purpose of qadā is to deliver rights, which can be achieved by acting on another's fatwa.
The Hanafi school does not require the judge to be a mujtahid.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that it is not valid to appoint a fasiq (sinner) or ignorant person. The judge must be knowledgeable, just, and trustworthy.
The Shafi'i position is based on the hadith of the three judges.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
The Shafi'i cite the hadith: 'The judges are three: two in the Fire and one in Paradise.' They interpret the first two as the ignorant judge and the unjust judge, implying that only the knowledgeable just judge is valid.
They also argue that the command to judge requires capability, which requires knowledge.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response is twofold. First, the Prophet called the ignorant and unjust men 'judges', proving the appointment is legally valid (otherwise he would not have called them judges). Second, requiring ijtihad would make it impossible to find judges, leading to greater corruption. Hence, necessity allows a lower standard.
Ibn al-Humam states that the apparent madhhab is to execute the judgment of any judge appointed by a powerful ruler, even if ignorant and fasiq.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Practically, in the Hanafi school, a muqallid judge's rulings are enforceable, and he may follow the fatwa of a scholar. In the Shafi'i school, such a ruling would be invalid unless the judge is a mujtahid.
The Hanafi position is mufta bihi due to necessity (istihsan).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
صح، ويُختار الأقدر والأولى) وعند الشَّافِعِي له لا يصح تقليد الفاسق والجاهل. واعلم أنه قد كان الإحتياط فيما قال الشَّافِعِيُّ له لكن بحسب الزَّمانِ، لو شرط العلم والعدالة لارتفع أمر القضاء بالكلية، ووقعُ الشَّرِّ والفسادِ أعظم مما احترز عنه. لكن بالنظر إلى تعليلهم بقولهم : إنَّ الأمر بالقضاء يستدعي القدرة، ولا قدرة بدون العلم، يحتمل أن يكون المراد بالجاهل : مَن لا يحفظ شيئاً من أقوال الفقهاء ; لأنهم يقولون في التعليل بدون العلم، ولا يقولون بدون الاجتهاد. قوله : صح ; التقليد لما مر من أن المقصود من القضاء إيصال الحق إلى مستحقه ; وذلك يحصل بالعمل بفتوى غيره. قوله : ويختار...الخ; يعني أن المناسب للمقلد أن يختار لمن هو الأقدر على القضاء، والأولى علماً وديناً وأمانة لما أخرجَ الطَّبَرَانِيُّ عن ابن عباس قال : قال رسول الله : من تولى من أمر المسلمين شيئا فاستعمل عليهم رجلا وهو يعلم أنّ منهم من هو أولى بذلك، وأعلم منه بكتاب الله وسنة رسوله فقد خان الله وجماعة المسلمين، ذكره ابن الهمام في «الفتح». قوله : لا يصح تقليد الفاسق والجاهل ; فعنده لا يجوز إلا أن يكون عالماً عدلاً مأموناً ; لقوله : «القضاة ثلاثة : قاضيان في النار، وقاض في الجنة... الحديث ، ففسر القاضيين أحدهما جاهل يحكم بالجهل، والآخرُ عالم يحكم بالجور، والثالث العالم العادل يحكم بعلمه، وجوابنا عن هذا الاستدلال بأنه سماه قاضياً، ولولا أن التولية تصح لما سمّاه قاضياً. قوله : لارتفع ... الخ ; ولذا قال ابنُ الهمام في «الفتح» : والوجه تنفيذ قضاء كلِّ مَن ولاه سلطان ذو شوكة، وإن كان جاهلاً فاسقاً، وهو ظاهر المذهب عندنا ، وحينئذ فيحكم بفتوى غيره. انتهى.
It is valid, and the most capable is chosen (and it is better) and according to al-Shafi'i, it is not valid to follow (taqlid of) a fasiq and an ignorant person. Know that precaution was in what al-Shafi'i said, but according to the time, if knowledge and justice were required, the matter of judiciary would be completely eliminated, and the occurrence of evil and corruption would be greater than what he avoided. But considering their reasoning by saying that the command to judge requires capability, and no capability without knowledge, it is possible that what is meant by 'ignorant' is one who does not memorize anything from the statements of the jurists; because they say in the reasoning 'without knowledge' and do not say 'without ijtihad'. His statement: 'Saḥḥ' (it is valid) because taqlid [is valid] due to what has passed that the purpose of judging is to deliver the right to its owner; and that is achieved by acting on the fatwa of someone else. His statement: 'Wa yukhtār...' means that it is appropriate for the muqallid to choose the one most capable of judging, and the best in knowledge, religion, and trustworthiness, because al-Tabarani narrated from Ibn Abbas that the Prophet said: 'Whoever undertakes a matter of the Muslims and then appoints over them a man while knowing that among them is one more deserving of that and more knowledgeable of the Book of Allah and the Sunnah of His Messenger, then he has betrayed Allah and the Muslims.' Mentioned by Ibn al-Humam in al-Fath. His statement: 'It is not valid to follow the fasiq and ignorant' according to him (al-Shafi'i) it is not permissible unless he is knowledgeable, just, and trustworthy; because of his saying: 'The judges are three: two judges in the Fire, and one judge in Paradise...' the hadith. He interpreted the two judges: one ignorant who judges by ignorance, and another knowledgeable who judges by injustice, and the third is the knowledgeable just judge who judges by his knowledge. Our response to this inference is that he called him a judge, and had the appointment (tawliya) not been valid, he would not have called him a judge. His statement: 'Lārtafa'a...' thus Ibn al-Humam said in al-Fath: 'The correct view is to execute the judgment of everyone whom a powerful sultan appoints, even if he is ignorant and fasiq, and this is the apparent madhhab with us, and then he judges by the fatwa of others.' End.
Source pages p. 420

Commentary
Passage type: The author discusses the ruling on appointing a judge who is a muqallid (follower of a madhhab) and the disagreement with the Shafi'i school. The matn (base text) states: 'Saḥḥ, wa yukhtār al-aqdar' — it is valid to appoint a muqallid as judge, and it is recommended to choose the most capable. The author explains that 'saḥḥ' refers to the validity of taqlid in qadā because the purpose of qadā is to deliver rights to their owners, and this can be achieved by acting on the fatwa of another qualified scholar. The author then addresses the Shafi'i position that it is not valid to follow (i.e., appoint) a fasiq or ignorant person. The Shafi'i argument is based on the hadith about three judges. The Hanafi response is powerful: if the appointment were invalid, the Prophet would not have called such a person a 'judge'. The very fact that he is called a judge indicates the validity of the appointment. Furthermore, the author notes the practical reality: if we required the judge to be a mujtahid (fully qualified), the judiciary would collapse, leading to greater corruption. This is a classic Hanafi application of istihsān (juristic preference) to preserve the institution of qadā. The passage then cites Ibn al-Humam's ruling that the apparent madhhab (al-madhhab al-zāhir) in the Hanafi school is to execute the judgment of any judge appointed by a powerful ruler, even if ignorant or fasiq, because he will rule based on the fatwa of others. This reflects the school's pragmatism.

Key Terms
تقليد (taqlīd): Following the opinion of a mujtahid without knowing the evidence. In this passage, it refers to a judge acting on the fatwa of a scholar rather than exercising ijtihad.
قضاء (qaḍā): The office of judge, or the act of judging. The purpose is to deliver rights to their owners.
مقلد (muqallid): One who performs taqlid—here, a judge who follows the fatwa of a mujtahid rather than making ijtihad.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, can a judge who is not a mujtahid (muqallid) be validly appointed?
A) No, he must be a mujtahid.
B) Yes, but only if he is just.
C) Yes, it is valid even if he is ignorant and fasiq.
D) Yes, it is valid and recommended to choose the most capable.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, the appointment of a muqallid judge is valid due to practical necessity, even if the ideal would be a mujtahid, and the judge may rule by following the fatwa of a scholar.

The Prohibition of Seeking the Position of Judge
Explains the ruling on seeking the office of qadi and exceptions when it becomes obligatory.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on seeking the position of qadi, cite the primary evidence from the hadith, and explain when it becomes obligatory.

Core Idea
Seeking judgeship is prohibited unless you are uniquely qualified; then it becomes obligatory.

The position of judge (qadi) carries immense responsibility. The Prophet ﷺ warned against actively seeking it. Yet, when the community has no one else suitable, the ruling flips — it becomes a duty. This lesson unpacks the evidence and the exception.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its default ruling
The act is actively seeking the position of judge. The default ruling is prohibition (makruh tahrimi) as the matn states 'he should not seek'.
The prohibition applies to both heart and tongue.
2
Locate the dalil (proof)
The author cites the hadith: 'Whoever asks for the office of judge is left to himself, and whoever is forced upon it, an angel descends to guide him.' This is narrated by Abu Dawood, Tirmidhi, and Ibn Majah.
The hadith addresses both seeking and being forced.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah (indication)
The phrase 'left to himself' implies deprivation of divine support, indicating the act is disliked. The phrase 'angel descends' for the forced indicates divine aid, showing permissibility when not sought.
Seeking shows self-reliance; being forced shows reliance on Allah.
4
Note the exception
If no one else is qualified for the role, seeking becomes obligatory to protect rights and prevent oppression. The source quotes al-Bahr for this ruling.
This exception is based on necessity (darura).
5
Evaluate the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position: seeking is prohibited unless necessary. The appointment of a seeker is valid but he is sinful for seeking.
Sources like Jami' al-Rumuz and al-Mansuriyyah support this.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يطلب القضاء) أي لا يميل إليه بالقلب، لما أخرجه أبو داود والتُّرْمِذِيُّ وابن ماجة من حديث أنس بن مالك قال : قال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم: «من سأل القضاء وكل إلى نفسه، ومَن أُجبر عليه ينزل إليه مَلَك يسدده ) : أي يلهم الرشد ، ويوفقه الصواب ; ولأن من طلبه يعتمدُ على نفسه فيحرم من التوفيق ، ومن أجبر عليه يتوكل على ربه فيلهمه، فينبغي أن لا يشتغل أحدهما لو ناله (بما يحرم من التوفيق. وفي قوله : لا يطلب لقضاء ; إشعار بأنه لا ينبغي أن يميل إليه باللسان بالطريق الأولى. كذا في «جامع الرموز»، وقيل: الطلب أن يقول للإمام: ولني، والسؤال أن يقول للناس : لو ولاني الإمامُ لأجبته ، وغرضُهُ أن يبلغ ذلك الخبر إلى الإمام. وفي «المنصورية»: لا ينبغي لأحدٍ أن يطلب القضاء ، ولو فعل فهو مسيء وقيل : إن من طلب القضاء لا يقلد ، وفي «الخلاصة»: لا يترك القاضي على القضاء أكثر من سنته ; وذلك لأنه إذا ترك أكثر من ذلك استأنس ، وصار بحيث لو عزل لطلب ثانياً ، وأيضاً إذا استأنس به يداهنُ في معاملاتِ بعض الناس خوفاً من العزل. كذا في «شرح النقاية» للعلامة البرْجَنْدِي. أما إذا تعين بأن لم يكن أحد غيره يصلح للقضاء وجب عليه الطلب صيانة لحقوق المسلمين ودفعاً لظلم الظالمين. كذا في «البحر».
He should not seek the office of judge; i.e., he should not incline towards it in his heart, due to what Abu Dawood, al-Tirmidhi, and Ibn Majah narrated from Anas ibn Malik that the Prophet said: 'Whoever asks for the office of judge is left to himself, and whoever is forced upon it, an angel descends to him to guide him aright.' That is, he inspires him with rectitude and grants him success. Because the one who seeks it relies on himself and is deprived of success, while the one forced upon it relies on his Lord and is inspired. So he should not occupy himself with what deprives him of success if he obtains it. In his saying 'he should not seek the office of judge' there is an indication that he should not incline towards it with his tongue, a fortiori. As in Jami' al-Rumuz. It is said: Seeking (talab) is that he says to the ruler: 'Appoint me'; and asking (su'al) is that he says to the people: 'If the ruler appoints me, I would accept,' his intention being that the news reaches the ruler. In al-Mansuriyya: It is not appropriate for anyone to seek the office of judge, and if he does so, he is sinful. It is said: One who seeks the office should not be appointed. In al-Khulasa: The judge should not be kept in office for more than one year; because if he stays longer, he becomes accustomed, and if he is removed, he will seek it again. Also, if he becomes accustomed, he may flatter some people for fear of removal. As in Sharh al-Nuqaya by al-Barjandi. However, if it becomes specific that there is no one else suitable for the office, then seeking it becomes obligatory to protect the rights of Muslims and repel the oppression of oppressors. As in al-Bahr.
Source pages p. 421

Commentary
Passage type: This is a ruling on the prohibition of seeking the position of judge (qadi). The matn states: 'He should not seek the office of judge.' The author explains that this refers to seeking it in one's heart and desire, based on the well-known hadith: 'Whoever asks for the office of judge is left to himself, and whoever is forced upon it, an angel descends to help him.' This hadith indicates that seeking it leads to being deprived of divine support (tawfiq), whereas accepting it when forced brings divine aid. The author then discusses the proper attitude: one should not even speak about wanting to be a judge. However, there is an important exception: if the person is uniquely qualified such that no one else is suitable, then it becomes obligatory to seek the office to prevent injustice. This is a classic Hanafi balance between the general prohibition and the necessity of preserving the Islamic judiciary.

Key Terms
طلب القضاء (ṭalab al-qaḍā): Actively seeking appointment as a judge by asking the ruler or hinting to people. It is prohibited (makruh tahrimi) unless necessary.
إجبار (ijbār): Being forced to accept the position of judge by the ruler. In that case, it is permissible and the judge receives divine help.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling for seeking the position of judge when no one else is qualified?
A) Permissible (mubah)
B) Obligatory (wajib)
C) Prohibited (haram)
D) Recommended (mustahab)

Key Takeaway
Seeking judicial office is normally prohibited, but when you are the only qualified person, it becomes your duty to seek or accept it to uphold justice.

Permissibility of Accepting Judgeship for the Just Person
States the ruling that accepting judgeship is valid for a just person.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on accepting judgeship and cite the evidence from the practice of the Sahaba.

Core Idea
Accepting judgeship is valid for a just person, following the practice of the Companions.

This lesson examines a key ruling on judgeship: is it permissible to accept the position of qadi? The passage presents the Hanafi position that a just person may accept, supported by the example of the Companions, while also noting a stricter view. Understanding this ruling clarifies the balance between avoiding the pursuit of authority and fulfilling communal obligations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling
Find the explicit statement in the matn: it is valid (sahih) to enter judgeship for one who trusts his justice. Note the condition: confidence in one's own piety and capability.
The condition is self-awareness, not external certification.
2
Examine the Proof (Dalil Naqli)
The author cites the practice of the great Companions and Successors who accepted judgeship. This is a historical precedent (dalil naqli) that justifies the permissibility.
The Sahaba are a sufficient model (kafa bihim qadwah) — this carries weight in Hanafi usul.
3
Consider the Opposing View
A contrary opinion (qila) holds that it is not permissible at all without compulsion, based on the hadith: 'Whoever asks for judgeship is left to himself.'
This hadith is about SEEKING the office, not accepting when offered.
4
Reconcile and Derive the Hanafi Position
The Hanafi school distinguishes between seeking (which is discouraged) and accepting (which is valid for the just). The example of the Sahaba overrides the literal reading of the hadith.
The mufta bihi is permissibility with the condition of trust in one's own justice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وصح الدخول فيه لمن يثق عدله : أي يعتمد على ديانته وأداء حقه ; لأنّ كبار الصحابة والتابعين رضوان الله تعالى عليهم أجمعين تقلدوه، وكفى بهم قدوة ، وقيل : لا يجوز الدخول مطلقاً بلا إجبار ; لقوله : «من سأل القضاء وكل إلى نفسه» الحديث.
And it is valid to enter into it (i.e., judgeship) for one who trusts his justice, i.e., relies on his piety and fulfillment of rights; because the great Companions and Successors (may Allah be pleased with them all) undertook it, and they are sufficient as a model. And it is said: It is not permissible to enter into it at all without compulsion, because of his saying: 'Whoever asks for the office of judge is left to himself.'
Source pages p. 421

Commentary
Passage type: This is a ruling on the permissibility of accepting the position of judge for a person who trusts his own justice. The matn states: 'It is valid to enter into it for one who trusts his justice.' The author explains that this refers to someone who is confident in his own piety and ability to fulfill the rights of others. The proof is the practice of the Companions and Successors who accepted the role despite the general discouragement of seeking it. However, there is also a stricter view that it is absolutely not permissible without compulsion, based on the hadith about seeking judgeship. The Hanafi school reconciles these: it is permissible for a just person to accept if offered, but one should not actively seek it. The phrase 'trusts his justice' indicates that one must have self-awareness of one's own competence and integrity.

Key Terms
الدخول في القضاء (al-dukhūl fī al-qaḍā): Entering into the office of judge, i.e., accepting the appointment. It is permissible for a just person.
عدل ('adl): Justice, meaning uprightness and trustworthiness. In this context, it refers to the judge's own confidence in his integrity to deliver justice.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the author cite the practice of the Sahaba?
A) To prove that accepting judgeship is obligatory.
B) To prove that accepting judgeship is permissible.
C) To prove that accepting judgeship is forbidden.
D) To prove that accepting judgeship is disliked.

Key Takeaway
Accepting judgeship is valid for a just person who trusts his own integrity, following the precedent of the Companions.

Ruling on Seeking and Accepting Judgeship
Ruling on seeking and accepting judgeship with proofs and exceptions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on seeking judgeship and explain how the ruling changes based on circumstances (makruh, wajib, haram).

Core Idea
Seeking judgeship is makruh, but becomes wajib if you are the only qualified person.

In this passage, we explore the Hanafi ruling on seeking and accepting the position of a judge. The text presents a nuanced position: while generally disliked, seeking judgeship can become obligatory under necessity, and may even be forbidden if one anticipates injustice. The discussion includes the famous stance of Imam Abu Hanifa who refused judgeship despite persecution.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its default ruling
What is the act under discussion? (seeking judgeship). What is the default hukm according to the text? (makruh).
2
Locate the primary proof
What is the hadith cited and how does it support the ruling? It contrasts seeking with being forced, indicating blame for seeking.
Dalil: 'Whoever asks for judgeship is entrusted to himself...'
3
Trace the exceptions and conditions
Under what circumstances does the ruling change to wajib or haram? If one is the only qualified person, seeking becomes wajib. If one fears injustice, it becomes haram.
Also: if forced or appointed by a ruler, it is permissible.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi and cross-madhhab ikhtilaf
What is the disagreement among Hanafis (Qadi Khan's dislike) and with other schools (Shafi'is deem it haram)?
5
Determine the practical consequence
What does this mean for someone considering judgeship today? The ruling guides whether to accept or decline based on personal circumstances and the legal environment.

[٢] قوله : وصح الدخولُ فيه ؛ أي في القضاء لمن يثق عدله : أي يعتمد على ديانته وأداء حقه ؛ لأنّ كبار الصحابة والتابعين رضوان الله تعالى عليهم أجمعين تقلدوه، وكفى بهم قدوة ، وقيل : لا يجوز الدخول مطلقاً بلا إجبار ؛ لقوله : «من
ابتلي بالقضاء فكأنما ذبح بغير سكين).
وقد روي أنَّ الإمام الأعظم دعي للقضاء ثلاث مرات، فأبى حتى حبس وجلد في كل مرّة ثلاثون سوطاً، حتى قال له أبو يوسف : لو تقلّدت لنفعت الناس، فنظر إليه شبه المغضب، فقال : لو أمرت أن أقطع البحر سباحة لكنتُ أقدر عليه ، قال أبو يوسف الله : البحر عميق، والسفينة وثيق، والملاح عالم، فقال الإمام :
كأني بك قاضياً. وذكر البَزَّازي : إنّ الإمام لم يقبل القضاء، ومات على الإباء، وكره بعضُهم الدخول فيه مختاراً، والصحيح أنّ الدخول فيه رخصة طمعاً في إقامة العدل، والترك عزيمة، إلا إذا تعيّن للقضاء، فحينئذ يعترض عليه، واختار قاضي خان كراهة الدخول مع استجماع الشرائط، والتفصيل في المبسوطات.
(۱) قوله : وكره...الخ ؛ لئلا يكون ذريعة إلى مباشرة الظلم والعجز، إما أن يراد به العجز عن سماع دعاوى كل الخصوم بأن قدرَ على البعض فقط، وإما أن يراد به العجزُ عن القيام بواجباتِهِ من إظهار الحق وعدم أخذ الرشوة ، ولو كان غالب ظنه أنه يجوز في الحكم ينبغي أن يكون حراماً. كذا في البحر )
(۲)
[٢]قوله : سأل ديوان قاض قبله ؛ الديوان الخرائط، جمع خريطة، وهو الكيس
الذي فيه السجلات، جمع السجل ، بكسر السين والجيم، وتشديد اللام، وهـو الصك، ومنه إسجال القاضي وتسجيله المحاضر وغيرهـا مـن الصكوك التي فيها الإقرارات، أو قيم الأشياء، أو نصبُ الأوصياء في أموال اليتامى ونحو ذلك ؛ لأنَّ
الديوان وضع ليكون حجّةٌ عند الحاجة ، فيجعل في يد من له ولاية القضاء.
And he should not seek judgeship; and it is valid to enter into it for one who trusts his justice. [From] everyone whom a sultan possessing power appoints, even if he is an ignorant sinner, and this is the apparent [position] of our school. And then he rules by the fatwa of others. End. [1] His statement: and he should not seek judgeship; meaning he should not incline towards it in his heart, due to what Abu Dawud, al-Tirmidhi, and Ibn Majah narrated from the hadith of Anas ibn Malik that the Messenger of Allah said: 'Whoever asks for judgeship is entrusted to himself, and whoever is forced upon it, an angel descends to guide him' — i.e., he is inspired with rectitude and granted success. And because whoever seeks it relies on himself and is deprived of divine guidance, and whoever is forced upon it relies on his Lord and is inspired. So one should not engage in it if he obtains it by [means that] deprive him of guidance. And in his statement 'he should not seek judgeship' there is indication that he should not incline towards it with his tongue, a fortiori. Thus in Jami' al-Rumuz. And it is said: 'seeking' is to say to the ruler: appoint me; and 'asking' is to say to people: if the ruler appointed me, I would accept it, intending that the news reaches the ruler. In al-Mansuriyya: no one should seek judgeship, and if he does, he is sinful. And it is said: whoever seeks judgeship is not appointed. In al-Khulasa: the judge should not be left in office for more than one year, because if left longer, he becomes accustomed and if removed he would seek it again, and also if he becomes accustomed he flatters some people for fear of removal. Thus in Sharh al-Niqaya by al-Birjandi. But if he is the only qualified person, then seeking becomes obligatory to protect the rights of Muslims and repel the oppression of oppressors. Thus in al-Bahr. [2] His statement: and it is valid to enter into it; i.e., judgeship, for one who trusts his justice — i.e., relies on his uprightness and fulfilling his duty. Because the senior Companions and Successors undertook it, and they suffice as role models. And it is said: entering it absolutely without compulsion is not permitted, due to his saying: 'whoever is tried with judgeship is as if slaughtered without a knife.' And it is narrated that the Greatest Imam was called to judgeship three times, and he refused until he was imprisoned and flogged thirty lashes each time, until Abu Yusuf said to him: If you accept it, you would benefit the people. He looked at him angrily and said: If I were ordered to cut the sea by swimming, I would be able to do it. Abu Yusuf said: The sea is deep, the ship is sturdy, and the pilot is knowledgeable. The Imam said: It is as if I see you as a judge. And al-Bazzazi mentioned that the Imam did not accept judgeship and died refusing. Some dislike entering it voluntarily, but the correct view is that entering it is a concession out of hope for establishing justice, and refraining is a resolve, unless one is specifically designated for judgeship — then it becomes objectionable. Qadi Khan chose the dislike of entering it even if all conditions are met, and the detail is in the works. [1] His statement: and it is disliked, etc.; so that it does not become a means to commit oppression and inability. Either what is meant is inability to hear all the litigants' claims, i.e., he can only hear some, or inability to fulfill his duties of manifesting truth and not taking bribes. If his predominant opinion is that he will commit injustice in ruling, it should be forbidden. Thus in al-Bahr. [2] His statement: he should ask for the register of the previous judge; al-diwan means the bundles, plural of kharita, which is the bag containing the records. Records are the documents, and from this is the judge's issuance of records and registration of proceedings and other documents containing acknowledgments, values of things, or appointment of guardians over orphans' wealth, etc., because the register is placed to serve as evidence when needed, and it is placed in the hands of the one who has authority of judgeship.
Source pages p. 421

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling on seeking and accepting the position of judge (qada). It begins by stating that one should not seek judgeship, meaning he should not desire it in his heart, based on the hadith: 'Whoever asks for judgeship is entrusted to himself, and whoever is forced upon it, an angel descends to guide him.' The commentator explains that seeking judgeship leads to relying on oneself, thereby losing divine guidance, while being forced leads to reliance on Allah and thus inspiration. This principle extends to not seeking it verbally either. However, if no one else is qualified, seeking becomes obligatory to protect Muslims' rights. The passage then confirms the validity of entering judgeship for one who is confident in his justice, citing the practice of senior Companions and Successors. However, if one fears inability or oppression, it is disliked (makruh) and can even be haram if he knows he will commit injustice. Finally, when appointed, the judge should ask for the records (diwan) of the previous judge to ensure continuity and evidence. The text also relates the famous story of Imam Abu Hanifa's refusal of judgeship despite severe pressure, demonstrating the preferability of avoidance.

Key Terms
القضاء (al-qada): The office of judge; the authority to adjudicate disputes and implement Islamic law.
الطلب (al-talab): Actively seeking or soliciting the position of judge from the ruler or others.
الديوان (al-diwan): The register or bundle of records containing court documents, judgments, and official papers.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling for a person who fears he will be unable to fulfill the duties of a judge or fears committing oppression?
A) It is permissible as a concession.
B) It is recommended (mandub).
C) It is disliked (makruh).
D) It is obligatory (wajib).

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi ruling on seeking judgeship is nuanced: generally disliked, but can become obligatory under necessity or forbidden if injustice is anticipated.

Qada — Judiciary
