Judgeship When Fearing Injustice: Ruling and Proof
States the ruling of dislike for accepting judgeship when fearing incapacity or injustice

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for accepting judgeship when fearing incapacity, identify the proof, and distinguish between the azima and rukhsa positions.

Core Idea
Accepting judgeship is disliked when fearing injustice, but it is a concession (rukhsa) for the capable.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya discusses the delicate balance between the duty of justice and the risk of falling into injustice. The matn states that it is disliked for one who fears his own incapacity or injustice to accept judgeship. The commentary then weaves together a hadith about the severity of judgeship, the exemplary caution of Imam Abu Hanifa, and the nuanced ikhtilaf among later Hanafi scholars. Understanding this ruling requires distinguishing between the ideal (azima) and the permissible (rukhsa), and recognizing that necessity can transform the ruling entirely.

Guided Path
1
Identify the prima facie ruling
The matn states 'ukriha' (it is disliked). Determine the type of karaaha: tanzihiyya (recommended avoidance) or tahrimiyya (near haram)? The fear of incapacity or injustice makes it tanzihiyya, but it could become haram if certain of injustice.
Karaaha tanzihiyya is common when the source of dislike is a worldly risk or a missing virtue, not a clear prohibition.
2
Locate the proofs (dalil naqli)
The commentator provides two proofs: a hadith (the one afflicted with judgeship is like being slaughtered without a knife, indicating extreme danger) and the narrative of Imam Abu Hanifa's refusal despite severe pressure. These demonstrate the dislike and the preference for avoidance.
The hadith is not quoted verbatim but referenced. In Hanafi usul, a hadith like this provides a strong discouragement (karahiyya).
3
Trace the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
After stating the matn, the commentary presents variations: al-Bazzazi reports Imam Abu Hanifa's absolute refusal and death upon it. Some scholars maintain dislike even voluntarily. The correct view (sahih) per this sharh is that entering is a rukhsa (permissible) with hope of justice, while abstention is the azima. Qadi Khan still chose dislike even with all conditions present.
The ikhtilaf is about the weight given to the risk versus the potential benefit. A jurist's assessment of probability influences the ruling.
4
Determine the mufta bihi (operative position)
The commentator clearly states 'wa al-sahih anna al-dukhul fihi rukhsah' – the sound position is that it is a concession. However, if the person is uniquely qualified (ta'ayyun), it becomes obligatory (fard kifayah). This is the relied-upon view for fatwa.
The tarjih authority is the commentator himself, and his view is from the later Hanafi tradition (fatawa mutakhkhirin).
5
Apply to modern scenarios
Today, the same principles apply to any position of authority where there is fear of bias or inability. The key is to evaluate one's own capability and the necessity of the role. If no one else is qualified, acceptance becomes compulsory.
This is a living fiqh principle: the ruling changes with the situation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكره لمن خاف عجزه وحيفه. ابتلي بالقضاء فكأنما ذبح بغير سكين. وقد روي أنَّ الإمام الأعظم دعي للقضاء ثلاث مرات، فأبى حتى حبس وجلد في كل مرّة ثلاثون سوطاً، حتى قال له أبو يوسف : لو تقلّدت لنفعت الناس، فنظر إليه شبه المغضب، فقال : لو أمرت أن أقطع البحر سباحة لكنتُ أقدر عليه ، قال أبو يوسف : البحر عميق، والسفينة وثيق، والملاح عالم، فقال الإمام : كأني بك قاضياً. وذكر البَزَّازي : إنّ الإمام لم يقبل القضاء، ومات على الإباء، وكره بعضُهم الدخول فيه مختاراً، والصحيح أنّ الدخول فيه رخصة طمعاً في إقامة العدل، والترك عزيمة، إلا إذا تعيّن للقضاء، فحينئذ يعترض عليه، واختار قاضي خان كراهة الدخول مع استجماع الشرائط، والتفصيل في المبسوطات.
And it is disliked for one who fears his incapacity and injustice. He who is afflicted with judgeship is as if he is slaughtered without a knife. And it is narrated that the Greatest Imam was called to judgeship three times, so he refused until he was imprisoned and flogged each time thirty lashes, until Abu Yusuf said to him: If you would accept, you would benefit the people. So he looked at him with something like anger and said: If I were ordered to cross the sea by swimming, I would be able to do it. Abu Yusuf said: The sea is deep, the ship is sturdy, and the captain is knowledgeable. So the Imam said: I see you as a judge. And al-Bazzazi mentioned that the Imam did not accept judgeship and died in refusal. And some disliked entering into it voluntarily. And the correct view is that entering into it is a concession (rukhsa) hoping to establish justice, and leaving it is the original determination (azima), except if it becomes specified for him (there is no other qualified person), then he is compelled to accept it. And Qadi Khan chose the dislike of entering it even when all conditions are present. The detail is in the larger works.
Source pages p. 422

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof (hukm with dalil) regarding the acceptance of judgeship (qada). The matn states that it is disliked (makruh) for a person who fears his own incapacity (ajz) to fulfill the duties or injustice (hayf) in judgment. The commentator then provides a hadith (narrated in Tirmidhi, Abu Dawud, and others) that whoever is burdened with judgeship is like being slaughtered without a knife, indicating the immense responsibility and danger. The story of Imam Abu Hanifa is cited to illustrate the extreme caution of the early scholars: he was forced to accept judgeship, imprisoned and flogged, yet he steadfastly refused, even predicting that Abu Yusuf would eventually become a judge. This shows the preferred avoidance. The commentator then presents an intra-Hanafi dispute: some scholars (like al-Bazzazi) maintain the dislike of voluntary acceptance, while the correct (sahih) view according to the commentator is that entering is a rukhsa (concession) permitted with the hope of establishing justice, but abstention remains the azima (more precautionary ruling). However, if the person is specifically designated (ta'ayyun) and no one else is qualified, then it becomes obligatory (fard kifayah) and he must accept; otherwise he is sinning. Qadi Khan held that it is still disliked even when all conditions are met. The detailed discussion is found in extended works like al-Bahr al-Ra'iq. The mukhtar (preferred) position is the rukhsa view, but the text leans toward the dislike as a matter of precaution.

Key Terms
القضاء (al-qada): Judgeship; the official authority to settle disputes and implement rulings.
كراهة (karaaha): Dislike; a ruling that recommends avoidance without being sinful, but doing it is permissible.
عزيمة (azima): The original, stricter ruling that applies in the absence of a valid excuse or need.
رخصة (rukhsa): A concession that allows an act normally prohibited or discouraged due to a valid reason.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling (hukm) for a person who fears he will be unable to fulfill the duties of a judge or fears injustice if he accepts the position?
A) Haram
B) Makruh
C) Wajib
D) Sunnah

Key Takeaway
For a person who fears incapacity or injustice, accepting judgeship is disliked (makruh tanzihi), but if he intends justice, it is a concession (rukhsa); however, if no one else is qualified, it becomes obligatory.

Transfer of Judicial Records: Procedure and Definitions
Defines the procedure and key terms for judicial record transfer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi procedure for transferring judicial records between judges and identify the key documents (diwan, sijill, sakk, mahdar).

Core Idea
Judicial continuity requires a formal transfer of records with witnesses and sealing.

This lesson examines the meticulous procedure outlined by Hanafi jurists for a newly appointed judge to obtain the official records from his predecessor. The passage defines key document types and explains the steps to ensure integrity and prevent tampering. Understanding this procedure reveals the depth of Hanafi judicial administration.

Guided Path
1
Identify the governing hukm
The passage begins with 'yas'alu' (he should ask), which indicates a command. In Hanafi usul, commands from a competent authority (here the judge's duty) imply obligation (wajib). Determine the hukm level of requesting the diwan.
The hukm is wajib, as failing to do so could lead to loss of evidence.
2
Define the key terms
Parse the definitions provided: al-diwan (the pouch/bag of records), al-sijill (court record/judgment), al-sakk (deed/transaction document), al-mahdar (minutes of proceedings). Note their functions and how they differ.
Each term has a specific legal role; segregation is important for clarity.
3
Outline the transfer procedure
List the steps: the new judge sends at least one upright witness (preferably two) to the previous judge; witnesses ask for the documents item by item; separate sijillat and sukkuk into different pouches; seal the pouches to prevent alteration.
The number of witnesses (adl) is critical – one is sufficient but two is more precautionary.
4
Distinguish between document types
Why separate sijillat (judgments/orders) from sukkuk (deeds)? Each serves a different purpose: sijillat record court rulings, while sukkuk record private transactions. The separation facilitates verification and reduces confusion.
The passage also defines mahdar as distinct from sijill and sakk – it records the adversarial process before judgment.
5
Note the source of documents
The passage addresses whether documents belong to bayt al-mal (public treasury) or to the parties/judge. If from bayt al-mal, no issue; if from others, the same procedure applies according to the correct view (al-sahih).
This ensures that regardless of ownership, the records are properly transferred.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن قلد سأل ديوان قاض قبله. الديوان الخرائط، جمع خريطة، وهو الكيس الذي فيه السجلات، جمع السجل، بكسر السين والجيم، وتشديد اللام، وهو الصك، ومنه إسجال القاضي وتسجيله المحاضر وغيرها من الصكوك التي فيها الإقرارات، أو قيم الأشياء، أو نصب الأوصياء في أموال اليتامى ونحو ذلك؛ لأنَّ الديوان وضع ليكون حجَّة عند الحاجة، فيجعل في يد من له ولاية القضاء. وهذا لأن القاضي يكتب نسختين، أحدهما في يده لاحتمال الحاجة إليها، والأخرى في يد الخصم، ولا يؤمن عليه التغير بزيادة أو نقصان، فإن كانت الأوراق من بيت المال فلا إشكال في وضعها في يد القاضي الجديد، وإذا كانت من مال الخصوم أو من مال القاضي في الصحيح. ويبعث عدلين من أمنائه، أو عدلاً واحداً، والاثنان أحوط؛ ليقبضان ديوان المعزول بحضرته أو بحضرة أمينه، ويسألان المعزول شيئاً فشيئاً، فما كان فيها من نسخ السجلات يجمعانه في خريطة، وما كان من الصكوك يجمعانه في خريطة، فإذا قبضا ذلك ختما عليه تحرزاً عن التغير. كذا في «المنح». والديوان من دونت الكلمة: أي ضبطها، أصله دووان، فهربوا من التضعيف إلى إبدال الواو ياء استثقالاً، قال في «القاموس»: الديوان ويفتح، مجتمع الصحف، والكتاب يكتب فيه أهل الجيش وأهل العطيّة، وأوّل من وضعه عمر، جمعه دواوين، ودياوين. انتهى. والصك الكتاب الذي يكتب في المعاملات والأقارير، وجمعه صكوك وأصك، مثل فلس وفلوس وأفلس. كذا في «المصباح المنير». والسجل: كتاب القاضي، والجمع سجلات. كذا في «المصباح». وقال في «الدرر»: إنّ المحضر ما كُتِبَ فيه ما جرى بين الخصمين من إقرار أو إنكار، والحكم ببينة أو نكول على وجه يرفع الاشتباه، وكذا السجل والصك ما كُتِبَ فيه البيع والرهن والإقرار وغيرها، والحجة والوثيقة يتناولان الثلاثة.
And whoever is appointed as judge, he should ask for the diwan (records) of the judge before him. Al-Diwan is the bags (khara'it) – plural of kharita – which is a pouch containing the sijillat (official records), plural of sijill – with kasra on sin and jim and shadd on lam – which is the sakk (deed). From this, the judge's isjal (recording) and his tasjil (recording) of mahadir (minutes) and other sakk (deeds) that contain acknowledgements, or values of things, or appointments of guardians over orphans' wealth, etc. Because the diwan was established to be a proof when needed; it is placed in the hands of the one who has authority of judgeship. And this is because the judge writes two copies: one in his hand for potential need, and the other in the hand of the opponent. And it is not safe from alteration by increase or decrease. If the papers are from the public treasury (bayt al-mal), then there is no issue in placing them in the hands of the new judge. And if they are from the property of the opponents or from the property of the judge, then the correct view (is that they are also transferred). And he sends two upright witnesses (adlan) from his trustworthy men, or one single witness, but two are more precautionary, so that they may take possession of the diwan of the removed judge in his presence or in the presence of his agent. And they ask the removed judge item by item. What is in them of copies of sijillat, they collect in one pouch, and what is of sakk, they collect in another pouch. When they have taken possession, they seal them to protect against alteration. Thus it is in al-Munah. And al-diwan is derived from 'dawwana al-kalima' meaning he recorded it. Its original is 'dawwan', then they fled from doubling by substituting the waw with ya for heaviness. Al-Qamus said: al-diwan (and it is pronounced with fath) is the gathering place of papers, and the book in which the army and the people of stipends are recorded. The first to institute it was 'Umar. Its plural is dawawin and diyawin. End. And al-sakk is the book that is written in transactions and acknowledgements, its plural is sukuk and asukk, like fals (coin) and fulus and aflus. Thus in al-Misbah al-Munir. And al-sijill is the book of the judge, its plural is sijillat. Thus in al-Misbah. And in al-Durar: Indeed, al-mahdar is what is written of what occurred between the two adversaries of acknowledgement or denial, and the ruling based on evidence or refusal (nukul) in a manner that removes ambiguity. Similarly, al-sijill and al-sakk are what is written of sale, mortgage, acknowledgement, etc. And al-hujja and al-wathiqua encompass all three.
Source pages p. 422

Commentary
This passage is a definition and procedural ruling (hukm of procedure) from the chapter of qada. It explains the matn phrase 'And whoever is appointed judge, he should ask for the diwan of the judge before him.' The commentator defines diwan as the pouches (khara'it) that contain legal documents: sijillat (official court records) and sukkuk (deeds). The procedure is described: the new judge must send at least one upright witness (adl) – preferably two – to take possession of the diwan from the dismissed judge in his presence or the presence of his agent. The witnesses ask for the documents item by item, separate the sijillat in one pouch and the sakk in another, then seal them to prevent tampering. This is to preserve the integrity of the records. If the papers were from bayt al-mal (public treasury), they are simply handed over to the new judge. If they were from the parties or the previous judge's own property, the same procedure applies according to the soundest view. The passage also provides lexical definitions: sakk is a deed for transactions/acknowledgements, sijill is the judge's record, mahdar is the minute of court proceedings. The term diwan originally referred to the room where documents are kept, later the collection of records itself. The first caliph to establish the diwan was 'Umar ibn al-Khattab. This ruling ensures continuity and trust in the judicial system by properly transferring all case files.

Key Terms
الديوان (al-diwan): The collection of legal documents and records (the bag containing sijillat and sakk) that are transferred between judges.
السجل (al-sijill (pl. sijillat)): An official court record containing judgments, acknowledgements, or other case details.
الصك (al-sakk (pl. sukuk/asukk)): A deed or document recording transactions, mortgages, acknowledgements, etc.
المحضر (al-mahdar (pl. mahadir)): The written minutes of court proceedings capturing statements and decisions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) One witness is sufficient
B) Two witnesses are more precautionary
C) No witnesses needed if trusted
D) Three witnesses are required

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi procedure for transferring judicial records requires formal request, witness involvement, itemization, separation, and sealing to preserve evidence and ensure justice.

New Judge's Duty: Compelling Prisoners with Established Rights
Ruling on compelling confessing prisoners

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling that a new judge compels a prisoner who has confessed to a right, but not a denier, and distinguish between financial rights and hudud punishments.

Core Idea
A new judge must independently verify confessions: hudud require fresh proceedings, financial rights are enforceable from prior records.

When a new judge takes office, he is obligated to examine all prisoners in the jail. Some have confessed; others deny. The Hanafi school provides a nuanced ruling: the new judge compels a confessor, but the meaning of 'compel' is debated among scholars. The key distinction lies between hudud punishments and the rights of servants. In this lesson, you will learn the ruling and its practical application.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The new judge must examine prisoners. For a prisoner who has confessed to a right (financial or otherwise), the judge is obligated to compel him (ulzima). For a denier, he does not compel unless evidence (bayyinah) is established, even if the dismissed judge informs him.
2
Understand the meaning of 'ulzima'
There are two interpretations: (1) Continue his imprisonment until the matter is clarified (Mulla Miskin). (2) Enforce the right immediately and then return him to prison (al-Ayni, Ibn al-Humam). The preferred view is the first, because the second does not apply to hudud punishments like zina.
See al-Bahr's preference for Miskin's view.
3
Apply the distinction: hudud vs. financial rights
If a prisoner confessed to a pure criminal penalty (e.g., zina) before the old judge, the new judge cannot execute the hadd based on that confession alone—the old judge's jurisdiction is void. He must start anew. For financial rights (e.g., debt), the confession is a binding proof (hujja mulzima) and the new judge can enforce it immediately.
Allama al-Shami notes that 'right' most commonly refers to the right of a servant.
4
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position is that the new judge continues imprisonment of a confessor until the matter is clarified, except for financial rights which he enforces immediately. For a denier, he does not continue imprisonment unless evidence is presented.
This position is from al-Bahr and endorsed by later Hanafi authorities.

They are the pouches containing the deeds and records. This is because the judge writes two copies: one in his hand due to the possibility of needing it, and the other in the hand of the litigant, and it is not safe from alteration by addition or deletion. If the papers are from the public treasury, there is no problem in placing them in the hand of the new judge; and if they are from the wealth of the litigants or from the wealth of the judge, according to the correct view. He sends two trustworthy persons from his trustees, or one person, and two is more precautionary, to take the register of the dismissed judge in his presence or in the presence of his trustee, and they ask the dismissed judge item by item. Whatever is from the copies of the records, they collect in one pouch, and whatever is from the deeds, they collect in another pouch. When they take them, they seal them to guard against alteration. Thus in al-Munah. And al-diwan is from dūna (to record): i.e., to record it. Its origin is dūwān, and they avoided doubling by substituting wāw with yā’ due to heaviness. He said in al-Qamus: al-diwan, and it is opened, the collection of papers, and the book in which the army and the recipients of stipends are written, and the first to establish it was Umar. Its plural is dawāwīn and dayāwīn. End. His statement: al-sukūk wa al-sijillāt: al-sakk is a document written in transactions and acknowledgments, and its plural is sukūk and ask, like fals and fulūs and afulus. Thus in al-Misbah al-Munir. And al-sijill is the judge's document, and the plural is sijillāt. Thus in al-Misbah. And he said in al-Durar: Indeed, al-mahdar is what is written about what occurred between the two litigants of acknowledgment or denial, and judgment by evidence or refusal in a way that removes ambiguity. Similarly, al-sijill and al-sakk are what is written regarding sale, pledge, acknowledgment, and others, and al-hujjah and al-wathīqah encompass all three. End. And he compels a prisoner who acknowledged a right, not one who denies except with evidence, even if the dismissed judge informed him. And he said in al-Bahr: The current custom is that al-sijill is what is written about the incident and remains with the judge, and it does not have his signature; and al-hujjah is what has the judge's mark at the top and the handwriting of the two witnesses at the bottom, and it is given to the litigant. End, summarized. [Commentary] His statement: wa ulzim, etc. Means the new judge examines the situation of the prisoners who are in his prison because he was appointed as a supervisor for the Muslims. So he sends a trustworthy person to count them in the prison, and writes their names, their stories, the reason for their imprisonment, and who imprisoned them. Whoever acknowledges a right or evidence is established against him with the right, the judge compels him because both acknowledgment and evidence are binding proofs. His statement: ulzim could mean he continued his imprisonment as Mulla Miskin indicated, and could mean he compels him with the right as the statement of al-Ayni in Ramz al-Haqaiq indicates, and Ibn al-Humam in al-Fath pointed to it when he said: Whoever confesses a right, he compels him with it and returns him to prison. End. And he said in al-Bahr: The apparent is what Miskin said because the second does not apply to every acknowledgment, since if a prisoner acknowledged a pure punishment like zina and said: I acknowledged before the dismissed judge for zina, and he did not execute the hadd upon me, then the judge does not execute it upon him because what was in the court of the dismissed judge is void. But the new judge starts the matter anew; if he acknowledges four times in four sessions, he is to be hadd. End. And Allama al-Shami said in Radd al-Muhtar: In it, the apparent meaning of 'right' is the right of the servant. End. His statement: la man ankara, etc.: Means he does not compel one who denies that right for which he was imprisoned, even if the dismissed judge informed him and said: I imprisoned him for a right upon him, or said: I had ruled against him for so-and-so with such-and-such, unless evidence is established for that right, and the judge knows the justice of the witnesses, then he compels him as he compels the one who acknowledged.
Source pages p. 423

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with commentary from Sharh al-Wiqaya regarding the duties of a newly appointed judge in taking over the office. The text first discusses the transfer of records: the new judge secures the pouches containing deeds (sukuk) and records (sijillat) from the dismissed judge. Since the original judge kept one copy and the litigant another, the new judge sends one or two trustworthy persons to collect and seal the register (diwan) of the dismissed judge. The definitions of key terms are given: 'sakk' is a document for transactions, 'sijill' is the judge's record, 'mahdar' records court proceedings, and 'hujjah' and 'wathiqah' are overarching terms. The core ruling states that the new judge compels a prisoner who has confessed to a right (haqq, typically a financial right) to fulfill it, but he does not compel a prisoner who denies the claim unless clear evidence (bayyinah) is presented—even if the dismissed judge personally informs him. The commentary explores the meaning of 'ulzima' (compels): some say it means to continue his imprisonment (Miskin), others say it means to enforce the right (al-Ayni, Ibn al-Humam). The preferred view in al-Bahr is that it means to continue imprisonment, because if the confession is for a pure corporal penalty like zina, the new judge cannot execute the hadd based solely on the old judge's record; he must start afresh. However, if the confessed right is a financial right (haqq al-abd), the new judge enforces it. This distinction is practical for a Hanafi judge: he must verify the basis of each prisoner's detention and only act on established evidence or valid confession made before him or before the previous judge for non-penal matters.

Key Terms
صك (sakk): A document written in transactions and acknowledgments, e.g., sale, pledge.
سجل (sijill): The judge's record of the case, kept at the court, often without the judge's signature in current custom.
إقرار (iqrar): Acknowledgment or confession of a right, considered a binding proof.
بينة (bayyinah): Evidence, usually testimony of two male witnesses or one male and two females, that establishes a right.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the new judge's ruling regarding a prisoner who denies the right for which he was imprisoned, even if the dismissed judge informs him?
A) He must imprison him until he confesses.
B) He must release him immediately.
C) He does not compel him unless evidence (bayyinah) is established.
D) He compels him solely based on the dismissed judge's report.

Key Takeaway
A new judge compels a confessing prisoner (financial right enforceable immediately; hudud require fresh proceedings) but does not compel a denier without evidence.

Qada — Judiciary

Duty of New Judge Regarding Prisoners: Confession and Denial
Ruling on action of new judge with prisoners confessing or denying

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the new judge's duty toward prisoners and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on the meaning of 'alzamahu' (enforce).

Core Idea
Confession or evidence binds; denial requires fresh proof, even if the deposed judge reported the reason.

This passage, from a Hanafi commentary on al-Hidayah, outlines the procedure a newly appointed judge must follow for prisoners left by his predecessor. The ruling distinguishes between those who confess and those who deny, and it includes an important internal debate about what it means to 'enforce' a right against a confessor. Understanding this ruling clarifies the Hanafi commitment to procedural fairness and the independence of each judge's authority.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core obligation of the new judge
The text states the judge is appointed as a supervisor (nazir) for Muslims, so he must investigate prisoners. This is an obligation (wajib) derived from his general duty to oversee the affairs of the community.
2
Distinguish between confessors and deniers
For a prisoner who confesses to a right (haqq), the judge enforces the confession. For a prisoner who denies, the judge can only enforce if evidence (bayyinah) is established. The deposed judge's report alone is insufficient.
3
Analyze the intra-Hanafi debate on 'alzamahu'
The verb 'alzamahu' (enforce) has two interpretations: Mulla Miskin says it means continue imprisonment; al-Ayni and Ibn al-Humam say it means impose the right and return to prison. The preferred view in al-Bahr is Mulla Miskin's, because the alternative fails for confessions of pure punishments like zina.
This debate affects how the new judge handles confessions made before the deposed judge.
4
Apply the preferred view to hadd crimes
If a prisoner confessed to zina before the deposed judge, that confession is invalid. The new judge must start afresh and require four confessions in four sessions for hadd.
5
Understand the procedure for deniers without evidence
If no evidence is brought, the judge announces the claim publicly for a few days. If no claimant appears, the judge takes a surety (kafil) from the prisoner and releases him.
This balances the apparent right from the deposed judge's action with the need for proper proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وألزم محبوساً أقرَّ بحق لا مَن أنكر إلا ببينةٍ وإن أخبر به المعزول. قوله : وألزم الخ؛ يعني نظر القاضي الجديد في حال المحبوسين الذين هم في سجنه ؛ لأنه نُصبَ ناظراً للمسلمين، فيبعث ثقة يحصيهم في السجن، ويكتب أسماءهم وأخبارهم وسب وسبب حبسهم ، ومن حبسهم ، فمن أقرَّ بحق أو قامت عليه بالحقِّ بينة ألزمه القاضي ؛ لأن كلاً من الإقرار والبينة حجة ملزمة. وقوله : ألزم يحتمل أن يكون معناه : أدام حبسه كما أشار إليه الملا مسكين، ويحتمل أن يراد ألزمه بالحقِّ كما يفيدُهُ كلامُ العَيْني في رمز الحقائق، وإليه أشار ابن الهمام في «الفتح» حيث قال : من اعترف بحق ألزمه إياه وردّه إلى السجن. انتهى. وقال في «البحر» : الظاهرُ ما قاله مسكين ؛ لأنَّ الثاني لا يطَّردُ في كلِّ إقرار إذ المحبوس لـو أقر بسبب عقوبة خالصة كالزنا فقال : إني أقررتُ عند القاضي المعزول بالزنا، ولم يقم الحد علي، فإنَّ القاضي لا يقيمُهُ عليه ؛ لأنّ ما كان في مجلس المعزول بطل، لكنَّ المولى يستقبل الأمر، فإن أقرَّ أربع مرات في أربع مجالس حد. انتهى. وقال العلامة الشامي في رد المحتار : فيه أنَّ المتبادر من الحق حق العبد. انتهى. (٢) قوله : لا من أنكر ...الخ ؛ أي لا يلزم من أنكر ذلك الحق الذي حبس به، وإن أخبر به القاضي المعزول، وقال: حبسته بحق عليه أو قال : كنت حكمت عليه لفلان بكذا إلا إذا قامت البينة على ذلك الحق، والقاضي يعرفُ عدالة الشهود ألزمه كما ألزمه وإلا ينادي عليه، ثم يخليه. [١]قوله : ينادى...الخ ؛ لأنَّ المعزول حبسه بحق ظاهر، فلا يجعل بتخليته حتى ينكشف له حاله وينادى إليه إذا جلس للحكم أياماً، ويقول المنادي : من كان يطالب فلان ابن فلان المحبوس بحق فليحضر حتى يجمع بينه وبينه ، فإن حضر له خصم فيها ، وإلا أخذ منه كفيلاً ،وأطلقه صرَّح به في غير واحد من الكتب الفقهية. والفرق لأبي حنيفة لله بين هذه المسألة وبين قسمة التركة، حيث لا يؤخذ منه كفيل إذا أراد القسمة عنده أنَّ الورثة ظهر حقهم في المال فلا يوفر إلى التكفيل ؛ لاحتمال أن يكون له وارث غيرهم؛ لأنَّ ذلك موهوم، فلا يعارض المحقق، وفي هذه المسألة أنَّ القاضي لا يحبسه إلا بحق ظاهر، واحتمال حبسه بغـيـر حـق مـوهــوم ، فـلا يعارض الظاهر.
And he enforces against a prisoner who confessed to a right, not against one who denies except with evidence, even if the deposed judge informed him about it. His statement: 'And he enforces' etc.; meaning the new judge examines the situation of the prisoners who are in his prison because he is appointed as a supervisor for Muslims, so he sends a trustworthy person to count them in prison, write their names, their news, the reason for their imprisonment, and who imprisoned them. Whoever confessed to a right or against whom evidence of the right was established, the judge enforces it against him because both confession and evidence are binding proofs. His statement: 'enforces' may mean he continues his imprisonment as Mulla Miskin indicated, and may mean he imposes the right upon him as the statement of al-Ayni in Ramz al-Haqaiq indicates, and Ibn al-Humam in al-Fath referred to it where he said: whoever admits to a right, he imposes it upon him and returns him to prison. End quote. And in al-Bahr: the apparent is what Miskin said because the second does not apply in every confession; if a prisoner confessed due to a pure punishment like zina saying 'I confessed before the deposed judge to zina and he did not enforce the hadd on me,' the judge does not enforce it on him because whatever was in the deposed judge's session is invalid, but the new judge starts afresh; if he confesses four times in four sessions, hadd is applied. End quote. And Allama Shami in Radd al-Muhtar said: the predominant understanding of 'right' is the right of a servant. End quote. (2) His statement: 'not one who denies' etc.; meaning he does not enforce against one who denies that right for which he was imprisoned, even if the deposed judge informed him and said 'I imprisoned him due to a right upon him' or said 'I had ruled against him for so-and-so,' unless evidence of that right is established, and the judge knows the witnesses' integrity, he enforces it as he would, otherwise he makes an announcement about him and then releases him. [1] His statement: 'he announces' etc.; because the deposed judge imprisoned him due to an apparent right, so he should not be released until his situation is disclosed and he makes an announcement for days when he sits for judgment, and the announcer says: whoever has a claim against so-and-so son of so-and-so the prisoner should come so that he can bring them together, and if a claimant appears he is dealt with, otherwise he takes a surety from him and releases him; this is explicitly stated in more than one fiqh book. And the distinction made by Abu Hanifa between this issue and the division of inheritance is that there surety is not taken from him when he wants division, because the heirs' right in the property is apparent, so there is no need for surety due to the possibility of another heir; that possibility is fanciful and does not counteract the established reality. In this issue, the judge does not imprison him except with an apparent right, and the possibility of imprisonment without a right is fanciful, so it does not counteract the apparent.
Source pages p. 424

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from a Hanafi juristic work explaining the duty of a newly appointed judge regarding prisoners left by his predecessor. The ruling: The new judge must investigate the situation of all prisoners. If a prisoner confesses to a right (e.g., a debt) or evidence is produced against him, the judge enforces that right upon him. This enforcement can mean either continuing his imprisonment (according to Mulla Miskin) or actually imposing the right and returning him to prison (according to al-Ayni). The preferred view in al-Bahr is the first because the second fails in cases where the confession involves a pure punishment like zina; if a prisoner confessed to zina before the deposed judge, the new judge cannot enforce the hadd based on that confession because the deposed judge's session is invalid. The new judge must start anew, requiring four confessions in four sessions. As for a denier, the judge cannot enforce anything based merely on the deposed judge's statement. The judge must require evidence (bayyinah) and verify the witnesses. If no evidence is brought, the judge announces the matter publicly for several days, and if no claimant appears, the judge releases the prisoner after taking a surety (kafil) in case a hidden claimant emerges. The text then notes a distinction by Abu Hanifa between this case and the division of an estate (qismat al-tarikah): in estate division, the heirs' right is established in the property, so no surety is needed; the possibility of an unknown heir is fanciful and does not override the established reality. In contrast, imprisonment may be based on an apparent right, but the possibility of imprisonment without right is fanciful, so it does not override the apparent reality of the deposed judge's action. This distinction shows the Hanafi commitment to balancing established reality with fanciful possibilities.

Key Terms
محبوس (mahbus): A person imprisoned under the authority of the previous judge.
إقرار (iqrar): A confession or admission of a right or liability.
بينة (bayyinah): Legal evidence, typically testimony of two male witnesses.
كفيل (kafil): A surety or guarantor who ensures the prisoner's appearance if a claim arises later.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what must the new judge do when a prisoner denies the right for which he was imprisoned, and the deposed judge informs the new judge of the reason?
A) The new judge must immediately release the prisoner because the deposed judge's statement is invalid.
B) The new judge must enforce the right based on the deposed judge's information alone.
C) The new judge must require evidence (bayyinah) and verify witnesses; if no evidence, announce publicly and then release with a surety.
D) The new judge must keep the prisoner imprisoned indefinitely until evidence appears.

Key Takeaway
A new judge must independently verify confessions and evidence; for deniers, the deposed judge's report is not sufficient, requiring fresh evidence or public announcement with surety.

Evidence Required for Deposed Judge's Actions on Deposits and Endowment Revenues
Ruling that deposed judge's actions on deposits and waqf revenues require evidence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the evidentiary requirement for a deposed judge's actions regarding orphan deposits and waqf revenues, and explain the underlying reasoning based on Hanafi custom.

Core Idea
Deposed judge's statements on deposits and waqf revenues need evidence—his former office does not exempt him from proof.

This lesson examines a specific ruling from the chapter of qada (judicial procedure): how must a new judge handle the actions of his predecessor concerning orphan deposits and endowment revenues? The Hanafi school insists on evidence (bayyinah) because the deposed judge is no longer in authority. We will analyze the distinction between the original endowment and its revenues, and understand how later custom shifted the management of these assets.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling
What is the evidentiary rule for the deposed judge's actions on deposits and waqf revenues? The new judge only acts based on evidence, not mere statements.
The ruling applies specifically to revenues (ghallah), not the original endowment (asl al-waqf).
2
Locate the Distinction
Why is the ruling specific to revenues? The passage states that the possessor's statement about the original endowment is not accepted if the heir denies and there is no evidence. Revenues are more fluid and require confirmation.
The original endowment is a fixed asset; revenues are periodic and can be misappropriated.
3
Examine the Hypothetical
Consider: deposed judge claims a property is a waqf and he handed it to a person; the current possessor affirms, but the heir denies. According to the passage, neither the deposed judge's statement nor the possessor's affirmation is accepted without evidence.
This shows that the deposed judge's word carries no special weight.
4
Understand the Evidence
Who brings the evidence? The executor (wasi) for orphan deposits, or the waqf supervisor (nazir) for endowment revenues. They must prove that the property or revenue belongs to the orphan or waqf.
Evidence typically includes witnesses or documents.
5
Note the Custom Shift
al-Nahr notes that the ruling was based on the earlier custom where all such assets were under the judge's amin. In later times, waqf properties are under supervisors and orphan deposits under guardians. This affects how evidence is produced.
The ruling's application adapts to changing administrative practices.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعمل في الودائع وغلة الوقوف بالبينة. [٢] قوله : وغلة الوقوف ؛ قيَّد بغلة الوقوف ؛ لأنه لا يعمل بإقرار ذي اليد في أصل الوقف إذا جحده الوارث ولا بيّنة، ولو قال المعزول : إن هذا وقف فلان سلمته إلى هذا ، وأقرّ ذو اليد وكذَّبه الوارث، لم يقبل قول القاضي المعزول، وذي اليد إن لم يقم عليه البينة. كذا في «البحر» [۳] قوله : بالبينة ؛ التي يقيمها الوصي مثلاً على من هي تحت يده أنها ليتيم فلان، أو ناظر الواقف، إن هذه الغلة لوقف فلان ، قال في «النهر»: وكأنه مبني على عرفهم من أن الكل تحت يد أمين القاضي، وفي زماننا أموال الأوقاف تحت يد نظارها ، وودائعُ اليتامى تحت يد الأوصياء، ولو فرض أنَّ المعزول وضع ذلك تحت أمين، عمل
And he acts regarding deposits and revenues of endowments with evidence. [2] His statement: 'and revenues of endowments'; he specified revenues of endowments because the statement of the possessor about the original endowment is not acted upon if the heir denies it and there is no evidence; and if the deposed judge said 'this is a waqf of so-and-so, I handed it over to this person,' and the possessor affirmed and the heir denied, the statement of the deposed judge and the possessor is not accepted if no evidence is established. Thus in al-Bahr. [3] His statement: 'with evidence' that which the executor, for example, brings to prove that what is in his hand belongs to an orphan of so-and-so, or the supervisor of the waqf brings to prove that this revenue belongs to the waqf of so-and-so. He said in al-Nahr: it seems to be based on their custom that everything is under the hand of the judge's trustee; but in our time, waqf properties are under the hands of their supervisors, and deposits of orphans are under the hands of guardians. If it is assumed that the deposed judge placed that under a trustee, then he acts...
Source pages p. 425

Commentary
This passage addresses the validity of the deposed judge's actions concerning deposits (wada'i') and revenues (ghallah) of endowments (awqaf). The ruling: The new judge only acts on the deposed judge's actions regarding these matters if evidence (bayyinah) is presented. The commentator explains that the ruling is specifically for revenues of endowments, not the original endowment itself, because the possessor's statement about the original endowment is not accepted if the heir denies it and there is no evidence. For example, if the deposed judge claims that a property is a waqf and that he handed it to someone, and the current possessor affirms but the heir denies, neither the deposed judge's statement nor the possessor's affirmation is accepted without evidence. The 'evidence' referred to is that brought by the executor (wasi) or the waqf supervisor (nazir) to prove that the property is for an orphan or that the revenue belongs to a specific waqf. The commentator from al-Nahr notes that this ruling was based on the custom that all such properties were under the authority of the judge's trustee (amin), but in later times, waqf properties are under the supervisors and orphan deposits under guardians. The statement ends with a conditional: if the deposed judge placed the property under a trustee, then the new judge acts on that, but the text is cut off. This shows the Hanafi concern for protecting rights through established evidence, especially when the original judge's actions are no longer authoritative.

Key Terms
ودائع (wada'i'): Deposits, typically of orphans' property held by a guardian or judge's trustee.
غلة الوقوف (ghallah al-wuquf): Revenues generated from endowment properties, such as rent or produce.
أمين القاضي (amin al-qadi): The judge's appointed trustee who manages deposits and waqf revenues.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Because the deposed judge's testimony about revenues is inherently unreliable.
B) Because the original endowment is already established by a previous ruling, unlike revenues which may change.
C) Because the passage specifies that revenues are restricted to evidence, while the original endowment is accepted on the judge's statement.
D) Because the possessor's confession alone is sufficient for revenues.

Key Takeaway
A deposed judge's actions regarding deposits and waqf revenues require independent evidence because his status no longer carries presumptive authority.

The Dismissed Qadi's Testimony: Deposits and Waqf Revenues
Ruling on accepting a dismissed qadi's statement about deposits and waqf revenues

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the testimony of a dismissed qadi and apply the four scenarios to determine when his statement is accepted.

Core Idea
A dismissed qadi is like any layperson—his word alone is not accepted as proof.

This passage from a Hanafi fiqh text addresses a specific procedural issue: what happens to deposits (wadāʾiʿ) and endowment revenues (ghallat al-wuqūf) after a qadi is dismissed? The ruling is that his statement about these assets is not accepted unless he produces evidence or the possessor acknowledges receipt. To clarify, the text then presents four scenarios that illustrate the application of this principle.

Guided Path
1
Identify the basic ruling
The dismissed qadi's statement regarding deposits and waqf revenues is not accepted on its own. He must produce evidence (bayyinah) or obtain an acknowledgment (iqrār) from the possessor (dhū al-yad).
The ruling rests on the analogy that after dismissal, the qadi is an ordinary person whose single testimony is insufficient.
2
Understand the proof (dalīl)
The text provides the reasoning: 'because after dismissal he became one of the subjects, and the testimony of one person is not accepted.' This is a qiyās (analogy) to a layperson.
The proof is analogical (ʿaqlī), not based on a specific text.
3
Analyze the four scenarios
The passage lists four possibilities for the possessor's response to the dismissed qadi's claim. Each scenario changes the legal outcome.
Focus on the effect of the possessor's acknowledgment: it can either validate the qadi's statement or block further claims.
4
Apply the principle to each scenario
Work through each scenario step by step, noting when the qadi's statement is accepted and when it is not. Pay attention to the key turning point: acknowledgment of receipt from the qadi.
Scenario 3 is critical: once the possessor confirms receipt, it is as if the property is still in the qadi's hand.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعمل في الودائع وغلة الوقوف بالبينة لأنَّه بالعزل التحق بواحدٍ من الرَّعايا وشهادة الواحد لا تقبل. أو بإقرار ذي اليد لا بقول المعزول إلا إذا أقر ذو اليد بالتسليم منه. والمَسألة على أربعة أوجه: ١. إما أن يقر بأنه سلمه إليه بعدما أقرَّ به لغيره. ٢. أو ينكر التسليم، فحكمهما ما ذكرنا. ٣. أو يقر بأن المعزول سلّمه إليه، ثمَّ يقرّ به لغيره فلا يقبلُ إقراره للثاني؛ لأنه لما أقرَّ بأنَّ القاضي سلمه إليه صار كأنه في يد القاضي. ٤. والرابع: أن يقرّ بأن القاضي سلّمه إليه يقول: لا أدري من هو، فحكمه ظاهر.
And he [the dismissed qadi] acts regarding deposits and endowment revenues on the basis of evidence, because after dismissal he became one of the subjects, and the testimony of one person is not accepted. Or on the basis of the acknowledgment of the possessor, not on the statement of the dismissed qadi, unless the possessor acknowledges receipt from him. And the case is of four types: 1. Either he acknowledges that he [the qadi] delivered it to him after he had acknowledged it to someone else. 2. Or he denies the delivery; their ruling is as we mentioned. 3. Or he acknowledges that the dismissed qadi delivered it to him, then acknowledges it to someone else; his acknowledgment for the second is not accepted, because when he acknowledged that the qadi delivered it to him, he became as if it were in the qadi's hand. 4. And the fourth: he acknowledges that the qadi delivered it to him, saying: I do not know who it is; its ruling is apparent.
Source pages p. 425

Commentary
This passage addresses the testimonial capacity of a qadi after his dismissal regarding deposits (wadā'iʿ) and the revenues of endowments (ghallat al-wuqūf). The ruling is that a dismissed qadi is treated like any ordinary citizen (لأنه بالعزل التحق بواحد من الرعايا); thus, his sole statement does not constitute sufficient proof (شهادة الواحد لا تقبل). Therefore, his claim regarding such assets is not accepted unless he produces clear evidence (bīna) or the person in possession of the property (dhū al-yad) acknowledges that the qadi had delivered it to him. This aligns with general Hanafi principles of testimony and agency. The passage then elaborates four scenarios to clarify the application: (1) if the possessor acknowledges receipt from the qadi after previously acknowledging the property belongs to another, (2) if he denies receipt, (3) if he acknowledges receipt from the qadi but then later acknowledges the property to a third party, and (4) if he acknowledges receipt but claims ignorance of the rightful owner. The text emphasizes that once the possessor confirms the qadi's delivery, the qadi's statement becomes effective as if the property were still in his hand.

Key Terms
القاضي المعزول (al-qāḍī al-maʿzūl): A judge who has been dismissed from his post; after dismissal he is treated like an ordinary person.
ذي اليد (dhū al-yad): The person who physically possesses the deposit or endowment revenue in question.
الودائع (al-wadāʾiʿ): Deposits or items entrusted for safekeeping, often held by the qadi as administrator.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because the third party is not present during the acknowledgment.
B) Because after acknowledging receipt from the dismissed qadi, the property is considered to be in the qadi's hand, so the possessor has no authority to acknowledge it to another.
C) Because the dismissed qadi has already acknowledged the property to someone else.
D) Because the possessor's first acknowledgment is invalid.

Key Takeaway
A dismissed qadi is like a layperson; his statement about deposits and waqf revenues is not accepted unless supported by evidence or the possessor's acknowledgment.

Acceptance of the Dismissed Judge's Statement via Possessor's Acknowledgment
Ruling on dismissing judge's claim via possessor's acknowledgment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition under which a dismissed judge's claim about a deposit is accepted and apply the ruling to the four enumerated cases.

Core Idea
A dismissed judge's word alone is not accepted; only the possessor's acknowledgment validates the claim.

This passage from a Hanafi commentary expands on a base text concerning a dismissed judge (al-ma'zul) who claims he handed over a deposit to a person, but that person denies. The ruling hinges on the evidentiary weight of the judge's statement and the role of the current possessor's acknowledgment. Understanding this helps clarify procedural rules in Islamic judiciary (qada).

Guided Path
1
Identify the base ruling
The base text states: 'the dismissed judge's statement is not accepted' (لا يقبل قول المعزول). Understand that this is the default position due to his own interest and his status as a layman after dismissal.
2
Find the exception
The exception is introduced by 'except if the possessor acknowledges receipt from him'. Identify this as the key condition that makes the judge's claim valid.
3
Understand the rationale
The commentary explains that the possessor's acknowledgment establishes that the judge previously had possession, so the judge's acknowledgment becomes valid as if the item were still in his hand.
4
Analyze the four cases
The passage lists four scenarios (وجوه) based on timing of acknowledgments and denials. Work through each: (1) possessor acknowledges for another first, then acknowledges judge's delivery; (2) possessor denies delivery; (3) possessor acknowledges judge's delivery, then acknowledges for another; (4) possessor acknowledges judge's delivery but does not know the owner.
Focus on how the ruling changes in each case.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

[٢] قوله : لا يقبل ؛ كما سبق من أنه بالعزل التحق بالرعايا، وشهادة الواحد لا تقبل لا سيما إذا كانت على فعل نفسه. [٣] قوله : إلا إذا أقر ...الخ ؛ يعني إلا أن يعترف الذي في يده أنّ القاضي سلمها إليه فيُقْبَلُ قولُه ؛ إذ بإقراره ثبت أنَّ اليد كانت للمعزول سابقاً فصح إقرار المعزول، كأنه في يده حالاً ؛ لأنَّ مَن كان بيده حقيقةً يقبل إقراره. فكذا إذا كان في يد مودعه ؛ لأنَّ يده كيد المودع، إلا إذا بدأ صاحب اليد بالإقرار لغيره، ثم أقر بتسليم القاضي إليه، والقاضي يقرُّ به لغيره ، فيسلم إلى المقر له الأول، ويضمن المقر قيمته للقاضي بالإقرار. كما صرحوا به. والمسألة على أربعة أوجه : .١ إما أن يقر بأنه سلمه إليه بعدما أقرَّ به لغيره. ٢. أو ينكر التسليم، فحكمهما ما ذكرنا. ٣. أو يقر بأن المعزول سلّمه إليه ، ثمَّ يقرّ به لغيره فلا يقبلُ إقراره للثاني ؛ لأنه لما أقرَّ بأنَّ القاضي سلمه إليه صار كأنه في يد القاضي. ٤. والرابع : أن يقرّ بأن القاضي سلّمه إليه يقول : لا أدري من هو، فحكمه ظاهر. كذا في «الرمز»، و«التبيين»)، وغيرهما.
Or by the acknowledgment of the possessor, not by the statement of the dismissed [judge]: i.e., the statement of the dismissed judge is not accepted if he says, 'This is so-and-so's deposit, I handed it to this man,' and he denies, except if the possessor acknowledges receipt from him—i.e., from the dismissed judge. The judge with what was mentioned. End. 11 His statement: 'Or by the acknowledgment of the possessor' because the acknowledgment of an outsider is not accepted, as they have clarified. [2] His statement: 'It is not accepted' as previously stated that upon dismissal he becomes like the subjects, and the testimony of a single person is not accepted, especially if it is about his own action. [3] His statement: 'Except if he acknowledges...' means except if the one in whose hand it is admits that the judge handed it over to him, then his statement is accepted; because by his acknowledgment it is established that the hand was previously that of the dismissed judge, so the dismissed judge's acknowledgment becomes valid, as if it were in his hand currently; because the statement of one who has actual possession is accepted. Similarly, if it is in the hand of his depositary, because his hand is like the hand of the depositor, except if the possessor first acknowledges for another, then acknowledges the judge's delivery to him, and the judge acknowledges for another, then it is handed over to the first acknowledged recipient, and the acknowledger guarantees its value to the judge for the acknowledgment, as they have clarified. The issue has four aspects: 1. He acknowledges that he handed it over after having acknowledged it for another. 2. He denies the delivery, so their ruling is as we mentioned. 3. He acknowledges that the dismissed judge handed it to him, then he acknowledges it for another, so his acknowledgment for the second is not accepted; because when he acknowledged that the judge handed it to him, it becomes as if it is in the judge's hand. 4. The fourth: He acknowledges that the judge handed it to him but says, 'I do not know who it is,' so its ruling is apparent. Thus in al-Ramz and al-Tabyin and others.
Source pages p. 426

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from the commentary expanding on a base text (matn). It addresses a scenario where a dismissed judge (al-ma'zul) claims he handed a deposit (wadi'ah) to a certain person, but that person denies receiving it. The ruling is that the dismissed judge's word is not accepted due to his interest (he would be liable if he did not deliver), and his testimony is like that of a layman—one person's claim is insufficient. However, if the possessor (dhu al-yad) acknowledges that the judge did hand it over to him, then the judge's claim is accepted retroactively, because the possessor's admission proves the judge's prior hand, making his acknowledgment valid as if it were in his hand now. If the possessor later acknowledges it for another, the first acknowledgment takes precedence. The passage then outlines four cases: (1) If the possessor first acknowledges the deposit to a third party, then acknowledges the judge's delivery to himself, the item is given to the first acknowledged party, and the possessor must guarantee its value to the judge. (2) If he denies delivery, the judge's word is not accepted unless he acknowledges. (3) If he acknowledges delivery from the judge, then acknowledges it for another, the second acknowledgment is not accepted because the item is considered in the judge's hand. (4) If he acknowledges the judge's delivery but does not know the owner, the ruling is apparent (the judge should take it).

Key Terms
إقرار (iqrar): An acknowledgment or admission by a person that something is owned or owed to another.
المعزول (al-ma'zul): The dismissed judge; a judge who has been removed from office.
ذو اليد (dhu al-yad): The possessor; the person who currently holds the item.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Always, because he was once a judge.
B) Never, even if the possessor admits.
C) Only if the possessor acknowledges that the judge handed it over.
D) Only if the judge provides two witnesses.

Key Takeaway
The dismissed judge's claim is accepted only when the possessor confirms the delivery, as this establishes prior possession and validates the judge's acknowledgment.

The Judge Sitting for Judgment in the Mosque
Ruling on the recommended practice of the judge sitting in the mosque for hearings, with etiquettes and conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the recommended practice for a judge sitting in the mosque, list the etiquettes and conditions for valid judgment, and evaluate the temporal change in the ruling's applicability.

Core Idea
Sitting for judgment in the mosque is a sunnah, but later scholars advise caution if mosque respect declines.

This passage from the commentary expands on the matn's instruction that a judge should sit visibly for judgment in the mosque. It explains the reasoning, provides detailed etiquettes, and notes a later view that the recommendation may be conditioned on the respect shown to mosques in each era.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The base ruling is that the judge sits for judgment openly in the mosque. Determine the hukm level (sunnah, not wajib).
The passage states 'ويجلس' as an instruction, but the comment indicates it is recommended, not obligatory.
2
List the etiquettes (adab) for the judge
Extract the recommended actions: pray tahiyyah, face qiblah, wear best clothes, etc.
These are sunan, not conditions of validity.
3
Identify conditions for valid judgment
Note the prohibitions: judge not to judge when distracted, in extreme states, or while fasting voluntarily.
These are conditions for the permissibility of judgment.
4
Understand the temporal change (al-Hamawi's remark)
Al-Hamawi states that the recommendation was correct in earlier times, but in his time, due to lack of respect, it may not apply.
This shows that rulings can be time-specific based on 'urf.
5
Analyze the preference for the congregational mosque
The matn says 'الجامع أولى' (congregational mosque is preferable). The commentary explains it is more visible and best if centrally located.
Location matters: if at the edge of town, another mosque may be chosen.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويجلس للحكم ظاهراً في المسجد (ويجلس للحكم ظاهراً في المسجد والجامع أولى) ٤٢٧ [1] قوله : ويجلس ؛ أي القاضي، وكذا السلطان يجلس للحكم ظاهراً بهيئة يعلمُ الناس أنه جلس لفصل الخصومات [لا] لعبادة أُخرى في المسجد ؛ لئلا يشتبه حاله على المسافرين والمقيمين الذين ليس لهم اختلاط بالقضاة، ولأنه قضى فيه، وكذا الخلفاء الراشدون بعده ، ولأنه أبعد من التهمة للقاضي، ولأنه عبادة، والمساجد وضعت لها. وقال الحموي : كذا قالوا ، وهو صحيح بالنظر إلى زمانهم، وبالنظر إلى زماننا فلا، فإنّ أهله لا يحترمون المساجد، ولا يقدرون قدرها ، وربَّما يجلسون فيها بالجنابة، ويفعلون فيها ما لا يليق. انتهى. وإن دخل المسجد لفصل الخصومات يستحب أن يصلّي للتحية ركعتين، والأربعُ أفضل ، ثم يدعو لله تعالى أن يوفق الحق ، ويستقبل القبلة بوجهه. كذا في «جامع الرموز»، ويستدبر القبلة كخطيب ومدرس. كذا في «الدر المختار) نقلاً عن «الخانية»(۲)، ولا يمنع المشرك من دخوله للقضاء ؛ لأنَّ نجاسته نجاسة اعتقاد على معنى التشبيه. وأما الحائض فتخير بحالها ؛ ليخرج إليها القاضي، أو يرسل نائبه، كما لو كانت الدعوى في دابة، ولا يقضي حال شغل قليه بفرح ، أو غضب، أو هم أو حاجة إلى الجماع، أو برد، أو حر شديد، أو مدافعة الأخبثين. ولا ينبغي أن يتطوع بالصوم في اليوم الذي يريد الجلوس فيه للقضاء، ويخرج في أحسن ثيابه، وأعدل أحواله ، ذكره العلامة الطحطاوي في «حاشيته عـلـى الــدر المختار (۳). [٢] قوله : والجامع أولى؛ لأنه غير خفي على الغرباء وغيرهم، وذكر في «الخزانة»: إنّ الجامع أفضل إذا كان في وسط البلد، فإن كان في طرف البلد فيختار
And he sits for judgment openly in the mosque (And he sits for judgment openly in the mosque, and the congregational mosque is preferable) 427 [1] His statement: 'And he sits' i.e., the judge, and likewise the ruler sits for judgment openly in a manner that people know that he sat for adjudicating disputes, [not] for another worship in the mosque; lest his situation be ambiguous to travelers and residents who are not familiar with judges, and because he (the Prophet) conducted judgment in it, and likewise the Rightly Guided Caliphs after him, and because it is further from suspicion for the judge, and because it is worship, and mosques are established for it. Al-Hamawi said: Thus they said, and it is correct with regard to their time, but with regard to our time, no, because its people do not respect the mosques, nor do they appreciate their worth, and perhaps they sit in them in a state of major impurity (janabah) and do unbecoming things. End. And if he enters the mosque for adjudicating disputes, it is recommended that he pray two rak'ahs for the greeting (tahiyyah), and four are better, then he supplicates to Allah Almighty to grant him success for the truth, and he faces the qiblah with his face. Thus in Jami' al-Rumuz, and he turns his back to the qiblah like a preacher (khatib) or teacher. Thus in al-Durr al-Mukhtar quoting al-Khaniyyah, and he does not prevent a polytheist from entering for judgment because his impurity is an impurity of belief in the sense of anthropomorphism (tashbih). As for a menstruating woman, she is given an option regarding her situation: the judge goes out to her or sends his deputy, as if the claim is about an animal. And he does not judge while his heart is occupied with joy, anger, worry, need for intercourse, extreme cold or heat, or pressing desire to relieve the two excrements. And he should not voluntarily fast on the day he intends to sit for judgment, and he goes out in his best clothes and most balanced state. This is mentioned by 'Allamah al-Tahtawi in his Hashiyah on al-Durr al-Mukhtar. [2] His statement: 'And the congregational mosque is preferable' because it is not hidden from strangers and others, and it is mentioned in al-Khazinah: The congregational mosque is better if it is in the middle of the town; if it is at the edge of the town, then one chooses (another place).
Source pages p. 427

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from the commentary, expanding on the matn's instruction that a judge should sit for judgment openly in the mosque, and that the congregational mosque (jami') is preferable. The commentary explains that the judge should sit in a visible manner so people know he is there for adjudication, not for other worship, to avoid confusion. This is based on the practice of the Prophet and the Rightly Guided Caliphs, and it distances the judge from suspicion. However, Al-Hamawi notes that while this was suitable in earlier times, in later times people may lack respect for mosques, so the ruling may be different. The commentary then details recommended etiquettes: when entering the mosque, the judge should pray two rak'ahs (greeting), four being better, and face the qiblah when supplicating. However, like a preacher or teacher, he should turn his back to the qiblah (while sitting). A polytheist is not prevented from entering for litigation because their impurity is one of belief, not physical. A menstruating woman is given an option: the judge may go out to her or send a deputy. The judge should not adjudicate when his attention is distracted by emotions, physical needs, or extreme temperatures. He should not fast voluntarily on a judgment day, and should dress in his best clothes and be in his most balanced state. Finally, the congregational mosque is preferred if centrally located; otherwise a local mosque may be chosen.

Key Terms
المسجد الجامع (al-masjid al-jami'): The congregational mosque where Friday prayers are held; considered more public and suitable for judgment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, why is the congregational mosque (الجامع) preferable for a judge to sit for judgment?
A) Because it is larger and more comfortable.
B) Because it is not hidden from strangers and others.
C) Because it is the only place where judgment is valid.
D) Because the judge can perform Friday prayer there.

Key Takeaway
The judge's sitting in the mosque is a sunnah practice, contingent on the respect and appropriateness of the venue in its time and place.

Qada — Judiciary

8 min
Where Should a Judge Sit for Judgment? The Hanafi View on Mosques and Homes
Ruling on judge's seating with Hanafi proof against Shafi'i

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on where a judge sits for judgment and explain the Hanafi proof against the Shafi'i objection.

Core Idea
A judge sitting in the mosque for judgment is recommended, not disliked, following the Prophet's practice.

In this lesson, we examine a classic inter-madhhab dispute: is it permissible for a judge to hold court in a mosque? The Hanafi school says yes—it is recommended (mandub) and even preferable in a congregational mosque (jami'). This ruling is based on the practice of the Prophet and the Rightly Guided Caliphs. The Shafi'i school, however, considers it disliked (makruh) due to the potential presence of polytheists (mushrik) and menstruating women (ha'id). Let us unpack the Hanafi argument and its rebuttal.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling (mandub) and the recommended location (mosque, preferably jami'). Understand that the judge must sit publicly so that people know he is there for judgment.
The ruling is not wajib; it is based on the Sunnah of the Prophet and caliphs.
2
Identify the opposing school and its objection
The Shafi'i school holds that sitting in the mosque for judgment is makruh. Their main proof: the presence of polytheists (who are impure) and menstruating women (who cannot enter the mosque) may be problematic.
Identify the specific elements that the Shafi'is find objectionable.
3
Examine the Hanafi proof (dalil)
The Hanafis rely on the Prophet's action (fi'l) of settling disputes in his place of seclusion (mu'takaf) and the practice of the caliphs. They also argue that judgment is an act of worship (ibadah), and mosques are established for worship.
This is a dalil naqli (transmitted evidence) with zanni thubut and zanni dalala.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response to the Shafi'i objections
The Hanafis respond: the polytheist's impurity is spiritual (najasat al-i'tiqad), not physical, so it does not contaminate the mosque. The menstruating woman informs the judge, who then goes out to her or sends a deputy. This accommodates her situation without preventing the use of the mosque.
The Hanafi response shows a practical solution that removes the Shafi'i concern.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: In Hanafi practice, judges commonly hold court in mosques; in Shafi'i practice, they avoid mosques for this purpose. The Hanafi position is mufta bihi due to the stronger evidence from the Prophet's example.
This is a clear illustration of how usul differences affect daily legal practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب القضاء الشهادة
ويجلس للحكم ظاهراً في المسجد
(ويجلس للحكم ظاهرا في المسجد والجامع أولى")
[1] قوله : ويجلس ؛ أي القاضي، وكذا السلطان يجلس للحكم ظاهراً بهيئة يعلمُ الناس أنه جلس لفصل الخصومات [لا] لعبادة أُخرى في المسجد ؛ لئلا يشتبه حاله على المسافرين والمقيمين الذين ليس لهم اختلاط بالقضاة، ولأنه قضى فيه، وكذا الخلفاء الراشدون بعده ، ولأنه أبعد من التهمة للقاضي، ولأنه عبادة، والمساجد وضعت لها. وقال الحموي : كذا قالوا ، وهو صحيح بالنظر إلى زمانهم، وبالنظر إلى زماننا فلا، فإنّ أهله لا يحترمون المساجد، ولا يقدرون قدرها ، وربَّما يجلسون فيها بالجنابة، ويفعلون فيها ما لا يليق. انتهى.
وإن دخل المسجد لفصل الخصومات يستحب أن يصلّي للتحية ركعتين، والأربعُ أفضل ، ثم يدعو لله تعالى أن يوفق الحق ، ويستقبل القبلة بوجهه. كذا في «جامع الرموز»، ويستدبر القبلة كخطيب ومدرس. كذا في «الدر المختار» نقلاً عن «الخانية»(۲)، ولا يمنع المشرك من دخوله للقضاء ؛ لأنَّ نجاسته نجاسة اعتقاد على معنى التشبيه.
وأما الحائض فتخير بحالها ؛ ليخرج إليها القاضي، أو يرسل نائبه، كما لو كانت الدعوى في دابة، ولا يقضي حال شغل قلبه بفرح ، أو غضب، أو هم أو حاجة إلى الجماع، أو برد، أو حر شديد، أو مدافعة الأخبثين.
ولا ينبغي أن يتطوع بالصوم في اليوم الذي يريد الجلوس فيه للقضاء، ويخرج في أحسن ثيابه، وأعدل أحواله ، ذكره العلامة الطحطاوي في «حاشيته على الدر المختار (۳).
[٢] قوله : والجامع أولى؛ لأنه غير خفي على الغرباء وغيرهم، وذكر في «الخزانة»: إنّ الجامع أفضل إذا كان في وسط البلد، فإن كان في طرف البلد فيختار مسجداً في السوق ليكون أشهر. انتهى. فالحاصل أنه يجلس للقضاء في أشهر الأماكن ومجامع الناس، وليس فيه حاجب ولا بوّاب.
وفي «شرح أدب القاضي»: له أن يتخذ بواباً ليمنع الخصوم من الازدحام، ولا يباحُ للبوَّابِ أن يأخذ شيئاً على الإذن في الدخول. كذا في «البحر» [1] قوله : جلوساً ظاهراً ولا يتعب نفسه بطول الجلوس، ولكن يجلس طرفي النهار، وكذا المفتي والقاضي. كذا في «الفتح»". [٢]قوله : ولنا جلوس النبي ... الخ ، حاصله : أنه كان يفصل الخصومات في معتكفه ، كما ثبت في كتب الحديث، والخلفاء الراشدون كانوا يجلسون للحكم في المساجد ؛ ولأن القضاء عبادة كما قررنا سابقاً.
فيجوز إقامته في المسجد كالصلاة، والمسجد وضع للعبادة، والمشرك ليس في بدنِهِ نجاسة تلوث، وإنما ذلك في اعتقاده، والحائض تخيرُ بحالها ؛ لأنها مسلمة، فيخرج إليه القاضي أو يرسل نائبه ، كما إذا كانت الدعوى في دابة كما ذكرنا. [٣] قوله : جاز؛ لأنَّ الحكم عبادةٌ لا يتقيَّدُ بمكان، والأولى أن تكون الدار في وسط البلد كالمسجد. كذا في «النهر».
ولا يحكم وهو ماشي؛ لأنَّ الرأي لا يجتمعُ وهو مشغول بالمشي، ولا بأس بأن
The Book of Judiciary and Testimony. And he sits for judgment publicly in the mosque (and the Jami' is more preferable). [1] His statement: 'and he sits': meaning the judge, and similarly the sovereign sits for judgment publicly in a manner that people know that he sits for settling disputes, not for another act of worship in the mosque; so that his condition is not ambiguous to travelers and residents who have no dealings with judges, and because he judged in it, and similarly the Rightly Guided Caliphs after him, and because it is further from suspicion for the judge, and because it is an act of worship, and mosques are established for acts of worship. Al-Hamawi said: Thus they spoke, and it is correct according to their time, but according to our time it is not, for its people do not respect the mosques nor appreciate their due, and perhaps they sit in them in a state of major impurity (janabah) and do in them what is not fitting. End. And if he enters the mosque for resolving disputes, it is recommended that he pray two rak'ahs for greeting (tahiyyah), and four is better, then he supplicates to Allah to grant him success in justice, and faces the qiblah with his face. Thus in 'Jami' al-Rumuz', and he turns his back to the qiblah like a preacher and teacher. Thus in 'al-Durr al-Mukhtar' quoting from 'al-Khaniyyah'. And the polytheist is not forbidden from entering for judgment, because his impurity is an impurity of belief in a metaphorical sense. As for the menstruating woman, she informs him of her condition, so that the judge goes out to her or sends his deputy, as if the claim was concerning an animal. And he does not judge while his heart is preoccupied with joy, anger, worry, need for intercourse, severe cold, severe heat, or the urge to relieve oneself of the two excrements. And it is not befitting that he volunteer a fast on the day he intends to sit for judgment, and he should go out in his best clothes and his most moderate state. Mentioned by 'Allamah al-Tahtawi in his Hashiyah on al-Durr al-Mukhtar. [2] His statement: 'and the Jami' is more preferable' because it is not hidden from strangers and others. And it is mentioned in 'al-Khazanah': The Jami' is better if it is in the middle of the city, but if it is on the outskirts, then he chooses a mosque in the market to be more famous. End. So the conclusion is that he sits for judgment in the most famous places and gatherings of people, and there is no chamberlain or gatekeeper for it. In 'Sharh Adab al-Qadi': He may appoint a gatekeeper to prevent the litigants from crowding, and it is not permissible for the gatekeeper to take anything for permission to enter. Thus in 'al-Bahr'. [1] His statement: 'public sitting' and he should not exhaust himself with prolonged sitting, but he sits at the two ends of the day, and similarly the mufti and the judge. Thus in 'al-Fath'. [2] His statement: 'and for us is the sitting of the Prophet', summary: He used to resolve disputes in his place of seclusion (mu'takaf), as established in the books of hadith, and the Rightly Guided Caliphs used to sit for judgment in the mosques; and because judgment is an act of worship as we have previously established. So its establishment in the mosque is permissible like prayer, and the mosque is placed for worship, and the polytheist does not have a physical impurity that contaminates, rather that is in his belief, and the menstruating woman informs of her condition because she is a Muslim, so the judge goes out to her or sends his deputy, as if the claim was concerning an animal as we have mentioned. [3] His statement: 'it is permissible' because judgment is an act of worship not restricted to a place, and it is better that the house be in the middle of the city like the mosque. Thus in 'al-Nahr'. And he should not judge while walking, because opinion is not gathered while he is occupied with walking, and there is no harm in...
Source pages p. 427

Commentary
This is an inter-madhhab dispute about where a judge should sit for court proceedings, with the Hanafi position that sitting in the mosque is permissible and preferred, contrary to the Shafi'i school which considers it disliked. The passage begins by stating that the judge sits publicly in the mosque, and the congregational mosque (jami') is more preferable. The commentator explains that the judge must sit in a manner that makes it clear he is there for judging, not for other worship, to avoid confusion. This is based on the practice of the Prophet and the Rightly Guided Caliphs. It is also further from suspicion and because judging is an act of worship, and mosques are made for worship. However, al-Hamawi notes that in his time, people do not respect mosques, so the ruling may differ practically. Then the text discusses recommended etiquettes: praying greeting rak'ahs, supplicating, facing qiblah, and turning back to qiblah like a preacher. It also addresses the entry of a polytheist: he is not prevented because his impurity is spiritual, not physical. For a menstruating woman, she should inform the judge so he can go out to her or send a deputy. The judge should not judge when his heart is occupied with various distractions. He should not fast on the day of judging and should dress well. The passage then clarifies that the jami' is more preferable due to being widely known, especially if in the city center; otherwise a mosque in the market is better. The overall aim is to judge in the most famous places. The text also notes that a gatekeeper may be appointed to prevent crowding but cannot charge. Finally, the second ruling allows the judge to sit in his own house and give permission for entry, as judgment is an act of worship not tied to a specific location, but it is better for the house to be centrally located. The passage ends by stating that a judge should not judge while walking, as deliberation is compromised.

Key Terms
مسجد (masjid): A place of worship for Muslims, where the judge may sit for judgment.
جامع (jami'): A congregational mosque where Friday prayers are held, considered more preferable for judgment due to its visibility.
قضاء (qada): Judicial decision-making and dispute resolution by a qualified judge.
مشرك (mushrik): A polytheist, who is allowed to enter the mosque for judicial proceedings because his impurity is spiritual.
حائض (ha'id): A menstruating woman; she should inform the judge so he can go out to her or send a deputy, as she cannot enter the mosque.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because the mushrik's impurity is physical and can be removed.
B) Because the mushrik's impurity is spiritual and does not contaminate the mosque.
C) Because the judge can expel the mushrik from the mosque.
D) Because the mosque is only for prayer, so judgment is separate.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school recommends a judge sit publicly in the mosque (preferably the jami') for judgment, rebutting Shafi'i concerns about impurity with the distinction between spiritual and physical impurity and by accommodating menstruating women outside the mosque.

Judge's Place of Sitting: Mosque Permissibility and Conditions
Ruling on judge sitting openly with inter-madhhab dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a judge sitting in the mosque for judgment, distinguish it from the Shafi'i position, and apply the conditions of the judge's sitting (openness, doorkeeper, posture, consultation).

Core Idea
A judge may sit in the mosque for judgment; the Prophet and Caliphs did so, and the Shafi'i objection is answered by distinguishing spiritual from physical impurity.

This passage addresses whether a judge can hold court in a mosque. The Hanafi school permits it, following the Prophet's practice. The Shafi'i school objects due to potential impurity from polytheists or menstruating women. The Hanafi response clarifies that polytheist impurity is spiritual, and menstruating women are accommodated by the judge going out. The passage further details conditions: open sitting, doorkeeper rules, posture, and consultation.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling on a judge sitting in the mosque: it is permissible (jā'iz) and even preferable if the mosque is in the center of town.
The base text says 'jāz' (permissible) for sitting in the house, and the commentary extends this to the mosque.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
The Shafi'i school considers sitting in the mosque disliked because polytheists or menstruating women might enter, causing impurity.
Shafi'i dalil: avoidance of impurity in the mosque.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Shafi'i argues that the mosque is for worship and should be kept free of anything that could cause ritual impurity (e.g., polytheist's presence, menstruating woman).
This is based on general principles of mosque sanctity.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
Hanafi rebuts: polytheist impurity is in belief, not body; menstruating women are Muslims—judge goes out or sends a deputy. Also cites Prophetic practice as stronger proof.
The Hanafi position is supported by the Prophet's actual conduct (dalīl naqlī).
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
In Hanafi, judge can hold court in mosque; in Shafi'i, it is disliked and should be avoided. Practical consequence: different venue choices.
The difference matters for court location planning.
