There is no harm in judging while reclining because it increases opinion due to increased comfort; but sitting upright for judgment is better to magnify the matter of judgment. It is reported from Abu Yusuf that he was asked for a fatwa while reclining, so he sat upright, wrapped his garment, put on a turban, then gave the fatwa; to magnify the matter of fatwa. And he should not sit alone because it gives rise to suspicion. If he sits alone, there is no harm if he is knowledgeable about judgment, but if he is ignorant, it is recommended that he sit with knowledgeable people because it is not safe that he might stray from the truth, so they warn him. And as for the assistants, he seats them near him for consultation, and likewise the just persons for witnessing against him. As for the guards, they are far from him because they are for awe, and he is more awe-inspiring. Thus in al-Tabyin.)
Source pages p. 428

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute regarding the permissibility of a judge sitting in the mosque for giving judgments. The base text states that if the judge sits in his house and permits entry, it is permissible, but the commentary addresses the Shafi'i view that sitting in the mosque is disliked because polytheists or menstruating women might enter. The Hanafi response is twofold: first, the Prophet himself sat in the mosque to settle disputes, so it is established by practice; second, the impurity of the polytheist is spiritual, not physical, and menstruating women are Muslims who can be handled by the judge going out to her. Therefore, sitting in the mosque is permissible. The passage further details conditions: the judge should sit openly in a well-known place, should not have a doorkeeper (though he may appoint one to prevent crowding, and that doorkeeper cannot take fees), should not tire himself with long sitting, and should not judge while walking. Sitting upright is better. Abu Yusuf's practice of straightening up when giving a fatwa is cited. The judge should not sit alone to avoid suspicion, but if he is knowledgeable it is permissible; if ignorant, he should have scholars sit with him for advice. Assistants sit near for consultation, guards far for awe.

Key Terms
جلوس ظاهر (julūs ẓāhir): Sitting in a visible, well-known place where people gather around, not a private session.
حاجب (ḥājib): A doorkeeper who prevents crowding; he cannot take payment for permission to enter.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A judge may sit in the mosque for judgment; the Prophet and Caliphs did so, and the Hanafi school answers Shafi'i objections with spiritual-physical impurity distinction and accommodation for menstruating women.

Judge Accepting Gifts Prohibited
Ruling on judge accepting gifts with proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a judge accepting gifts and explain the proof from hadith and the reasoning (ʿilla).

Core Idea
A judge may not accept any gift—it is prohibited like a bribe.

This lesson addresses a critical aspect of judicial integrity in Islam: the prohibition of a judge accepting gifts. Even if a gift is given without an explicit condition, it is prohibited because it resembles a bribe and corrupts the judiciary. The passage provides the ruling, defines key terms, and supports it with a hadith from Sahih al-Bukhari and the practice of the Companions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its foundation
Start by extracting the explicit prohibition: 'a judge should not accept a gift.' Note that the ruling is based on the principle of avoiding anything that resembles a bribe.
The ruling is clear in the text: (ولا يقبل هدية).
2
Define and distinguish key terms
Understand the difference between a gift (هدية) and a bribe (رشوة). A gift is given without any condition of assistance, while a bribe is conditional. This distinction shows that even unconditional gifts are prohibited for a judge.
The text explicitly distinguishes them.
3
Analyze the primary proof (dalil)
Examine the hadith of Abu Humayd al-Sa'idi: the Prophet criticized a charity collector who accepted gifts. The rhetorical question 'Why did he not sit in his father's house…?' indicates that gifts given due to an official position are prohibited.
The hadith is narrated in Sahih al-Bukhari and is considered sound (zanni al-thubut).
4
Understand the effective cause (ʿilla)
The reasoning (taʿlil) of the Prophet—that gifts would not have been given were it not for his position—establishes that any gift received because of a judicial role is prohibited. This is the core legal principle derived from the proof.
The text mentions: 'تعليل النبي دليل على تحريم الهدية التي سببها الولاية'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(ولا يقبل هدية) وهي ما يعطيه بلا شرط أن يعينه بخلاف الرشوة، فإنّه شرط معها، ووجه التأكيد لعدم قبول الهدية أنها شبه الرشوة، فيتجنب عنها، وعلى هذا كانت الصحابة رضوان الله تعالى عليهم أجمعين. والأصل في ذلك ما روي عن أبي حميد الساعدي صلى الله عليه وسلم قال: «استعمل النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم رجلاً من الأزد، يقال له: أبي الثبية على الصدقة، فلما قدم قال: هذا لكم وهذا لي، قال: هلا جلس في بيت أبيه أو بيت أمه، فينظر أيهدى له أم لا»، قال عمر بن عبد العزيز له كانت الهدية على عهد رسول الله هدية، واليوم رشوة. ذكره البخاري. واستعمل عمر أبا هريرة، فقدم بمال فقال له: من أين لك هذا، قال: تلاحقت الهدايا، فقال له: أي عدو الله هلا قعدت في بيتك فتنظر أيهدى لك عمر أم لا، فأخذ ذلك منه ووجعله في بيت المال، وتعليلُ النبي دليل على تحريم الهدية التي سببها الولاية. كذا في «الفتح» وغيره.
(And he should not accept a gift) which is what is given without condition that he help him, unlike a bribe, where there is a condition along with it. The emphasis on not accepting a gift is that it resembles a bribe, so he should avoid it. And upon this were the Companions, may Allah be pleased with them all. The basis for this is what was narrated from Abu Humayd al-Sa'idi, may Allah be pleased with him: 'The Prophet, peace be upon him, appointed a man from the Azd tribe, called Ibn al-Thabiyah, over the charity. When he returned, he said: This is for you and this is for me. He said: Why did he not sit in his father's house or his mother's house to see if he would be given a gift?'. 'Umar ibn 'Abd al-'Aziz said: 'Gifts during the time of the Messenger of Allah were gifts, but today they are bribes.' Al-Bukhari mentioned it. And 'Umar appointed Abu Hurayrah, and he brought money and said: Where did you get this? He said: Gifts came one after another. He said: Enemy of Allah, why did you not sit in your house to see if you would be given a gift? So he took it from him and placed it in the treasury. And the Prophet's reasoning is evidence for the prohibition of gifts that are caused by holding a position of authority. Thus in al-Fath and others.)
Source pages p. 429

Commentary
This passage states the ruling that a judge should not accept gifts (هدية) because they resemble bribes (رشوة). The key difference is that a gift is given without any condition of assistance, while a bribe is conditional. The passage emphasizes that the judge must avoid any gift, as the Companions did. The proof comes from a hadith in Sahih al-Bukhari: the Prophet criticized a charity collector who kept part for himself, implying that gifts given due to one's official position are prohibited. Furthermore, 'Umar ibn al-Khattab confiscated a similar gift from Abu Hurayrah. The 'illa (effective cause) is that the gift is given because of the person's position of authority (wilayah). Therefore, the ruling is clear: a judge may not accept gifts during his tenure.

Key Terms
هدية (hadiyyah): A gift given without any condition of assistance, as opposed to a bribe.
رشوة (rishwah): A bribe given with the explicit or implicit condition of being helped or favored in a legal matter.

Key Takeaway
A judge must avoid all gifts because they resemble bribes and are prohibited by the evidence of the Prophet's hadith and the practice of the Companions.

Prohibition of Gifts to Judges and Exceptions
Explaining the prohibition of gift acceptance by judges with proof and exceptions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a judge accepting gifts, explain the evidence, and identify the exceptions for mahram relatives and habitual givers.

Core Idea
A judge's gift from a non-mahram is haram unless the giver habitually gave before the appointment.

In this lesson, we examine the strict prohibition on judges accepting gifts, rooted in the principle that such gifts resemble bribery. The text provides evidence from the Prophet's hadith and the practice of Caliph Umar, and then outlines exceptions for close relatives and those with a prior habit of giving. We will also explore how this ruling differs for muftis and teachers.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what is being ruled upon: a judge accepting a gift. Identify the hukm level: haram. Distinguish between a gift (hadiyyah) and bribery (rishwa).
The text emphasizes that gifts resemble bribery, hence the strict prohibition.
2
Locate the dalil
Examine the evidence: the hadith of Ibn al-Thubayya (Bukhari) and the incident with Abu Hurayrah under Umar. Understand the Prophet's rhetorical question as proof of prohibition.
The reasoning (ta'lil) is that gifts given due to office are blameworthy.
3
Note the exceptions
Identify the two exceptions: gifts from mahram relatives (where marriage is permanently prohibited) and from those who habitually gave before the judge's appointment.
Accepting from mahram preserves family ties; rejecting would be a prohibited severance.
4
Apply the distinction for other roles
Recognize that muftis, preachers, and teachers do not have authority to enforce (ilzam), so the strict prohibition does not apply to them. They have their own guidelines.
The first preference for them is to avoid gifts for services to maintain sincerity, but accept if given as greeting or affection.

بطريق الأجرة بل مجرد هدية؛ لأن أخذ الأجرة على بيان الحكم الشرعي لا يحل عندنا، وإنما يحل على الكتابة؛ لأنها غير واجبة عليه. هنا ذكره العلامة الشامي في رد المحتار ونقلناه بأدنى اختصار وتغيير. قوله: إلا من ذي رحم؛ لأن قبول هديته من صلة الرحم، وورود التأكيد فيها، وفي رد الهدية عليه قطيعة الرحم وهي حرام. وقيده بقوله: محرم منه، ليخرج ابن العم ونحوه فإنه ليس داخلاً في هذا الحكم. قوله: أو من اعتاد ما كان إلا الفقر.
And he does not accept a gift except from a close blood relative (mahram) or from one who habitually gave gifts to him. The statement: 'And he does not accept a gift' – a gift is what is given without the condition of assistance, unlike bribery where assistance is conditioned. The emphasis on not accepting gifts is because it resembles bribery, so one should avoid it, and upon this were the Companions. The basis for this is what was narrated from Abu Hamid al-Sa'idi that the Prophet appointed a man from al-Azd called Ibn al-Thubayya to collect sadaqah. When he returned, he said: 'This is for you and this is for me.' The Prophet said: 'Why did he not sit in his father's or mother's house to see whether he would be given gifts or not?' 'Umar ibn 'Abd al-'Aziz said: 'Gifts during the time of the Messenger were gifts, but today they are bribery.' Bukhari mentioned it. And 'Umar appointed Abu Hurayrah, who brought money. 'Umar asked: 'Where did you get this?' He said: 'Gifts came to me.' 'Umar said: 'O enemy of Allah, why did you not sit in your house to see whether gifts would be given to you or not?' He took it and placed it in the public treasury. The Prophet's reasoning indicates the prohibition of gifts that arise from office. The mufti, preacher, Quran teacher, and scholar are not like the judge in this ruling, because they lack the capacity to enforce obligation. It is better for them not to accept if the gift is for their services, so that their work is purely for Allah. If it is given as a greeting or affection for their knowledge and righteousness, then acceptance is better. If a mufti takes a gift to give a lenient ruling, if it is invalid, he is a sinner who changes Allah's rulings; if it is correct, it is severely disliked. If he takes it not for leniency but to explain the law, then it is as mentioned, provided it is not a fee, because taking a fee for explaining the law is not permissible in our school, except for writing since it is not obligatory. The statement: 'Except from a close relative' because accepting his gift is upholding kinship ties, which is emphasized, and refusing it is severing ties, which is forbidden. Restricting it to mahram excludes paternal cousins and the like. The statement: 'Or from one who habitually gave gifts' – the apparent form of the conjunction implies that he may accept from a relative even without habit, but some scholars say that like a non-relative, habit is necessary; otherwise, it is not acceptable unless the giver was poor and then became wealthy, because the apparent reason for the gift was poverty.
Source pages p. 429

Commentary
This passage is an expansion on the ruling that a judge (qadi) should not accept gifts. The text begins by defining a gift (hiba) as something given without the condition of assistance, distinguishing it from bribery (rishwa). The prohibition is emphasized because gifts to a judge are akin to bribery. Evidence is presented from a hadith where the Prophet criticized a collector who kept some gifts for himself, and from the practice of Umar ibn al-Khattab with Abu Hurayrah. The text then states that this ruling does not apply to a mufti, preacher, or teacher because they do not have the authority to enforce (ilzam). However, they should be cautious to ensure their work remains sincere. A mufti who takes a gift to give a lenient ruling is sinful if the ruling is false, and if correct, it is severely disliked. If he takes it without expecting leniency, it is permissible provided it is not a fee. The passage then explains the exceptions: gifts from mahram relatives (those marriage is permanently prohibited) are acceptable because refusing would break family ties. Also, gifts from those who habitually gave before appointment are acceptable, because rejecting them would indicate the gift was only for the position. The text notes a difference of opinion whether a relative must have a prior habit of giving.

Key Terms
هدية (hadiyyah): Something given without condition of assistance, distinguished from bribery.
رشوة (rishwa): Bribery: something given with the condition of assistance or favor.
ذو رحم محرم (dhu rahim muharram): A blood relative with whom marriage is permanently prohibited (e.g., parent, child, sibling).
إلزام (ilzam): The authority to enforce a ruling; judges have it, muftis do not.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Exceptions to a Judge Accepting Gifts
Commentary expanding exceptions to gifts for judge

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two exceptions allowing a judge to accept gifts and apply the distinct ruling for muftis, preachers, and teachers.

Core Idea
A judge may accept gifts only from mahram relatives or customary givers; others must refuse to preserve integrity.

The general rule is that a judge may not accept gifts from anyone, as it could influence judgment. However, the Hanafi school makes two clear exceptions: gifts from mahram relatives (to maintain family ties) and from those who customarily gave gifts before the judge assumed office. This lesson expands on these exceptions, addresses borrowing and compensation, and distinguishes judges from other religious figures like muftis and teachers, whose rulings differ.

Guided Path
1
Identify the General Prohibition
Understand that the baseline ruling is that a judge cannot accept gifts, borrow items, or receive loans from anyone, as these are akin to bribery.
The text explicitly extends prohibition to borrowing and borrowing items.
2
Recognize the Two Exceptions
The exceptions are: (1) gifts from a mahram relative (e.g., parent, child, sibling), and (2) gifts from a person who customarily gave gifts before the judge became a judge.
Customary gift-giving must be established and regular; excess over the usual amount is not allowed.
3
Understand the Compensation Option
If a non-mahram, non-customary giver would be offended by refusal, the judge may accept the gift but must give its value in return.
This is based on the principle of avoiding harm to relationships.
4
Distinguish Judges from Other Religious Figures
Muftis, preachers, Quran teachers, and scholars do not have the authority of compulsion (al-ilzam). Therefore, the ruling is different: if the gift is given for their services, it is better to refuse; if given out of respect, acceptance is preferred.
The distinction hinges on the absence of legal authority to impose rulings.
5
Analyze the Mufti's Special Case
If a mufti takes a gift to be lenient in a fatwa: if the leniency is invalid, it is a grave sin; if valid, it is severely disliked. Taking a gift without any condition is permissible.
Intent matters: gifts for leniency corrupt the fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

وذكر الهدية في الكتاب ليس احترازياً إذ يحرم عليه الاستقراض والاستعارة من يحرم عليه قبول هدية. كذا في «البحر» نقلاً عن «الخانية» : فإن كان المهدي يتأدّى بالردّ يقبلها ويعطيه مثل قيمتها. كذا في «الخلاصة».

Except from a mahram relative, or from one who customarily gives gifts to him. And the mention of gift in the book is not restrictive, for it is prohibited for him to borrow or borrow an item from one from whom it is prohibited to accept a gift. Likewise in 'al-Bahr' quoting 'al-Khaniyya': if the giver would be offended by return, he may accept it and give him its value. Likewise in 'al-Khulasa'. And the mufti, preacher, Quran teacher, and scholar are not included with the judge in what was mentioned, because they do not have the authority of compulsion. The better for them, if the gift is for what they do of ifta', preaching, and teaching, is not to accept, so that their work is purely for Allah. But if they are given a gift as a greeting and affection for their knowledge and righteousness, then acceptance is better. As for the mufti who takes a gift to be lenient in the fatwa: if it is by an invalid means, he is a wicked man who changes Allah's rulings and sells them for a small price; if by a valid means, it is severely disliked. As for taking it not to be lenient but to explain the legal ruling, this is what was mentioned.

Commentary
This passage is a commentary expanding on the ruling of a judge accepting gifts. The base text states that a judge may accept a gift only from a mahram relative (e.g., father, son, brother) or from someone who habitually gave gifts before the judge assumed office. The commentator explains that the prohibition of accepting gifts extends to borrowing and borrowing items, as these are similar to gifts. If a non-mahram giver would be offended by refusal, the judge may accept the gift and compensate its value. The commentator then distinguishes between judges and other religious figures like muftis, preachers, and teachers: since they lack the power to impose rulings, the ruling is different. For them, if the gift is given for their services, it is better to refuse to maintain sincerity; but if given purely out of respect, acceptance is preferred. Finally, a mufti who takes a gift to be lenient in issuing fatwas is condemned: if the leniency is invalid, it is a great sin; if valid, it is severely disliked. However, taking a gift without any condition is permissible as per the earlier ruling.

Key Terms
ذو رحم محرم (dhu rahim muharram): A relative with whom marriage is permanently prohibited, such as parent, child, sibling, etc.
مهاداة (muhada'at): Customary mutual gift-giving between two parties, established before one became a judge.
قدر معهود (qadr ma'hud): The customary amount of the gift, not exceeding what was previously given.

When May a Judge Accept Gifts? Hanafi Conditions for Permissibility
Explains when a judge may accept gifts without suspicion of bribery

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a Hanafi judge may accept a gift and distinguish between permissible and prohibited gifts.

Core Idea
A judge may accept a customary gift from a pre-existing habit or from a mahram, but never during litigation.

The judiciary demands absolute impartiality. Yet, judges are human—they have relatives and friends. How does Hanafi fiqh navigate this tension? This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah explains that a judge may accept gifts if they are part of a pre-existing custom or from a close relative, provided no lawsuit is involved. This lesson unpacks those conditions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is a judge accepting a gift. The ruling is mubah (permissible) under specific conditions; otherwise, it becomes haram if it creates suspicion of bribery.
The default prohibition is rooted in the principle of sadd al-dhara'i' (blocking the means to evil).
2
List the permitted categories
The passage permits gifts from two types: (1) a mahram relative, (2) someone who habitually gave gifts before the judge's appointment.
The habit must be established before judgeship, not after.
3
Analyze the condition of customary amount
The gift must be of a customary amount (ma'hud). If the donor's wealth increased, a proportionally larger gift may be acceptable.
The increase must correspond to the donor's increased means, not be arbitrary.
4
Examine the prohibition during litigation
If there is an ongoing lawsuit between the judge and donor, the gift is not accepted because it is then for the judgment.
This applies even to mahram relatives and habitual donors.
5
Consider the after-litigation situation and borrowing
After the lawsuit ends, the judge may accept the gift. Also, a judge should only borrow from pre-existing friends to avoid suspicion.
Borrowing is governed by similar principles to prevent tuhmah (suspicion).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب القضاء الشهادة
قدْراً معهوداً إذا لم يكن لهما خصومة
قَدْراً معهوداً إذا لم يكن لهما خصومة
الفقر. كذا في «البحر»

وإنما يقبلُ الهدية ممن اعتاد مهاداته ؛ لأنها ليست للقضاء، بل هي جري على العادة، فلا يتوهم فيها الرشوة، وقال في «الأشباه»: لم أر بماذا ثبتت العادة، ونقل الحموي عن بعضهم : إنها تثبت عبرة.
[ ۱ ] قوله : قدراً معهوداً ؛ فلو زادَ لا يقبلُ الزيادة، وذكر فخر الإسلام : إلا أن يكون مال المهدي قد زاد ، فبقدر ما زادَ ،ماله إذا زاد في الهدية لا بأس بقبولها. كذا في «الفتح».
[٢) قوله : إذا لم يكن ... الخ ؛ أي يقبلُ الهدية من ذي رحم محرم منه، أو ممن اعتاد قبل القضاء مهاداته بالقدر الذي كان . معهود وداً عنده ، إذا لم يكن لذي رحم محرم منه ، ولمن اعتاد مهاداته خصومة فإذا كان بينهما خصومة لا يقبلُ الهدية منهما ؛ لأنها حينئذ للقضاء، فإن قبلها بعد انقطاع الخصومة جاز، كما صرحوابه. قال العلامة شيخنا الطحطاوي في حاشيته على الدر المختار ) ، فإن كان لها خصومة ردّها ، قال في «النهر»: أما إذا تمت الخصومة فينبغي أن لا يتردد في جواز قبولها ، قلت : إلا أن يكون ممن لا تناهى خصوماته ، كنظار الأوقاف ومباشر بهما انتهى. حموي. قلت : وعلى بحث صاحب «النهر» نص ابن ملك في «شرح المجمع»: قال في الهندية (4): ولا ينبغي أن يستقرض إلا من صديق أو خليط له كان قبل أن يستقضي، ولا يخاصم إليه، ولا يتهمه أن يعين خصماً ، وكذلك الاستعارة. انتهـى كــلام الطحطاوي.
The book of judiciary and testimony. A customary amount, if there is no litigation between them. A customary amount, if there is no litigation between them. The poor. Thus in al-Bahr. And he only accepts the gift from one who habitually gave to him; because it is not for the judgment, rather it is a matter of custom, so no bribery is suspected. And he said in al-Ashbah: I have not seen by what the custom is established, and al-Himawi transmitted from some: it is established by experience. [1] His saying: a customary amount; if he increased, he does not accept the increase, and Fakhr al-Islam mentioned: unless the donor's wealth has increased, then according to the increase, his wealth; if he increased in the gift, there is no harm in accepting it. Thus in al-Fath. [2] His saying: if there is not... i.e., he accepts the gift from a mahram relative, or from one who habitually gave to him before the judiciary in the customary amount, if it is not a mahram relative, and from one who habitually gave to him there is litigation; if there is litigation between them, he does not accept the gift from them because then it is for the judgment; if he accepts it after the litigation ends, it is permissible, as they explicitly stated. The scholar our master al-Tahtawi said in his hashya on al-Durr al-Mukhtar: if there is litigation, he returns it; he said in al-Nahr: after the litigation is complete, one should not hesitate in the permissibility of accepting it; I said: unless he is someone whose litigations never end, like the supervisors of endowments and their directors. al-Himawi. I said: and according to the research of the author of al-Nahr, Ibn Malik in Sharh al-Majma' stated: he said in al-Hindiyya: He should not borrow except from a friend or associate he had before becoming a judge, and he should not litigate with him, and he should not suspect him of helping an opponent, and likewise borrowing. End of the words of al-Tahtawi.
Source pages p. 431

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah is a commentary on the ruling of a judge accepting gifts. The author explains that a judge may accept a gift from someone who habitually gave gifts to him before his appointment as judge, because such a gift is customary and not given for the sake of the judgment, thus no suspicion of bribery arises. Similarly, a judge may accept gifts from a close relative (mahram) such as his spouse or blood relatives within the prohibited degrees. However, if there is an ongoing litigation between the judge and the donor, or if the judge has a lawsuit with that person, he must not accept the gift. If the litigation ends, he may accept it. The commentators also discuss the issue of increase in the gift: if the donor's wealth has increased, the judge may accept a proportionally larger gift. The passage emphasizes that the judge must avoid any action that could lead to suspicion of bribery, as honesty and impartiality are paramount in the judiciary.

Key Terms
الهدية (al-hadiyyah): A gift given to a judge, which is evaluated based on custom and intention.
الرشوة (al-rashwah): Bribery, which is giving something to a judge to influence his decision.
ذو رحم محرم (dhu rahim mahram): A relative with whom marriage is permanently prohibited, such as mother, father, sibling, etc.
معهود (ma'hud): Customary amount or practice that is well-known and habitual.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Under what condition may a judge accept a gift from a non-relative after his appointment?
A) The gift is of small value.
B) The donor habitually gave gifts before the judge's appointment and there is no litigation.
C) The donor is a friend of the judge.
D) The judge has never judged a case involving the donor.

Key Takeaway
A Hanafi judge may accept a gift only if it is from a mahram relative or from a pre-existing habit of gift-giving, and only if no ongoing litigation exists between them.

Attending Invitations as a Judge
Explains the rule on judge attending invitations and intra-Hanafi dispute on relatives

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a judge attending invitations and distinguish between the positions of Abu Hanifa/Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad regarding relatives.

Core Idea
A judge avoids all special invitations to maintain integrity; even family ties do not override suspicion.

A judge's integrity is paramount, and even the appearance of bias must be avoided. This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya discusses when a judge may accept food invitations, focusing on the critical distinction between general and special invitations. It also presents an intra-Hanafi dispute: while Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf absolutely prohibit attending special invitations, Imam Muhammad allows them for relatives to uphold family ties.

Guided Path
1
State the General Rule
Identify the baseline ruling: a judge may only attend general invitations (e.g., weddings, circumcisions) where food is prepared for the public, not specifically for him.
A general invitation is one the host would have prepared even without the judge's presence.
2
Identify the Intra-Hanafi Dispute
The dispute is about special invitations from relatives. Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf say the judge never attends a special invitation, even from a relative. Imam Muhammad permits it if the invitation is from a relative to maintain family ties.
The disagreement is over whether the duty to avoid suspicion outweighs the duty of silat al-rahim.
3
Examine the Majority Proof
The majority (Abu Hanifa & Abu Yusuf) rely on the principle of avoiding suspicion (sadd al-dhara'i'). Any special invitation creates an appearance of bias, so it must be avoided absolutely.
The text emphasizes 'because that brings suspicion upon him' (لما فيه من التهمة).
4
Examine Muhammad's Proof
Imam Muhammad argues that maintaining family ties (silat al-rahim) is a command, and the judiciary does not override it. Attending a relative's special invitation is permissible if it does not involve bribery.
The text cites al-Hidayah: 'because in that is maintaining family ties, and he is commanded to do so'.
5
Determine the Mufta Bihi and Practical Consequence
The majority view (Abu Hanifa & Abu Yusuf) is mufta bihi to prioritize judicial integrity. The practical difference: a judge following the majority never attends a relative's special invitation; a follower of Muhammad may attend.
Later commentators like al-Bahr and al-Tatarkhaniyyah add further conditions for general invitations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يَحْضُرُ دعوة إلا عامة : العامّةُ : هي التي يتَّخذها النَّاس . قوله : دعوة ؛ قال في «المصباح المنير » : الدعوة : بالفتح في الطعام : اسم من دعوت النّاس : إذا طلبتهم ليأكلوا عندك ، يقال : نحنُ في دعوة فلان ومَدْعاتِه ودُعائِهِ بمعنى، قال أبو عبيدة : وهذا كلام أكثر العرب إلا عَدي الرباب، فإنّهم يعكسون، ويجعلون الفتح في النسب، والكسر في الطعام. انتهى. قوله : ولا يحضر دعوة إلا عامة ؛ لأنَّ الخاصة لأجل القضاء، فيتهم بالإجابة بخلاف العامة. وقيد في «السراج الوهاج» جواز حضور القاضي الدعوة العامة بما إذا كان صاحبها ممن يعتاد اتخاذها له قبل تقلّد القضاء، أما إذا لم يكن كذلك لا يحضرها لما في من التهمة، قال : وأما إذا كانت الدعوة خاصة لا يحضرها أبداً سواء كانت لقريبه أو لأجنبي عندهما ؛ لأنَّ ذلك يدخل عليه التهمة. وقال محمد : لا بأس أن يجيبها إذا كانت لقريبه . كما في «الهداية» ؛ لأنَّ في ذلك صلة الرحم، وهو مأمور بها ، فلا يمنع القضاء من ذلك. ثم بحث وقال : وذكر صدر الإسلام أبو اليسر الله إذا كان للمضيف خصومة لا يحضر دعوته العامة لما فيه من التهمة. انتهى. قال في «البحر»: الأحسنُ أن يقال : ولا يقبل هدية ودعوة خاصة إلا من محرم أو ممن له عادة، فإن للقاضي أن يجيب الدعوة الخاصة من أجنبي له عادة، باتخاذها كالهدية، فلو كان من عادته الدَّعوة له في كلِّ شهر مرَّة فدعاه كل إسبوع بعد القضاء لا يجيبه، ولو اتخذ له طعاماً أكثر من الأوَّل لا يجيبه إلا أن يكون ماله قد زاد. كذا في «التاتارخانية» انتهى. [۳] قوله : العامة هي التي..الخ ؛ فالخاصة هي التي لا يتخذها صاحبها ، لولا حضور القاضي ، وقيل : دعوة العرس والختان عامة وما سواهما خاصة، وقيل: إن
He (the judge) does not attend an invitation except a general one. The general is that which people prepare. His saying: invitation; he said in al-Misbah al-Munir: invitation, with fathah in food, is a noun from 'I invited people' when you ask them to eat with you. It is said: we are at the invitation of so-and-so, and his mad'ah, and his du'a, meaning the same. Abu Ubaydah said: This is the speech of most Arabs except Adi al-Ribab, for they reverse, making fathah in lineage and kasrah in food. End. His saying: And he does not attend an invitation except a general one; because the special one is for the sake of the judiciary, so he is suspected of responding, unlike the general. And it is restricted in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj regarding the permissibility of the judge attending a general invitation to when its preparer is one who habitually made it for him before undertaking the judiciary; otherwise, he does not attend it because of the suspicion involved. He said: And if the invitation is special, he never attends it, whether for his relative or a stranger, according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Abu Hanifa?); because that brings suspicion upon him. And Muhammad said: There is no harm in responding if it is for his relative, as in al-Hidayah; because in that is maintaining family ties, and he is commanded to do so, so the judiciary does not prevent that. Then he discussed and said: And Sadr al-Islam Abu al-Yusr Allah mentioned that if the host has a litigation, he does not attend even his general invitation because of the suspicion. End. He said in al-Bahr: The best is to say: And he does not accept a gift or a special invitation except from a mahram or from one with a custom; because the judge may respond to a special invitation from a stranger who has a habit of making it, like a gift. So if his custom was to invite him once a month, then he invites him every week after the judiciary, he does not respond; and if he prepares food more than before, he does not respond unless his wealth has increased. Thus in al-Tatarkhaniyyah. End. [3] His saying: The general is that which...; and the special is that which its preparer would not prepare except for the judge's presence. And it is said: The invitation of wedding and circumcision is general, and what is besides them is special. And it is said: Indeed...
Source pages p. 432

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling on a judge attending invitations. The general principle is that a judge should only attend general invitations (such as weddings or circumcision feasts) where the food is prepared for the public, to avoid suspicion of bribery. Special invitations made specifically for the judge are prohibited because they create a conflict of interest. However, there is an intra-Hanafi dispute: The majority (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) hold that a judge should never attend a special invitation, even from a relative, due to suspicion. Imam Muhammad allows attending a relative's special invitation because maintaining family ties (silat al-rahim) is a religious obligation. The commentators further refine the conditions: the permissibility of attending general invitations depends on whether the host habitually invited the judge before his appointment. If the host is a litigant, even general invitations cannot be attended. The relied-upon view is that of the majority to prioritize avoidance of suspicion, but the custom-based exceptions for habitual invitations are accepted.

Key Terms
دعوة عامة (da'wah 'ammah): A general invitation where food is prepared for the public, not specifically for the judge.
دعوة خاصة (da'wah khassah): A special invitation made specifically for the judge, which suggests intent to influence.
صلة الرحم (silat al-rahim): Maintaining family ties, an obligatory act in Islam according to some views.
التهمة (al-tuhmah): Suspicion or accusation of wrongdoing, which must be avoided by a judge.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the majority of Hanafi imams (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf), may a judge attend a special invitation from his brother after becoming a judge?
A) Yes, because maintaining family ties is obligatory.
B) No, because it creates suspicion irrespective of the relationship.
C) Yes, if the brother does not have a case with the judge.
D) No, but he may attend if the invitation is general.

Key Takeaway
A judge must avoid any invitation that creates suspicion of bias, even from relatives; the majority Hanafi view prioritizes judicial integrity over family ties in this context.

Ruling on Judge Attending Invitations
Ruling on judge's attendance at invitations with intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between public and private invitations for a judge, state the ruling for each, and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding a judge attending a relative's private invitation.

Core Idea
A judge may only attend public invitations; private ones create suspicion and are prohibited except by Imam Muhammad for relatives.

This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling on a judge attending invitations. The core principle is that a judge must avoid anything that creates suspicion of bias. The passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah clarifies the distinction between public and private invitations and presents a difference between Abu Hanifa/Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad regarding relatives.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base ruling
The text states that a judge should not attend any invitation except a public one. This is the foundation.
2
Define public vs. private
Public invitations are customary events not arranged specifically for the judge. Private invitations are arranged because of the judge's position, creating suspicion.
Definitions may vary: some say wedding/circumcision feasts are public; others define by number of guests.
3
Analyze the suspicion rationale
Private invitations lead to tuhma (suspicion) that the judge may be influenced. Therefore, the majority of Hanafi imams prohibit them absolutely.
This is the 'illa (cause) behind the ruling.
4
Examine the intra-Hanafi dispute
Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf prohibit all private invitations. Imam Muhammad allows them if from a relative, citing the obligation of maintaining family ties.
The passage also discusses conditions: the public invitation must be from someone who habitually made it before the judge's appointment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يَحْضُرُ دعوة إلا عامة . العامّةُ : هي التي يتَّخذها النَّاس (1) قوله : دعوة ؛ قال في «المصباح المنير » ) : الدعوة : بالفتح في الطعام : اسم من دعوت النّاس : إذا طلبتهم ليأكلوا عندك ، يقال : نحنُ في دعوة فلان ومَدْعاتِه ودُعائِهِ بمعنى، قال أبو عبيدة : وهذا كلام أكثر العرب إلا عَدي الرباب، فإنّهم يعكسون، ويجعلون الفتح في النسب، والكسر في الطعام. انتهى. (2) قوله : ولا يحضر دعوة إلا عامة ؛ لأنَّ الخاصة لأجل القضاء، فيتهم بالإجابة بخلاف العامة. ذلك وقيد في «السراج الوهاج» جواز حضور القاضي الدعوة العامة بما إذا كان صاحبها ممن يعتاد اتخاذها له قبل تقلّد القضاء، أما إذا لم يكن كذلك لا يحضرها لما في من التهمة، قال : وأما إذا كانت الدعوة خاصة لا يحضرها أبداً سواء كانت لقريبه أو لأجنبي عندهما ؛ لأنَّ ذلك يدخل عليه التهمة. وقال محمد : لا بأس أن يجيبها إذا كانت لقريبه . كما في «الهداية»(٢) ؛ لأنَّ في ذلك صلة الرحم، وهو مأمور بها ، فلا يمنع القضاء من ذلك. ثم بحث وقال : وذكر صدر الإسلام أبو اليسر الله إذا كان للمضيف خصومة لا يحضر دعوته العامة لما فيه من التهمة. انتهى. قال في «البحر»: الأحسنُ أن يقال : ولا يقبل هدية ودعوة خاصة إلا من محرم أو ممن له عادة، فإن للقاضي أن يجيب الدعوة الخاصة من أجنبي له عادة، باتخاذها كالهدية، فلو كان من عادته الدَّعوة له في كلِّ شهر مرَّة فدعاه كل إسبوع بعد القضاء لا يجيبه، ولو اتخذ له طعاماً أكثر من الأوَّل لا يجيبه إلا أن يكون ماله قد زاد. كذا في «التاتارخانية» انتهى. (3) قوله : العامة هي التي..الخ ؛ فالخاصة هي التي لا يتخذها صاحبها ، لولا حضور القاضي ، وقيل : دعوة العرس والختان عامة وما سواهما خاصة، وقيل: إن كانت لخمسة إلى عشرة فخاصة ، وإن كانت لأكثر فعامّة. كذا في «البحر» (1) وعند محمد له الخاصة إن كانت من قريبه يجيبه كالهدية.
He does not attend an invitation except a public one. The public invitation is that which people habitually make. (1) His statement: 'invitation'; it is said in al-Misbah al-Munir: al-da'wah with fathah in the context of food is a noun meaning you summon people to eat with you. It is said: we are at so-and-so's invitation and his summons and his call, all with the same meaning. Abu Ubaydah said: This is the speech of most Arabs except for the tribe of Adi al-Ribab, for they reverse the usage, making fathah for lineage and kasrah for food. End. (2) His statement: 'he does not attend an invitation except a public one'; because the private one is for the sake of the judge, so he is accused of responding, unlike the public one. That is restricted in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj regarding the permissibility of the judge attending a public invitation to when the host is among those who habitually make it before his assuming the judgeship; otherwise, he does not attend it because of the suspicion involved. He said: As for a private invitation, he never attends it, whether for his relative or a stranger according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad? Actually 'indahuma refers to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf?). And Muhammad said: There is no harm in his responding if it is for his relative, as in al-Hidayah; because that involves maintaining family ties, and he is commanded to do so, so the judgeship does not prevent that. Then he discussed and said: The chief of Islam Abu al-Yusr mentioned that if the host has a lawsuit, he does not attend his public invitation because of the suspicion therein. End. He said in al-Bahr: The best is to say: He does not accept a gift or a private invitation except from a mahram or from one who has a habit (of inviting); for the judge may respond to a private invitation from a stranger who has a habit, like a gift. So if it was his habit to invite him once every month, but he invites him every week after his appointment as judge, he does not respond. And if he prepared more food than before, he does not respond unless his wealth has increased. Thus in al-Tatarkhaniyyah. End. (3) His statement: 'the public is that which...' etc.; so the private is that which the host would not make unless the judge were to attend. And it is said: The wedding and circumcision invitations are public, and others are private. And it is said: If it is for five to ten people, it is private, and if for more, it is public. Thus in al-Bahr. And according to Muhammad, he may respond to a private one if it is from his relative, like a gift.
Source pages p. 432

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding a judge's attendance at invitations. The base ruling states that a judge should not attend any invitation except a public one (da'wah 'ammah). The commentator begins by defining 'da'wah' (invitation) linguistically, then explains the reasoning: private invitations are suspected to be meant for the judge's influence, so attending would create suspicion (tuhma), whereas public invitations are general and thus free from such suspicion. The condition is then refined: the public invitation must be from someone who customarily held such gatherings before the judge's appointment. If it began afterward, attendance is prohibited. For private invitations, the majority position (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) is absolute prohibition, even for relatives. However, Imam Muhammad allows attending if it is from a relative, citing the obligation of maintaining family ties (silat al-rahim). The commentary further elaborates with a distinction: a judge may accept a private invitation from a non-relative only if the host had a prior habit of inviting him. The definition of 'public' vs 'private' is also discussed: some say wedding and circumcision feasts are public, others define by number (five to ten is private, more is public). The practical consequence is that a judge must exercise caution to avoid any appearance of favoritism.

Key Terms
دعوة عامة (da'wah 'ammah): An invitation that is customary and not specifically arranged for the judge; e.g., wedding feasts or large gatherings.
دعوة خاصة (da'wah khassah): An invitation arranged for the sake of the judge, or limited to a small number, leading to suspicion.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Muhammad, under what condition may a judge accept a private invitation?
A) If it is from a close friend
B) If it is from a relative
C) If it is a wedding invitation
D) If it is a large gathering

Key Takeaway
A judge must avoid private invitations to prevent suspicion; only public or habitual invitations are permissible, and even then caution is needed.

Judge Attending Funerals and Visiting the Sick
Ruling on judge's attendance at funerals and sick visits with proof and condition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling regarding a judge's participation in funerals and sick visits and identify the condition derived from al-Zayla'i's commentary.

Core Idea
A judge's duty to attend funerals and visit the sick is a right of Muslims, but must be avoided if it creates a conflict of interest.

This passage addresses whether a judge, burdened with court duties, should still perform communal acts like attending funerals and visiting the sick. The author rules affirmatively, grounding it in the general rights of Muslims. However, a key condition from later commentary ensures the judge's conduct does not compromise impartiality. You will learn the proof and the built-in safeguard.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the act under discussion? (attending funerals and visiting the sick) What ruling does the text give? (the judge should do them, but restricted)
2
Locate the dalil
What proof is cited? The hadith from Muslim listing the rights of Muslims. This is a dalil naqli from the Sunnah.
The hadith is from Abu Hurairah, recorded in Sahih Muslim.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
How does the hadith prove the ruling? The judge is a Muslim, so these rights apply to him. The text says 'it is not dropped by being a judge'.
The hadith uses 'haqq' (right), implying a duty.
4
Note the condition from the commentary
Al-Zayla'i adds a restriction: the judge should not prolong his stay, and must avoid visiting if the sick person has a lawsuit with anyone. Why? To prevent suspicion of bias.
This is an example of istihsan or public policy (siyasa).
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
What is the practical difference? Without the condition, a judge might inadvertently create a conflict of interest. The condition ensures the administration of justice remains impartial.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويشهد الجنازة ، ويعود المريض (1) قوله : ويشهد الجنازة ويعود المريض ؛ لأنَّ هذا من حقوق المسلمين، كما روى مسلم عن أبي هريرة الله قال : قال رسولُ الله : «حق المسلم على المسلم خمس : ردُّ السلام، وتشميت العاطس، وإجابة الدعوة وهو عيادة المريض، واتباع الجنازة، واذا استنصحك فانصح له (۲) ، فهذه هي السادسة، وهو لا يسقط بالقضاء. قال الزَّيْلَعِيُّ في «شرح الكنز (۳) : لكن لا يطيل مكثه في ذلك المكان، وإن كان للمريض خصومة مع أحد لا يعوده. انتهى.
And he attends the funeral and visits the sick. (1) His statement: 'and he attends the funeral and visits the sick'; because this is among the rights of Muslims, as Muslim narrated from Abu Hurairah who said: The Messenger of Allah said: 'The right of a Muslim upon another Muslim are five: returning the greeting, saying yarhamukallah to the sneezer, responding to the invitation, visiting the sick, following the funeral, and if he seeks your advice, advise him.' Commentary
This passage provides a ruling with proof (dalil naqli) that a judge should attend funerals and visit the sick. The commentator explains that these acts are rights of Muslims, based on the hadith from Abu Hurairah listing five rights. The judge is not exempt from these duties due to his position. However, al-Zayla'i adds an important condition: the judge should not prolong his stay, and if the sick person has a lawsuit with someone, the judge must avoid visiting altogether to prevent any suspicion of bias. Thus, the judge's participation is conditional on the absence of potential conflict of interest.

Key Terms
تشهد الجنازة (tashhad al-janazah): Attending the funeral prayer and procession of a deceased Muslim.
عيادة المريض (iyadat al-marid): Visiting a sick Muslim to provide comfort and inquire about health.

Key Takeaway
A judge should attend funerals and visit the sick because they are rights of Muslims, but must refrain if there is a conflict of interest, and should keep these visits brief.

Equality Between Litigants in the Courtroom
Ruling on the judge's obligation to treat litigants equally

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on equality between litigants and apply the hadith dalil to determine the scope of the judge's obligation.

Core Idea
Equality in seating, gesture, and eye contact is wajib on the judge; inner sentiments are not punishable.

The judge's duty to treat both parties equally is a fundamental principle of Islamic judicial ethics. This lesson examines the evidence from the Sunnah and explains what equality requires and what it does not. You will learn the precise scope of this obligation and its practical implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The passage states that the judge must make equality (taswiyah) between the two litigants in seating and attention. Determine the hukm level (fard/wajib/sunnah).
The hadith command 'falisaw' indicates obligation (wajib).
2
Locate the dalil and its transmission
The proof is a hadith narrated by Umm Salamah in the Musnad of Ishaq ibn Rahawayh. Identify the type of hadith (ahad) and its strength of transmission (zanni al-thubut).
Though ahad, it is accepted as proof for this ruling.
3
Analyze the wajh al-dalalah
The imperative verb 'falisaw' implies wujub according to Hanafi usul. The hadith mentions three specific actions: seating, gesture, and eye contact.
The inclusion of gesture and eye contact shows equality is not limited to seating.
4
Determine the scope of the ruling
The passage states that the ruling applies to all litigants regardless of age, status, religion, or relationship, except when the defendant is the caliph (where standing is permissible).
Internal feelings of preference are exempt from the obligation.
5
Note the exception and its rationale
The text mentions that when the defendant is the caliph, it is appropriate for the judge to stand. This is an exception based on respect, but the equality in other aspects remains.
The passage cuts off; further details may exist in later texts.

(1) His statement: 'and he makes equality...' etc.; meaning when the disputants come, the judge equalizes them in seating and attention. Because Ishaq ibn Rahawayh narrated in his Musnad from Umm Salamah who said: The Messenger of Allah said: 'Whoever is tried with judging between Muslims, let him make equality between them in seating, gesture, and eye contact.' إقبال (iqbal): Turning one's face and attention equally toward each litigant, not showing favoritism.

A Judge's Duties: Equality Between Litigants and Muslim Rights
Rules on qadi's personal conduct and impartiality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the obligations of a qadi concerning equality between litigants and attendance of funerals/sick visits, and cite the hadith evidence and its wajh al-dalalah.

Core Idea
A judge must treat litigants with perfect equality in seating, attention, and demeanor to avoid even the appearance of bias.

This passage from the judiciary chapter (Kitab al-Qada) sets out the personal conduct expected of a judge. While a judge retains general Muslim duties like attending funerals and visiting the sick, his unique obligation is to ensure absolute impartiality between the parties before him. The text grounds this in explicit prophetic command and then unfolds practical rules to prevent suspicion of favouritism.

Guided Path
1
Identify the acts and their rulings
Note that the judge is commanded to attend funerals, visit the sick, and above all to treat litigants equally in seating and facing. Distinguish the hukm level: funeral/sick visits are sunnah mu'akkadah (not waived by being a judge), while equality is wajib (obligatory).
The equality ruling is the core of this passage; the others are reminders of general Muslim rights.
2
Locate the primary dalil
The hadith from Umm Salamah in Musnad Ishaq ibn Rahwayh: 'Whoever is tried with judging between Muslims, let him treat them equally in sitting, gesture, and glance.' Note that this is a hadith ahad (zanni al-thubut) but its meaning is clear (qat'i al-dalala).
The imperative 'falisaw' (let him treat equally) indicates obligation per Hanafi usul.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The command is explicit ('falisaw') and addressed to a judge ('bil qada'). The specific elements—sitting, gesture, glance—show that equality extends to all visible aspects of judicial conduct. The text uses this to derive the rules against whispering, hosting one litigant, etc.
This shows how a general imperative is broken down into specific applications.
4
Note the scope and exceptions
Equality applies to all litigants regardless of age, status, religion, or relation, except when the defendant is the caliph—then the judge stands, seats him with his opponent, and sits on the ground. Also note that internal preference not acted upon is not blameworthy.
The caliph exception is not a breach of equality but a gesture of respect for the office without harming the opponent's case.
5
Evaluate the tarjih and practical application
The Hanafi school treats these rules as mufta bihi based on the clear hadith and the principle that even the appearance of bias undermines justice. In modern courts, the same principle applies: physical and verbal neutrality is essential.
Any deviation must be required by extreme necessity (e.g., security concerns).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويشهد الجنازة، ويعود المريض، ويسوي بين الخصمين جلوسا وإقبالا؛ لأن هذا من حقوق المسلمين، كما روى مسلم عن أبي هريرة قال: قال رسول الله: «حق المسلم على المسلم خمس: رد السلام، وتشميت العاطس، وإجابة الدعوة وهو عيادة المريض، واتباع الجنازة، وإذا استنصحك فانصح له»، فهذه هي السادسة، وهو لا يسقط بالقضاء. قال الزيلعي في شرح الكنز: لكن لا يطيل مكثه في ذلك المكان، وإن كان للمريض خصومة مع أحد لا يعوده. انتهى. ويسوي...الخ؛ يعني إذا حضر المتخاصمين سوى القاضي بينهما في الجلوس والإقبال؛ لما روى إسحاق بن راهويه في مسنده عن أم سلمة قالت: قال رسول الله: «من ابتلي بالقضاء بين المسلمين فليساو بينهم في الجلوس والإشارة والنظر». كذا في الفتح. ولأنه إذا لم يسو في ذلك بينهما يصير منهما بالميل، وهو مأمور بالتحرز عن ذلك بأقصى ما يمكنه، وهذا فيما يكون في سعة الامتناع عنه، أما فيما لا يكون كذلك، كما إذا تمنى بقلبه أن يظهر حجة أحدهما، فهو غير مؤاخذ بذلك. كذا في المبسوط. ذكره العلامة البرجندي في شرح النقاية. وإطلاق المتن يعم الصغير والكبير، والخليفة والرعية، والدني والشريف، والأب والابن والمسلم والكافر، إلا إذا به الشيخين، بل المستحب باتفاق أهل العلم أن يجلسهما بين يديه كالمتعلم بين يدي معلمه، ويكون بعدهما عنه قدر ذراعين أو نحوهما، ولا يمكنهما من التربع ونحوه، ويكون أعوانه قائمة بين يديه. وأما قيام الأخصام بين يديه فليس معروفا، وإنما حدث لما فيه من الحاجة إليه، والناس مختلفو الأحوال والأدب، وقد حدث في هذا الزمان أمور وسفهاء، فيعمل القاضي بمقتضى الحال. كذا في الفتح وغيره. ولا يسار أحدهما؛ لأن بالمسارة He attends the funeral, visits the sick, and treats the two litigants equally in sitting and facing them. Because this is among the rights of Muslims, as Muslim narrated from Abu Hurairah that the Messenger of Allah said: 'The right of a Muslim upon a Muslim are five: returning the greeting, blessing the sneezer, accepting the invitation, visiting the sick, and following the funeral; and if he asks you for sincere advice, give it to him.' So this is the sixth, and it is not waived by being a judge. al-Zayla'i said in Sharh al-Kanz: 'But he should not prolong his stay in that place, and if the patient has a dispute with someone, he should not visit him.' End. 'And treats equally...' meaning when the litigants are present, the judge must make them equal in sitting and facing; because Ishaq ibn Rahwayh narrated in his Musnad from Umm Salamah that the Messenger of Allah said: 'Whoever is tried with judging between Muslims, let him treat them equally in sitting, gesture, and glance.' So in al-Fath. And because if he does not treat them equally in that, he becomes inclined towards one, and he is commanded to avoid that as much as possible. This applies where it is possible to abstain; but where it is not, as when inwardly he wishes for one's evidence to prevail, he is not held accountable. So in al-Mabsut. Mentioned by al-Birjandi in Sharh al-Niqayah. The general wording of the text includes young and old, caliph and subject, low and noble, father and son, Muslim and disbeliever, except when the defendant is the caliph; then the judge should stand from his place, seat him with his opponent, sit on the ground, then judge between them. He should not seat one on his right and the other on his left because the right has virtue; thus the Prophet used to single out the two shaykhs with it. Rather, it is recommended by consensus of scholars to seat them in front of him like a student before his teacher, and he should be about two cubits away from them, not allow them to sit cross-legged etc., and his assistants should stand before him. As for the litigants standing before him, that is not known, rather it happened due to necessity, and people vary in states and etiquette, and in this time many foolish things have occurred, so the judge acts according to the situation. So in al-Fath and others. He should not whisper to one of them because whispering breaks the heart of the other and creates suspicion of bias. It is restricted by saying 'one of them' because if he whispers to both or invites both, it is fine, as stated by al-Samani and others. He should not invite one as a guest because of suspicion, and because al-Hasan narrated: 'A man came and stayed with Ali, who hosted him. When he sat down, he said: I want to litigate. Ali said: Move away, for the Prophet forbade us from hosting a litigant unless his opponent is with him.' So in Manh al-Ghaffar. He should not laugh in the face of one litigant because it emboldens him against his opponent and makes him hope to sway him with a bribe. He should not joke with one litigant in the court, nor with any litigants, and should not do much joking outside court either because it destroys the dignity of judgeship. He should not prompt a litigant with an argument because that is helping one of them; for this reason it is said: it is disliked for the judge to give fatwa to litigants in a case brought to him until the case is concluded, and it is said: it is disliked to give fatwa in
Source pages p. 433

Commentary
This passage from the chapter on judiciary (Kitab al-Qada) addresses the conduct of a judge (qadi) and his obligations both as a Muslim and as an arbiter. The author first states that a judge should attend funerals and visit the sick, because these are rights of Muslims in general, as established by the hadith of five rights. The judge is not exempt from these duties by virtue of his office, though he should not prolong his stay, especially if the sick person might have a pending case. The core ruling is that the judge must treat litigants equally in seating (جلوس) and facing (إقبال), meaning he should not turn more towards one or seat one closer. The proof is the hadith from Umm Salamah commanding equality in sitting, gesture, and glance. The commentary then explains that this equality applies to all litigants regardless of age, status, or religion, except when the defendant is the caliph; then the judge should stand and seat him with his opponent. The recommended seating is both litigants in front of the judge like students, not one on right and left. The passage then prohibits several actions that could create bias or suspicion: whispering to one litigant (unless to both), hosting one as a guest, laughing or joking with one, and prompting a litigant with an argument. All these are prohibited because they undermine impartiality and the dignity of the court. The commentary also notes that if a judge internally prefers one litigant's case, he is not held accountable as long as he does not act on it. This shows the Hanafi concern for both external conduct and internal state, with the principle that only outward actions are subject to legal ruling.

Key Terms
القضاء (al-qada): The function of judging between disputants; the office of a judge.
الخصمين (al-khasmayn): The two opposing parties in a lawsuit.
المسارة (al-musarrah): Whispering secretly to one litigant in front of the other.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Give him preference in speaking.
D) Dismiss the case out of respect.

Key Takeaway
The qadi must enforce perfect outward equality between litigants, as commanded by hadith, to maintain trust in the judiciary and avoid even the appearance of bias.

Ruling on Prompting a Witness (Talqin al-Shahid)
Intra-Hanafi dispute on prompting a witness

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between permissible and impermissible prompting of a witness (talqin) according to the Hanafi school, applying Abu Yusuf's istihsan and identifying when tuhma (suspicion) changes the ruling.

Core Idea
Prompting a witness is generally disliked, but Abu Yusuf permits it by istihsan when there is no suspicion, reviving rights without corrupting testimony.

This lesson examines the Hanafi school's position on a judge prompting a witness during testimony. While the general ruling is dislike (karaha) due to the risk of falsification, Imam Abu Yusuf introduced an exception based on istihsan in cases free from suspicion. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to apply Hanafi legal theory — especially the interaction between qiyas and istihsan — in practical judiciary settings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the general ruling
Determine what talqin is (the judge prompting the witness by asking 'Do you testify to such and such?') and state the base ruling according to the matn and Abu Hanifa: it is makruh.
The general ruling is based on qiyas to talqin of litigants, which is prohibited.
2
Locate Abu Yusuf's exception
Identify the condition under which Abu Yusuf permits talqin: when there is no suspicion (la tuhma fih). This is the key qualification.
The condition 'la tuhma' is critical — it distinguishes permissible from prohibited prompting.
3
Analyze the istihsan reasoning
Understand why Abu Yusuf uses istihsan: the witness may be confused or intimidated, omitting conditions of testimony. Prompting revives the right and falls under cooperation in righteousness.
Istihsan overrides qiyas when the qiyas leads to a harsh or inequitable outcome.
4
Understand the boundaries of the exception
Recognize when talqin is prohibited by consensus: when the witness gains new knowledge from the prompting (e.g., the judge suggests a legal interpretation the witness did not know).
This is akin to prompting one of the litigants, which is unanimously impermissible.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
Identify the relied-upon position for fatwa in judiciary matters: Abu Yusuf's view, due to his greater practical experience as a judge.
Al-Qunyah and the commentator (al-Birjandi) support this tarjih.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكَرِهَ تلقينُ الشَّاهد بقوله أتشهد بكذا وكذا ، واستحسنه أبو يوسف له فيما لا تهمة فيه
[۱] المعاملات أصلاً، بل يفتي في العبادات.
(1) وقيل : يكره أن يفتي في مجلس القضاء دون غيره، والأصح أنه لا بأس بأن يفتي في المعاملات والعبادات في مجلس القضاء وغيره. كذا في مبسوط الإمام السِّرَ ذكره البرْجَندِيُّ في «شرح النُّقاية».
[1] قوله : واستحسنه أبو يوسف له فيما لا تهمة فيه ؛ لأنَّ الشاهد لاستيلاء الحيرة والهيبة قد يترك شيئاً من شرائط الشهادة، ويقول: اعلم ؛ مكان قوله : أشهد، فكأنَّ في تلقينه إحياء الحق، ولا تهمةً في مثلِهِ فكان من باب التعاون على البر؛ وذلك فيما لا تهمة فيه. أما في موضع التهمة بأن ادعى المدعي ألفاً وخمسمئة، والمدعى عليه ينكـرُ الخمسمئة ، وشهد الشاهد بألف ، فيقول القاضي : يحتمل أنه إبراء من الخمسمئة ، واستفاد الشاهد بذلك علماً فوفق به في شهادته كما وفق القاضي ، فهذا لا يجوز بالاتفاق، كما في تلقين أحد الخصمين، كما صرحوا به.
وتسمية قول أبي يوسف الله بالاستحسان دليل على أنَّ المختار عند المصنف قول أبي يوسف الله ، وقال في «القُنية» (۲) : الفتوى على قول أبي يوسف الله فيما يتعلق بالقضاء ؛ لزيادة تجربته. انتهى. وذلك لأنَّ محمداً الله وإن تولّى قاضياً بالري لكن لم يصل إلى تجربة شيخه ، فإنّه طال مدَّةٍ قضائه، وزيادة التجربة تفيد زيادة العلم، ونقل الحَموي عن مجد الأئمة التَّرْجُماني أنَّ الذي يؤيده ما ذكر في الفتاوى أنّ أبا حنيفة له كان يقول : الصدقة أفضل من حج التطوع ، فلما حج وعرف مشاقه رجع وقال : الحج أفضل.
[۱] وذلك فيما لا يستفيد بتلقينه زيادة علم.
[١]قوله : وذلـك...الخ ؛ فينبغي أن يذكر ما سمع منه ، ولا يقول : أشهد بكذا لِمَا لم يسمع منه، فإنه هو التلقين المكروه اتفاقا، أما تلقين أحد الشاهدين للآخر فغير مکروه، صرح به البرْجَندِيُّ في «شرح النُّقاية».
He disliked prompting the witness by saying, 'Do you testify to such and such?' And Abu Yusuf deemed it permissible by istihsan in cases where there is no suspicion. ... It is said: it is disliked to give a fatwa in the court of judgment and not elsewhere. And the correct view is that there is no harm in giving fatwa regarding transactions and acts of worship in the court of judgment or elsewhere. ... His statement: 'And Abu Yusuf deemed it permissible by istihsan in cases where there is no suspicion' because the witness, due to overwhelming confusion and awe, may omit some conditions of testimony and say 'I know' instead of 'I testify'. So prompting him is like reviving a right, and there is no suspicion in that, so it falls under cooperation in righteousness. That is in cases where there is no suspicion. As for a case of suspicion, such as the plaintiff claiming one thousand five hundred, the defendant denies the five hundred, and the witness testifies to one thousand, then the judge says: 'Perhaps it is a discharge of the five hundred.' The witness thereby gains knowledge and adjusts his testimony accordingly, just as the judge adjusted. This is not permissible by consensus, similar to prompting one of the litigants, as they have explicitly stated. And calling Abu Yusuf's statement 'istihsan' indicates that the preferred view of the author is Abu Yusuf's opinion. And in al-Qunyah: the fatwa is according to Abu Yusuf's opinion in matters related to judiciary due to his greater experience. This is because Muhammad, although he served as a judge in Ray, did not attain the experience of his teacher; his judgeship was short, and greater experience yields greater knowledge. Al-Hamawi narrated from Majd al-A'imma al-Tarjumani that supporting this is what is mentioned in the fatawa: Abu Hanifa used to say that charity is better than voluntary Hajj, but when he performed Hajj and knew its hardships, he retracted and said: Hajj is better. ... [1] That is in cases where he does not gain additional knowledge from the prompting. [1] His statement: 'That...' etc.; he should mention what he heard from him, and should not say 'I testify to such and such' for what he did not hear from him, because that is the disliked prompting by consensus. As for one witness prompting another, it is not disliked, as al-Birjandi explicitly stated in Sharh al-Niqaya.
Source pages p. 435

Commentary
This passage addresses the ruling on talqin al-shahid, i.e., the judge prompting a witness during testimony. The base ruling is that it is makruh (disliked) for the judge to prompt the witness by asking, 'Do you testify to such and such?' because it may lead to the witness stating something he did not hear or altering his testimony. However, Imam Abu Yusuf made an exception based on istihsan (juristic preference) in cases where there is no suspicion (tuhma) of partiality or dishonesty. The reasoning is that witnesses may become confused or intimidated in court and inadvertently omit the proper wording of testimony (saying 'I know' instead of 'I testify'). Prompting in such a case revives the right (ihya' al-haqq) and falls under cooperation in righteousness (ta'awun 'ala al-birr). The author notes that calling Abu Yusuf's view 'istihsan' indicates it is the preferred position. Furthermore, al-Qunyah states that the fatwa is given according to Abu Yusuf's view in judiciary matters due to his extensive practical experience as a judge. The passage then distinguishes between permissible prompting (where the witness gains no new knowledge) and prohibited prompting (where the witness gains new information that he did not previously have, such as when the judge suggests a legal interpretation). Prohibited prompting is akin to prompting one of the litigants, which is unanimously impermissible. Finally, it clarifies that prompting by one witness to another is not disliked, as per al-Birjandi.

Key Terms
تلقين (talqin): Prompting or coaching a witness during testimony, e.g., saying 'Do you testify to such and such?'
تهمة (tuhma): Suspicion of partiality, dishonesty, or collusion between the witness and a litigant.
استحسان (istihsan): A method of juristic preference that overrides qiyas in favor of a more equitable outcome. Here, Abu Yusuf uses istihsan to permit talqin despite the general dislike.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In which situation does Abu Yusuf permit the judge to prompt a witness?
A) When the witness is confused about the facts
B) When there is no suspicion (tuhma) of partiality
C) When the judge suspects the witness is lying
D) Abu Yusuf never permits prompting

Key Takeaway
Prompting a witness is generally disliked, but Abu Yusuf permits it by istihsan in the absence of suspicion, and this is the operative ruling for judges in the Hanafi school.

Judicial Imprisonment: Ruling and Proofs
Opening of the chapter on imprisonment with rulings and proofs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on judicial imprisonment, identify its three proofs (Quran, Sunnah, Ijma), and explain the scope of the term 'khasm'.

Core Idea
Imprisonment is a permissible judicial tool; the judge may detain a litigant for any period he deems beneficial, based on Quran, Sunnah, and Ijma.

This lesson introduces the Hanafi view on imprisonment (habs) as a judicial measure. The passage explains that imprisonment is not inherently punitive but a discretionary tool for the judge to ensure justice. The commentator provides linguistic, scriptural, and historical foundations, demonstrating the broad discretion given to the Qadi.

Guided Path
1
State the ruling from the matn
Identify the exact ruling: 'The litigant is imprisoned for a period the judge sees as beneficial.' Note the judge's discretion.
The phrase 'fi al-sahih' indicates this is the correct view within the school.
2
Identify the three legislative proofs
List the proofs: Quran (5:33 interpreted as imprisonment), Sunnah (Prophet imprisoned a man on suspicion), and Ijma (consensus of the Companions).
The Quranic proof is qati in transmission but zanni in interpretation (exile understood as confinement).
3
Understand the historical development
Note that early caliphs used ad hoc detention in mosques or hallways; Ali ibn Abi Talib built the first formal prison, named Nafi', then Mukhayyas after renovation.
This shows the evolution of imprisonment as an institution, not a change in ruling.
4
Determine the scope of 'khasm'
The term includes free debtors, authorized slaves, and minors under guardianship. However, for a minor's consumption debt, the guardian is imprisoned instead.
This exception protects minors from direct imprisonment for debts they did not incur.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في الحبس ويُحْبَس الخصم مدة رآها مصلحة في الصحيح فصل في الحبس ( ويُحبس الخصم م مدة رآها مصلحة في الصحيح) [١] قوله : ويحبس ; لَمَّا كان الحبس من أحكام القضاء، وتعلق به أحكام فشرع فيه، والحبس : لغة : المنع ، وهو مصدر حَبَس من باب ضَرَب، ثمّ أطلق على الموضع ، وجمع على حبوس مثل فلس وفلوس، كذا في «المصباح»). والحبس مشروع : بالكتاب والسنة، والإجماع: أما الكتاب ; فقوله تعالى في قطاع الطريق : أَوْ يُنفَوْا مِنَ الْأَرْضِ ) ، والمراد به الحبس. وأما السنّة ; فهو ما روي أن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم حبس رجلاً بالتهمة. وأما الإجماع ; فإنَّ الصحابة رضي الله عنهم أجمعين ومن بعدهم أجمعوا إلا أنه لم يكن في زمن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم وأبي بكر وعمر وعثمان صلى الله عليه وسلم سجن، وكان يحبس في المسجد أو الدهليز حيث أمكن، ولما كان زمن علي الله أحدث السجن، بناه من قضب، وسماه نافعاً، فنقبه اللصوص، فبنى سجناً من بدر فسماه مخيساً بالخاء المعجمة والــياء التحتانية، مأخوذاً من التخميس، وهو السند، قيل : وقال فيه شعراً : قوله ألا تراني كيساً مُكيسا بنيت بعد نافع مُخيسا حصناً حصيناً وأميناً كيسا (۳) كما ذكره، وصفةُ الحبس وما يتعلق به مذكور في «الدر المختار وحواشيه تركناه بطول الكلام، وغرابة المقام [٢]قوله : الخصم ; أطلقه فشمل الحرَّ المديون، والعبد المأذون، الصبي المحجور، فإنهم يحبسون، ولكنَّ الصبي لا يحبس بدين الاستهلاك، بل يحبس والده أو وصيه
Chapter on Imprisonment. The litigant is imprisoned for a period that the judge sees as beneficial, according to the correct [view]. [1] His statement: 'He is imprisoned' – since imprisonment is among the rulings of judicial matters, and rulings are attached to it, he legislated regarding it. Imprisonment (habs) linguistically means prevention; it is the verbal noun of habasa from the pattern daraba, then it was applied to the place, and it is pluralized as hubus like fuls and fulus, as in al-Misbah. Imprisonment is legislated by the Book, the Sunnah, and consensus. As for the Book: Allah's saying regarding highway robbers: 'Or they be exiled from the land' (5:33), and what is meant is imprisonment. As for the Sunnah: it is what is narrated that the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) imprisoned a man on suspicion. As for consensus: the Companions (may Allah be pleased with them all) and those after them agreed, except that there was no prison in the time of the Prophet (peace be upon him), Abu Bakr, Umar, and Uthman – he would imprison in the mosque or hallway as possible. When it was the time of Ali (may Allah be pleased with him), he introduced the prison, building it from rods (qadab) and named it Nafi'; the thieves broke into it, so he built a prison from baked brick (bader) and called it Mukhayyas (with khā' and yā'), derived from takhmīs (reinforcement). It is said: he composed poetry about it: 'Do you not see me clever, clever? I built after Nafi' a Mukhayyas, a strong fortress and a safe clever [fortress].' As he mentioned. The description of imprisonment and what pertains to it is mentioned in al-Durr al-Mukhtar and its commentaries; we left it due to length of discussion and strangeness of the context. [2] His statement: 'the litigant' – it is general and includes the free debtor, the authorized slave, and the minor under guardianship; they are imprisoned. But the minor is not imprisoned for a debt of consumption; rather his father or guardian is imprisoned.
Source pages p. 437

Commentary
This passage opens the chapter on imprisonment (habs) in the commentary. The matn states: 'The litigant is imprisoned for a period the judge deems beneficial, according to the correct view.' The commentator begins by explaining that imprisonment is a judicial ruling and thus the author discusses it. He gives the linguistic definition of habs (prevention) and the derived term for a prison. Then he provides the legislative basis: 1) Book: Allah's verse about bandits (5:33) where 'exile' is interpreted as imprisonment. 2) Sunnah: The Prophet (peace be upon him) imprisoned a man on suspicion. 3) Consensus of the Companions and later scholars. The commentator also notes a historical point: there was no built prison in the early caliphate; people were held in mosques or hallways. Ali ibn Abi Talib (may Allah be pleased with him) built the first prison from rods (qadab) called Nafi', then after it was breached, he built one from brick called Mukhayyas. Finally, the commentator clarifies that the term 'khasm' (litigant) includes free debtors, authorized slaves, and minors under guardianship. However, a minor is not imprisoned for a consumption debt; instead, his guardian is imprisoned. The commentator notes that further details are in al-Durr al-Mukhtar, and they are omitted here due to length.

Key Terms
الحبس (al-ḥabs): Linguistically 'prevention'; legally, confinement of a person as a judicial measure. Also used for the place of confinement (prison).
الخصم (al-khaṣm): The litigant or opponent in a legal case; here it refers to any defendant or debtor who may be imprisoned.
تخميس (takhmīs): Reinforcement or strengthening, from which the name 'Mukhayyas' for a prison is derived.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) 'And do not kill the soul which Allah has forbidden'
B) 'Or they be exiled from the land' (5:33)
C) 'And establish the prayer'
D) 'And cooperate in righteousness'

Key Takeaway
Judicial imprisonment in Hanafi fiqh is a discretionary tool based on the judge's assessment of benefit, supported by Quran, Sunnah, and Ijma.
