Imprisonment in Hanafi Fiqh: Definition and Proofs
Ruling with proof on imprisonment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on imprisonment, identify its proofs from Quran, Sunnah, and Ijma, and explain the judge's discretionary power regarding its duration.

Core Idea
Imprisonment is a legitimate judicial tool, but its duration is left to the judge's discretion, not fixed by text.

Imprisonment (habs) is a judicial measure used to compel debtors or accused individuals. The Hanafi school affirms its legitimacy based on primary sources, yet emphasizes that the duration is not predetermined—rather, it depends on the judge's assessment of benefit (maslaha). This lesson unpacks the definition, the proofs, and the scope of judicial discretion as presented in the classical text.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
Locate the statement: 'The opponent is imprisoned for a period that the judge sees as beneficial.' Note that this is the correct view (fi al-sahih).
This ruling establishes imprisonment as a permissible judicial act with discretionary duration.
2
Understand the linguistic definition
The passage defines habs as prevention linguistically, also used for the place of detention. This clarifies that the core concept is confinement, not necessarily a building.
Linguistic analysis helps avoid confusion between the act and the infrastructure.
3
Examine the first proof: Quran
The verse on highway robbers (5:33) mentions 'exile' (yufnaw). The Hanafi interpretation includes imprisonment as a form of exile because it removes the person from society.
The wajh al-dalalah is that imprisonment achieves the same effect as exile.
4
Examine the second proof: Sunnah
The Prophet (peace be upon him) imprisoned a man on suspicion. This shows the practice was established in early Islam.
Though the hadith is not quoted verbatim, its mention suffices as evidence.
5
Examine the third proof: Ijma
The Companions and later scholars unanimously agreed on the permissibility of imprisonment, even though a dedicated prison building was not needed until the time of Caliph Ali.
Ijma solidifies the ruling as a consensus of the Ummah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في الحبس
ويُحْبَس الخصم مدة رآها مصلحة في الصحيح
فصل في الحبس
( ويُحبس الخصم مدة رآها مصلحة في الصحيح)
[١] قوله : ويحبس ؛ لَمَّا كان الحبس من أحكام القضاء، وتعلق به أحكام فشرع فيه، والحبس : لغة : المنع ، وهو مصدر حَبَس من باب ضَرَب، ثمّ أطلق على الموضع ، وجمع على حبوس مثل فلس وفلوس، كذا في «المصباح»).
والحبس مشروع : بالكتاب والسنة، والإجماع:
أما الكتاب ؛ فقوله تعالى في قطاع الطريق : ﴿أَوْ يُنفَوْا مِنَ الْأَرْضِ﴾ ، والمراد به الحبس.
وأما السنّة ؛ فهو ما روي أن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم حبس رجلاً بالتهمة.
وأما الإجماع ؛ فإنَّ الصحابة رضي الله عنهم أجمعين ومن بعدهم أجمعوا إلا أنه لم يكن في زمن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم وأبي بكر وعمر وعثمان صلى الله عليه وسلم سجن، وكان يحبس في المسجد أو الدهليز حيث أمكن، ولما كان زمن علي الله أحدث السجن، بناه من قضب، وسماه نافعاً، فنقبه اللصوص، فبنى سجناً من بدر فسماه مخيساً بالخاء المعجمة والياء التحتانية، مأخوذاً من التخميس، وهو السند، قيل : وقال فيه شعراً : ألا تراني كيساً مُكيسا بنيت بعد نافع مُخيسا حصناً حصيناً وأميناً كيسا كما ذكره، وصفةُ الحبس وما يتعلق به مذكور في «الدر المختار وحواشيه تركناه بطول الكلام، وغرابة المقام
Chapter on Imprisonment. The opponent is imprisoned for a period that the judge sees as beneficial, according to the correct view. [1] His saying: 'and he is imprisoned' - since imprisonment is among the rulings of the judiciary and related rulings are attached to it, so he legislated it. Imprisonment linguistically means prevention, it is the masdar of habasa from the paradigm of daraba, then it was used for the place, and it is pluralized as hubus like fals and fulus, as in al-Misbah. Imprisonment is legislated by the Book, the Sunnah, and Ijma. As for the Book: His statement regarding highway robbers: 'or they be exiled from the land' and what is meant is imprisonment. As for the Sunnah: It is narrated that the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) imprisoned a man on suspicion. As for Ijma: The Companions and those after them have consensus, except that there was no prison in the time of the Prophet, Abu Bakr, Umar, and Uthman (peace be upon him), and they would imprison in the mosque or the hallway wherever possible. When it was the time of Ali (may Allah be pleased with him), he established a prison, built it from rods, and named it Nafi'. The thieves dug through it, so he built a prison from [bricks?] and named it Mukhayyas (with khā' and yā'), derived from takhmis, which is [?]. It is said he said poetry about it: 'Do you not see me clever, clever? I built after Nafi' Mukhayyas, a strong fortress and a faithful, clever one.' As mentioned. The description of imprisonment and what pertains to it is mentioned in al-Durr al-Mukhtar and its glosses; we have left it due to lengthy discussion and the oddness of the context.
Source pages p. 437

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof on the topic of imprisonment (habs). The author begins by stating the ruling from the matn: 'The opponent is imprisoned for a period that the judge sees as beneficial.' He then provides a linguistic definition: habs means prevention, and it is also used for the physical place of detention. The commentary then establishes the legitimacy of imprisonment from three sources. First, the Quran: Allah's command to exile highway robbers is interpreted to include imprisonment, as imprisonment is a form of exile. Second, the Sunnah: a hadith reports that the Prophet himself imprisoned a man on suspicion of crime. Third, Ijma: the Companions and later scholars agreed on its permissibility, though the physical structure of a prison was not needed until the time of Caliph Ali, who built one. The passage also notes that detailed descriptions of prison rules are in other works, omitted here. The key point is that imprisonment is a legitimate judicial tool, but its duration is not fixed by text; the judge determines what is beneficial based on circumstances.

Key Terms
الحبس (al-habs): Linguistically prevention; technically, confinement of a debtor or accused by judicial order.
الخصم (al-khasm): The opponent, i.e., the party against whom the claim is made, often the debtor.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Imprisonment in Hanafi fiqh is a legitimate judicial tool with discretionary duration, proven by Quran, Sunnah, and Ijma.

Duration of Imprisonment: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on the fixed duration of imprisonment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the different narrations on the duration of imprisonment for debt in the Hanafi school and explain why the preferred view (al-asahh) delegates the duration to the judge's discretion.

Core Idea
Hanafi school does not fix imprisonment duration; it delegates to the judge's ijtihad based on the debtor's circumstances.

The Hanafi school presents a range of opinions on the duration of imprisonment for debt, from fixed periods to complete discretion. This lesson examines those narrations and the reasoning behind the authoritative view that the judge determines the duration according to the case.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core issue
The dispute centers on whether there is a fixed duration for imprisoning a debtor or if it is left to the judge's discretion.
2
List the narrations from early imams
Abu Hanifa: 6 months (via al-Hasan) or 1 month (via al-Tahawi). Imam Muhammad: 2-3 months or 4 months.
These are transmitted opinions (riwayat) from the founders of the school.
3
Examine later scholarly positions
Some adopted the one-month narration; others used a graded system based on the debtor's wealth and behavior; many said there is no fixed duration.
Al-Marghinani reportedly used the graded approach.
4
Understand the rationale for discretion
The purpose of imprisonment is to annoy the debtor into paying, and people's tolerance varies. Hence, it is appropriate to delegate to the judge's opinion.
5
Conclude with the mufta bihi
The text states 'al-asahh' (the most correct) is that the duration is delegated to the judge's discretion.
This is the operative ruling for fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إنما قال هذا لاختلاف الروايات في تعيين مدَّةِ الحبس، والأصح أن التقدير مفوض إلى رأى القاضي ؛ لتفاوتِ أحوال الأشخاص في ذلك
فإن لم يكونا أمر القاضي ببيع ماله في دينه كذا في «البحر») نقلاً عن «البزازية». [1]قوله : لاختلاف الروايات ... الخ ؛ اختلفت الروايات في تقدير تلك المدة :
فعن محمد أنه قدرها بشهرين إلى ثلاثة.
وعنه أيضاً : أنه قدرها بأربعة أشهر.
وعن أبي حنيفة برواية الحسن : أنه قدرها بستة أشهر.
وعنه برواية الطحاوي : أنه قدّرها بشهر وكثير من مشايخنا أخذوا برواية الطحاوي. وبعض مشايخنا قالوا : القاضي ينظر إلى المحبوس إن رأى عليه زي الفقراء، وهو صاحب عيال يشكو عياله إلى القاضي البؤس وضيق النفقة، وكان ليناً عند جواب خصمه حبسه شهراً، ثم يسأل.
وإن كان وقحاً عند جواب خصمه وعرف تمرّده ، ورأى عليه إمارة اليسار حبسه أربعة أشهر إلى ستة أشهر، ثم يسأل ، وإن كان فيما بين ذلك حبسه شهرين إلى ثلاثة أشهر، ثم يسأل، وبه كان يفتي الإمامُ ظهير الدين المرغيناني، وهو يحكي عن عمه شمس الأئمة الأوزجَنْدي، وكثير من المشايخ قالوا: ليس في هذا تقدير لازم. كذا «الفتاوى الهندية» نقلاً عن «الذخيرة».
[٢] قوله : لتفاوت أحوال الأشخاص ؛ وذلك يعني لأنَّ المقصود من الحبس الضجر والتسارع لقضاء الدين، وأحوال الناس فيه متفاوتة، فالمناسب أن يفوّض الأمرُ إلى رأي القاضي.
He only said this due to the difference of narrations regarding the determination of the period of imprisonment, and the correct view is that the estimation is delegated to the opinion of the judge, because of the difference in people's circumstances in that. Then if the two [debtors?] do not have the judge order the sale of his property for his debt, as in al-Bahr, quoting al-Bazzaziyyah. [1] His saying: 'Due to the difference of narrations' ... etc. The narrations differed in estimating that period: It is reported from Muhammad that he estimated it as two to three months. Also from him that he estimated it as four months. From Abu Hanifa in the narration of al-Hasan that he estimated it as six months. From him in the narration of al-Tahawi that he estimated it as one month, and many of our shaykhs adopted the narration of al-Tahawi. Some of our shaykhs said: The judge looks at the imprisoned person: if he sees on him the clothes of the poor, and he has a family, complaining to the judge of misery and tightness of expenses, and he is gentle in his answer to his opponent, he imprisons him for one month, then asks. If he is insolent in his answer to his opponent and his rebellion is known, and he sees on him signs of affluence, he imprisons him for four to six months, then asks. If he is in between, he imprisons him for two to three months, then asks. This was the fatwa of Imam Zahir al-Din al-Marghinani, reporting from his uncle Shams al-A'immah al-Uzjandi. Many shaykhs said: There is no necessary estimation in this. As in al-Fatawa al-Hindiyyah quoting al-Dhakhirah. [2] His saying: 'Because of the difference in people's circumstances' – that is because the purpose of imprisonment is to annoy and hasten the payment of debt, and people's circumstances vary in that, so it is appropriate to delegate the matter to the judge's opinion.
Source pages p. 438

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute on the duration of imprisonment for debtors. The author explains that the reason for the various narrations is the different opinions from early Hanafi imams. Abu Hanifa reportedly said six months (in one narration) or one month (in another narration from al-Tahawi). Imam Muhammad said two to three months or four months. Many later scholars adopted al-Tahawi's narration of one month, but others gave a more nuanced view: the judge should consider the debtor's financial status and behavior. A poor, gentle debtor may be imprisoned for one month; a wealthy, insolent one for four to six months; and those in between for two to three months. Imam al-Marghinani gave fatwa based on this graded approach. Finally, many scholars said there is no fixed duration at all, leaving it entirely to the judge's discretion. The correct view (al-sahih) is that the estimate is delegated to the judge's opinion, since the purpose is to pressure the debtor to pay, and people react differently. Thus, the Hanafi position is the judge's ijtihad, not a fixed number.

Key Terms
التقدير (al-taqdir): Estimation or determination of a fixed period for imprisonment.
الروايات (al-riwayat): Different transmitted opinions from the early Hanafi imams regarding the duration.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the correct view (al-asahh) regarding the duration of imprisonment?
A) Six months
B) One month
C) Delegated to the judge's opinion
D) Three months

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school ultimately leaves the duration of imprisonment to the judge's ijtihad, as the purpose is to press the debtor and people vary.

Fixed Period vs Judicial Discretion for Debtor Imprisonment
Discussion of differing narrations on the period of imprisonment for a procrastinating debtor

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on the duration of imprisonment for a procrastinating debtor and apply the preferred view of judicial discretion.

Core Idea
The duration of imprisonment for debt is not fixed—it is delegated to the judge's discretion based on the debtor's circumstances.

In this passage, the commentator addresses a key question: how long should a judge imprison a debtor who refuses to pay? You will encounter multiple narrations from Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani, and see how later Hanafi scholars reconciled them into a single principle: the duration is not fixed but is left to the judge's assessment of the debtor's situation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the divergent narrations
List the different periods narrated from Abu Hanifa (via Hasan: 6 months; via Tahawi: 1 month) and Muhammad al-Shaybani (2-3 months, 4 months).
These narrations are all recorded in Hanafi texts.
2
Understand the reasoning behind each
Each narration likely reflects a typical scenario; the purpose of imprisonment is to cause distress to force payment.
The variations suggest sensitivity to context.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response
The most correct view is that no fixed period is binding; the judge should determine based on the debtor's condition (wealth, defiance, family).
The passage gives a detailed three-tier classification.
4
Identify the preferred view
The mufta bihi is judicial discretion, as confirmed by later scholars like al-Marghinani and the Fatawa Hindiyya.
This is the operative ruling for fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إنما قال هذا لاختلاف الروايات في تعيين مدَّةِ الحبس، والأصح أن التقدير مفوض إلى رأى القاضي ؛ لتفاوتِ أحوال الأشخاص في ذلك. فإن لم يكونا أمر القاضي ببيع ماله في دينه كذا في «البحر») نقلاً عن «البزازية»(۳). [1]قوله : لاختلاف الروايات ... الخ ؛ اختلفت الروايات في تقدير تلك المدة : فعن محمد له أنه قدرها بشهرين إلى ثلاثة. وعنه أيضاً : أنه قدرها بأربعة أشهر. وعن أبي حنيفة ه برواية الحسن الله : أنه قدرها بستة أشهر. وعنه برواية الطحاوي له : أنه قدّرها بشهر وكثير من مشايخنا أخذوا برواية الطحاوي. وبعض مشايخنا قالوا : القاضي ينظر إلى المحبوس إن رأى عليه زي الفقراء، وهو صاحب عيال يشكو عياله إلى القاضي البؤس وضيق النفقة، وكان ليناً عند جواب خصيه حبسه شهراً، ثم يسأل. وإن كان وقحاً عند جواب خصمه وعرف تمرّده ، ورأى عليه إمارة اليسار حبسه أربعة أشهر إلى ستة أشهر، ثم يسأل ، وإن كان فيما بين ذلك حبسه شهرين إلى ثلاثة أشهر، ثم يسأل، وبه كان يفتي الإمامُ ظهير الدين المرغيناني، وهو يحكي عن عمه شمس الأئمة الأوزجَنْدي، وكثير من المشايخ قالوا: ليس في هذا تقدير لازم. كذا «الفتاوى الهندية»(۳) نقلاً عن «الذخيرة»(4). [٢] قوله : لتفاوت أحوال الأشخاص ؛ وذلك يعني لأنَّ المقصود من الحبس الضجر والتسارع لقضاء الدين، وأحوال الناس فيه متفاوتة، فالمناسب أن يفوّض الأمرُ إلى رأي القاضي.
He only said this due to the differing narrations regarding the specification of the period of imprisonment, and the most correct view is that the estimation is delegated to the opinion of the judge because of the variation in people's circumstances in that regard. ... [Detailed translation of the Arabic] ...
Source pages p. 438

Commentary
This passage deals with the length of time a debtor who refuses to pay may be imprisoned. The author explains that there are various narrations from the early Hanafi imams: Muhammad al-Shaybani said two to three months, also four months; from Abu Hanifa via Hasan ibn Ziyad: six months; via al-Tahawi: one month. Many later scholars adopted al-Tahawi's narration. Some scholars detailed that the judge should look at the debtor's condition: if poor and soft-spoken, imprison for one month; if wealthy and defiant, four to six months; if in between, two to three months. Imam Zahir al-Din al-Marghinani used to give fatwa accordingly. The preferred view is that no fixed period is necessary; rather, it is left to the judge's discretion because the purpose of imprisonment is to cause distress and hasten payment, and people's circumstances differ.

Key Terms
المماطلة (al-mumāṭalah): Procrastination or delaying payment of a debt despite ability to pay
الحبس (al-ḥabs): Imprisonment by the judge as a coercive measure to enforce debt payment
التقدير (al-taqdīr): The estimation or specification of a fixed period for imprisonment

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) A period of one month as per al-Tahawi's narration

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school’s position on imprisonment for debt: duration is not fixed; the judge determines based on the debtor’s financial status and attitude.

Imprisonment for Debt: Evidence vs. Confession
Clarifying when the judge must order payment before imprisonment based on type of proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the procedural conditions for imprisoning a debtor in Hanafi law and distinguish the required steps when the debt is proven by evidence vs. confession.

Core Idea
Imprisonment for debt requires proof of procrastination: denial after evidence or refusal after order.

When a creditor demands imprisonment of a debtor, the qadi must follow a precise procedure that depends on how the debt was proven. This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya clarifies a common confusion: the type of proof determines whether a prior order to pay is needed. Understanding this distinction is essential for anyone studying Hanafi judicial procedure (qada).

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is being discussed? Imprisonment for debt. What is its legal status? It is a permissible measure (ja'iz) that becomes wajib on the judge when conditions are met.
The hukm is not absolute; it depends on the creditor's request and proof of procrastination.
2
Distinguish the two modes of proof
The passage contrasts bayyinah (evidence, e.g., witnesses) and iqrar (confession). For bayyinah, the debtor's denial itself proves procrastination (mumatalah). For iqrar, the debtor has admitted the debt, so procrastination only appears after a refusal to pay following a judicial order.
3
Understand the legal reasoning (ta'lil)
Why the difference? Imprisonment is a punishment for mumatalah (procrastination). Denial of a debt proven by evidence shows reluctance to pay. Confession without refusal does not show unwillingness—the debtor may need time or have a valid excuse.
This is the 'illa (effective cause) for the procedural distinction.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Imam al-Sarakhsi reportedly held that even in the case of evidence, the judge should order payment first, because the debtor might claim ignorance of the debt. The majority (including al-Marghinani) disagreed and held that the denial itself is sufficient.
The mufta bihi is the majority view.
5
Identify the scope of applicable debts
Imprisonment applies only to debts voluntarily undertaken (lazimah bi-'aqd), such as mahr (dower), kafalah (guarantee), and thaman mabi' (price of goods). It does not apply to debts imposed without choice (e.g., compensation for damage).
This excludes debts like arsh (injury compensation) or kharaj (land tax).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بطلب ولي الحقِّ ذلك إن أمر القاضي المقرَّ بالايفاء فامتنع أو ثبت الحق ببينة، فيما لزمه بعقد كمهر. بطلب) ولي الحقِّ ذلك إن أمر القاضي المقرَّ بالايفاء فامتنع أو ثبت الحق ببينة) : أي إن ثبت الحق ببينة ، وطلب ولي الحقِّ الحبس، يحبسه القاضي من غير احتياج إلى أن يأمر القاضي بإيفاء الحقِّ فيمتنع ، وإن ثبت بالإقرارِ لا بُدَّ أن يأمرَهُ فيمتنع ، إذ في صورة البينةِ ظَهَرَ مَطْلُهُ بإنكارِهِ، وفي الإقرارِ إِنَّمَا يَظْهَرُ المطلُ بأن يمتنع من الإيفاء بعد الأمر، فإنّ الحبس جزاء المماطلة. (فيما لزمه بعقد كمهر. [١ ] قوله : بطلب ولي الحق ذلك ؛ أن يحبس القاضي الخصم إذا طلب صاحب الحق حبسه ؛ لأنه يحبس لأجل حقه، فلا بدَّ من طلبه، سواء كان ذلك الحق قليلاً أو كثيراً، حتى ذكر في «الخلاصة» وغيرها : أن يحبس الدانق، وهو سدس درهم، ولو لم يطلب صاحب الحق حبسه لا يحبس، حتى لو قال المطلوب : احبسني، وقال ولي الحق لا بل ألازمك ، لا يحبس، كما صرحوا به (۱). [٢]قوله : أي إن ثبت الحق... الخ ؛ حاصلُهُ أن ثبوت الحق لا يخلو : ١. إما أن يكون بإقرار الخصم. ٢. أو ببينة المدعي. ففي الأول : يأمره القاضي بالإيفاء، فإن أوفى بما أقر به فيها، وإن امتنع ولم يدفع ظهر مطله فيحبسه القاضي ؛ لظهورِ مطله الذي جزاؤه الحبس. وفي الثاني : لما أنكر الخصم ذلك الحق وأثبته المدّعي ظهر مطله، فيحبسه القاضي في أول الوهلة من غير حاجة إلى الأمر بالإبقاء ؛ لظهورِ مطلبه بإنكاره ، والمحكي عنه عن شمس الأئمة السِّرَخْسِيّ عكس هذا ؛ لأنه إذا ثبت بالبينة يقول : ما علمت إلا الساعة ؛ لخلاف الإقرار ذكره في «كمال الدراية». [٣]قوله : فيما لزمه ؛ أي يحبس القاضي الخصم في كل دين لزمه بالتزامه، مثل المهر والكفالة، ألزمه بدلاً عن مال حصل له كثمن مبيع ؛ لأنَّ التزامه المال باختياره دليل
By the request of the guardian of the right, that if the judge orders the confessor to pay and he refuses, or the right is established by evidence, regarding what is binding upon him by contract like dower. ... [Detailed translation] ...
Source pages p. 439

Commentary
This passage explains the procedural requirements for imprisoning a debtor. First, the creditor must request imprisonment; the judge cannot initiate it without the creditor's demand, even if the debtor asks to be imprisoned. Second, the type of proof matters: if the debt is proven by evidence (bayyinah), the judge can imprison immediately because the debtor's denial itself shows procrastination. If proven by confession (iqrar), the judge must first order payment; only if the debtor then refuses does his procrastination become evident and imprisonment valid. The text also mentions a contrary view from Imam al-Sarakhsi regarding evidence cases. Finally, imprisonment applies to debts arising from contracts like dower (mahr) and guarantee (kafalah), i.e., debts voluntarily undertaken, not those imposed without choice.

Key Terms
البينة (al-bayyinah): Legal evidence, especially testimony of witnesses that establishes a right
الإقرار (al-iqrār): Confession by the debtor that he owes the debt
المماطلة (al-mumāṭalah): Procrastination in paying a debt despite ability; grounds for imprisonment

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, imprisonment for debt requires proof of procrastination: denial after evidence suffices, but after confession, the judge must order payment first.

Conditions for Imprisonment of a Debtor
Expansion on debtor imprisonment conditions with proof and dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for imprisoning a debtor in Hanafi law and distinguish between the procedures for debts proven by evidence versus confession.

Core Idea
Imprisonment for debt is a penalty for evasion, not inability; its rules depend on how the debt is proven and what kind of debt it is.

This passage from Sharh al-Wiqayah explains the judicial procedure for imprisoning a debtor. The key distinction is whether the debt is proved by evidence (bayyinah) or by confession (iqrar). Additionally, the type of debt—whether it is a substitute for property (badal 'an mal) or not—determines the applicability of imprisonment when the debtor claims insolvency. The text also presents an important intra-Hanafi dispute resolved by later fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two modes of proof
Note that the debt can be established either by the debtor's confession (iqrar) or by the claimant's evidence (bayyinah). These trigger different procedures.
Bayyinah typically means two male witnesses.
2
Explain the procedural difference
For evidence, imprisonment is immediate because denial shows evasion. For confession, the judge must first order payment; only refusal shows evasion.
The rationale: denial is evasion, while confession initially admits the debt.
3
Identify the types of debts subject to imprisonment
Imprisonment applies to debts binding by contract, like mahr or kafalah, and debts that are substitutes for property (e.g., sale price, loan).
The voluntary undertaking indicates apparent solvency.
4
Analyze the rational proof for presuming solvency
The text argues that a rational person does not undertake a debt he cannot pay. So the undertaking itself proves solvency, and a later claim of insolvency is not accepted for substitute-for-property debts.
This is a dalil aqli based on custom and rationality.
5
Evaluate the intra-Hanafi dispute on insolvency
Contrast the three views: always accept debtor's claim, accept only for non-substitute debts (mufta bihi), or never accept. Understand the practical consequence.
The later fatwa restricts imprisonment to substitute-for-property debts, excluding mahr and kafalah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بطلب ولي الحقِّ ذلك إن أمر القاضي المقرَّ بالايفاء فامتنع أو ثبت الحق ببينة، فيما لزمه بعقد كمهر. بطلب ولي الحقِّ ذلك إن أمر القاضي المقرَّ بالايفاء فامتنع أو ثبت الحق ببينة: أي إن ثبت الحق ببينة، وطلب ولي الحقِّ الحبس، يحبسه القاضي من غير احتياج إلى أن يأمر القاضي بإيفاء الحقِّ فيمتنع، وإن ثبت بالإقرارِ لا بُدَّ أن يأمرَهُ فيمتنع، إذ في صورة البينةِ ظَهَرَ مَطْلُهُ بإنكارِهِ، وفي الإقرارِ إِنَّمَا يَظْهَرُ المطلُ بأن يمتنع من الإيفاء بعد الأمر، فإنّ الحبس جزاء المماطلة. فيما لزمه بعقد كمهر. قوله: أي إن ثبت الحق... الخ؛ حاصلُهُ أن ثبوت الحق لا يخلو: إما أن يكون بإقرار الخصم. أو ببينة المدعي. ففي الأول: يأمره القاضي بالإيفاء، فإن أوفى بما أقر به فيها، وإن امتنع ولم يدفع ظهر مطله فيحبسه القاضي؛ لظهورِ مطله الذي جزاؤه الحبس. وفي الثاني: لما أنكر الخصم ذلك الحق وأثبته المدّعي ظهر مطله، فيحبسه القاضي في أول الوهلة من غير حاجة إلى الأمر بالإبقاء؛ لظهورِ مطلبه بإنكاره، والمحكي عنه عن شمس الأئمة السِّرَخْسِيّ عكس هذا؛ لأنه إذا ثبت بالبينة يقول: ما علمت إلا الساعة؛ لخلاف الإقرار ذكره في «كمال الدراية». فيما لزمه: أي يحبس القاضي الخصم في كل دين لزمه بالتزامه، مثل المهر والكفالة، ألزمه بدلاً عن مال حصل له كثمن مبيع؛ لأنَّ التزامه المال باختياره دليل على يساره ظاهراً، إذ العاقل لا يلتزم ما لا يقدر على أدائه، فبعد ذلك لا يسمع قوله بأنه لا يقدر على تسليمه، فيحبس، وحصول المال في يده مثبت لغناه، وزواله عن الملك محتمل، والثابت لا يترك بالمحتمل فلا يسمع إلى فقير فيحبس. واعلم أن قاضي خان قال في «فتاواه»: فإن قال الطالب: هو موسر قادر على القضاء، وقال المديون: أنا معسر، تكلّموا فيه: قال بعضهم: القولُ قول المديون: إنه معسر. وقال بعضُهم: إن كان الدين واجباً بدلاً عن ما هو مال، كالقرض وثمن المبيع، فالقول قول مدعي اليسار، يروى ذلك عن أبي حنيفة له، وعليه الفتوى؛ لأنَّ قدرته كانت ثابتة بالمبدل، ولا يقبل قوله في زوال تلك القدرة، وإن لم يكن الدين بدلاً عمَّا هو مال كان القول فيه قوله؛ لأنَّ الضمان فيه وجب بدلاً عما هو مال، كان القول فيه قول المديون، والذي يؤيد هذا القول مسألتان: أحدهما: أحد الشريكين إذا أعتق العبد المشترك وادعى أنه معسر، كان القول فيه قوله؛ لأنَّ الضمان فيه وجب بدلاً عما هو ليس بمال، والأصل في الآدمي هو العسرة. والثانية: إن المرأة إذا طلبت نفقة المعسرين، والزوج يدعي العسرة، فكان القولُ قولُ الزوج. وقال بعضُهم: كلُّ مال وجب بعقده لا يقبل قول المديون أنه معسر، وإن لم يكن ذلك بدلاً عما هو مال. انتهى. فقد علمت أن الفتوى على أنه لا يحبس إلا فيما كان بدلاً عن مال، فلا يحبس في المهر والكفالة على المفتى به، وهو خلاف ما اختاره المصنف تبعاً لصاحب الهداية». وقال في «البحر»: وذكر الطرسوسي في «أنفع الوسائل: إنه المذهب المفتى به، فقد اختلف الإفتاء فيما التزمه بعقده، ولم يكن بدل مال، والعمل على ما هو في.
By the request of the owner of the right for that, if the judge ordered the one admitting to pay and he refused, or the right was established by evidence, regarding what is binding upon him by a contract like mahr. 'By the request of the owner of the right for that, if the judge ordered the one admitting to pay and he refused, or the right was established by evidence': That is, if the right is established by evidence, and the owner of the right requests imprisonment, the judge imprisons him without needing to order the judge to fulfill the right and then he refuses. And if it is established by confession, he must order him and then he refuses. For in the case of evidence, his evasion becomes apparent by his denial. In the case of confession, evasion appears only by his refusal to pay after the order. Indeed, imprisonment is the penalty for procrastination. 'Regarding what is binding upon him by a contract like mahr.' His statement: 'That is, if the right is established...' to the end; the summary is that the establishment of the right is not free from: either it is by the confession of the opponent, or by the evidence of the claimant. In the first: the judge orders him to pay; if he pays what he confessed to, fine; if he refuses and does not pay, his evasion appears and the judge imprisons him due to the appearance of his evasion, whose penalty is imprisonment. In the second: since the opponent denied that right and the claimant established it, his evasion appears, so the judge imprisons him immediately without needing to order payment, due to the appearance of his evasion by his denial. The transmitted from Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi is the opposite of this; because if it is established by evidence, he says: I only knew now; unlike confession, as mentioned in 'Kamal al-Dirayah'. 'Regarding what is binding upon him': i.e., the judge imprisons the opponent for every debt binding upon him by his undertaking, such as mahr and kafalah, or for what is binding upon him as a substitute for property obtained by him, like the price of a sale; because his undertaking of the property by his choice is an apparent proof of his solvency, since a rational person does not undertake what he cannot pay. After that, his statement that he is unable to deliver is not heard, so he is imprisoned. The obtaining of property in his hand establishes his wealth, and its removal from ownership is merely possible, and what is established is not abandoned for what is possible, so he is not heard claiming poverty, and he is imprisoned. Know that Qadi Khan said in his 'Fatawa': If the claimant says: he is wealthy, able to pay, and the debtor says: I am insolvent, they discussed it. Some said: The statement is the debtor's that he is insolvent. Some said: If the debt is obligatory as a substitute for what is property, like a loan or the price of a sale, then the statement is of the one claiming solvency; this is narrated from Abu Hanifa, and upon it is the fatwa; because his ability was established by the substitute, and his statement about the removal of that ability is not accepted. If the debt is not a substitute for what is property, then the statement is his; because the guarantee in it became obligatory as a substitute for what is not property, the statement is of the debtor. Supporting this view are two issues: One: One of two partners who manumits a jointly owned slave and claims he is insolvent, his statement is accepted; because the guarantee in it became obligatory as a substitute for what is not property, and the origin concerning a human is insolvency. The second: If a woman seeks maintenance from an insolvent husband, and the husband claims insolvency, the husband's statement is accepted. Some said: Every property that becomes obligatory by a contract, the debtor's claim of insolvency is not accepted, even if it is not a substitute for property. End. So you know that the fatwa is that he is not imprisoned except for what is a substitute for property; so he is not imprisoned for mahr and kafalah according to the fatwa, and this is contrary to what the author chose following the author of al-Hidayah. And it is said in al-Bahr: al-Tarsusi mentioned in Anfa' al-Wasa'il that it is the relied-upon madhhab. So the fatwa differs regarding what one undertakes by contract but is not a substitute for property, and practice is upon what is in...
Source pages p. 439

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof and includes an intra-Hanafi dispute. It addresses the conditions under which a judge may imprison a debtor. The text first explains that imprisonment requires a request from the creditor. It then distinguishes between debts proven by evidence (bayyinah) and those proven by confession (iqrar). If the debt is proven by evidence, the judge can imprison the debtor immediately because the debtor's denial itself shows evasion. If the debt is proven by confession, the judge must first order the debtor to pay; imprisonment only occurs if he then refuses. This is because in the case of confession, the debtor did not previously deny the debt, so his evasion is only revealed by his refusal to comply with the judge's order. The passage then specifies that imprisonment applies only to debts that become binding by contract (like mahr and kafalah) or as compensation for property received (like the price of a sale). The reasoning is that a rational person voluntarily undertakes only what he can pay, so the undertaking itself proves apparent solvency; a later claim of poverty is not accepted. The commentator then quotes Qadi Khan's Fatawa regarding the debtor's claim of insolvency. There are three views: (1) the debtor's claim is accepted; (2) if the debt is a substitute for property (e.g., loan, sale price), the creditor's claim of solvency is accepted (narrated from Abu Hanifa, and this is the fatwa); but if it is not a substitute for property (e.g., mahr, kafalah), the debtor's claim is accepted; (3) for any debt arising from a contract, the debtor's claim is not accepted. The passage concludes that the operative fatwa is that imprisonment is only for debts that are a substitute for property, so mahr and kafalah are excluded—contrary to what the author of this commentary (following al-Hidayah) chose, but this is the later mufta bihi view.

Key Terms
الحبس (al-habs): Imprisonment by the judge as a coercive measure against a debtor who delays payment.
المطل (al-matal): Procrastination or delay in paying a debt despite ability to pay.
الإقرار (al-iqrar): Confession by the debtor acknowledging the debt.
البينة (al-bayyinah): Evidence (usually two male witnesses) presented by the claimant to prove the debt.
الإعسار (al-i'sar): Insolvency; inability to pay the debt.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In Hanafi law, when a debt is proven by the debtor's confession, when can the judge imprison him?
A) Immediately, because confession proves the debt.
B) Only after ordering him to pay and he refuses.
C) Only if the creditor requests it, and even then after ordering payment.
D) Never, if the debtor claims insolvency.

Key Takeaway
A debtor's imprisonment requires a creditor request, a debt proven by evidence or confession, and the debt must be a substitute for property to allow for any claim of insolvency.

Imprisonment for Debt: The Hanafi Distinction Between Substitute and Non-Substitute Debts
Hanafi commentary on imprisoning debtors

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between debts that are substitutes for property and other debts, and state the Hanafi ruling on imprisonment for each type.

Core Idea
The debtor is imprisoned only for debts that are a substitute for property; claims of insolvency for other debts are accepted.

This lesson explores the Hanafi position on imprisoning debtors. While a debtor who is apparently affluent is imprisoned, the school makes a critical distinction based on the nature of the debt. If the debt is a substitute for property (like a loan or sale price), the debtor's claim of inability is not accepted; otherwise, it is accepted. The passage from Umdat al-Ri'ayah presents three intra-Hanafi opinions and clarifies the mufta bihi (relied-upon fatwa) with supporting analogies.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its key distinction
Determine that the Hanafi school has multiple positions on when a debtor's claim of insolvency is accepted. The mufta bihi distinguishes between debts that are substitutes for property and those that are not.
A debt that is a substitute for property is one where the debtor received property in exchange, e.g., loan, sale price.
2
Understand the three reported opinions
List the three opinions: (1) always accept debtor's claim; (2) accept only if debt is not a substitute for property; (3) never accept for any contractual debt. Note which is attributed to Abu Hanifa and which is mufta bihi.
The second opinion is attributed to Abu Hanifa and is the basis of fatwa according to Qadhi Khan.
3
Analyze the analogical support for the mufta bihi
Examine the two cases (partner freeing a jointly owned slave and claiming insolvency; husband claiming inability to pay maintenance) that support the second opinion. Understand the illa: the original state of humans is insolvency.
These cases show that when the obligation is not based on property, the law presumes insolvency.
4
Apply the distinction to specific debts
For dower (mahr), distinguish prompt vs deferred; for suretyship (kafalah), note the exception for surety of original debtor; for maintenance (nafaqah), note that it becomes a debt only after court decree.
Prompt mahr indicates ability, so imprisonment may apply; deferred does not.
5
Evaluate the tarjih between matn and fatawa
Understand that when there is conflict, the matn and its commentaries are preferred over later fatawa, as stated in al-Bahr and referenced in the commentary.
This explains why the author (al-Hidayah) chose a different view than the mufta bihi.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على يساره ظاهراً، إذ العاقل لا يلتزم ما لا يقدر على أدائه، فبعد ذلك لا يسمع قوله بأنه لا يقدر على تسليمه، فيحبس، وحصول المال في يده مثبت لغناه، وزواله عن الملك محتمل، والثابت لا يترك بالمحتمل فلا يسمع إلى فقير فيحبس. واعلم أن قاضي خان قال في «فتاواه» : فإن قال الطالب : هو موسر قادر على القضاء، وقال المديون: أنا معسر، تكلّموا فيه : قال بعضهم : القولُ قول المديون : إنه معسر. وقال بعضُهم : إن كان الدين واجباً بدلاً عن ما هو مال، كالقرض وثمن المبيع ، فالقول قول مدعي اليسار، يروى ذلك عن أبي حنيفة له، وعليه الفتوى ؛ لأنَّ قدرته كانت ثابتة بالمبدل، ولا يقبل قوله في زوال تلك القدرة، وإن لم يكن الدين بدلاً عمَّا هو مال كان القول فيه قوله ؛ لأنَّ الضمان فيه وجب بدلاً عما هو مال، كان القول فيه قول المديون، والذي يؤيد هذا القول مسألتان : أحدهما : أحد الشريكين إذا أعتق العبد المشترك وادعى أنه معسر، كان القول فيه قوله ؛ لأنَّ الضمان فيه وجب بدلاً عما هو ليس بمال ، والأصل في الآدمي هو العسرة. والثانية : إن المرأة إذا طلبت نفقة المعسرين، والزوج يدعي العسرة، فكان القولُ قولُ الزوج. وقال بعضُهم : كلُّ مال وجب بعقدة لا يقبل قول المديون أنه معسر، وإن لم يكن ذلك بدلاً عما هو مال. انتهى. فقد علمت أن الفتوى على أنه لا يحبس إلا فيما كان بدلاً عن مال، فلا يحبس في المهر والكفالة على المفتى به، وهو خلاف ما اختاره المصنف تبعاً لصاحب الهداية»). وقال في «البحر» : وذكر الطرسوسي في «أنفع الوسائل : : إنه المذهب المفتى به ، فقد اختلف الإفتاء فيما التزمه بعقده ، ولم يكن بدل مال، والعمل على ما هو في المتون ؛ لأنه إذا تعارض ما في المتون والفتاوى، فالمعتمدُ ما في المتون، وكذا يقدَّمُ ما في الشروح على ما في الفتاوى. انتهى. فلا يردُ أنّ ما في المتن مخالف لفتوى قاضي خان. [١] قوله : وكفالة ؛ واستثنى منه في «الشرنبلالية» : كفيل أصله، كما لو كفل أباه وأمه ، فإنّه لا يحبس مطلقاً لما يلزمُ عليه من حبس الأب معه. كذا في «رد المحتار». [٢] قوله : المراد المهر المعجل ؛ لأنَّ العادة جَرَت على تسليم المعجل، فكان إقدامه على النكاح دليلاً على قدرته على تسليم المعجل، فلا يحبس في المؤجل. فإن قلت : المهر المؤجّلُ أيضاً قد التزمه بعقد، فما الفرق؟ قلت : لما علم عدم مطالبتِه في الحالِ لم يدلُّ على قدرته عليه بخلاف المعجل شرطاً أو عرفاً، وقال في «المنح»، وفي «الأصل»: لا يصدق في الصداق بلا فصل بين مؤجله ومعجّله. كذا في «البزازية». انتهى. [٣] قوله : وفي نفقة عرسه ؛ أي إذا فرض القاضي على رجـل نفـقـة زوجته ، واصطلاحا على مقدار، فلم ينفق عليها ، ورافعت إلى الحاكم، حبسه ؛ لأنه في الامتناع عن الإنفاق عليهما صار ظالما. وولده ؛ أي يحبس في نفقة ولده ؛ لأنها لإحيائه. وإنما قلنا : إذا فرض القاضي ... الخ ؛ لأنَّ نفقة الزوجة لا تصير ديناً على الزوج إلا بالقضاء أو الرضاء، فإذا مضت مدة قبل القضاء أو الرضاء سقطت عنه ، وكذا نفقة الصغير الفقير، وأما نفقة سائر الأقارب، فإنّها تسقط بالمضي، ولـو بعـد القضاء أو الرضاء، إلا إذا كانت مستدانة بأمر قاض، فلا تسقط بالمضي. كذا في «رد المحتار)، وغيره. [٤]قوله : لا في دينه ؛ أي لا يحبس القاضي الأب في دين ولده، وكذا لا يحبس
He is apparently affluent, for a sane person does not undertake what he cannot fulfill; afterwards his statement that he cannot deliver is not heard, so he is imprisoned. The possession of wealth in his hand establishes his wealth, and its removal from ownership is possible, but what is established is not abandoned due to probability; thus he is not heard claiming poverty, so he is imprisoned. Know that Qadhi Khan said in his Fatawa: If the claimant says: he is affluent, able to pay, and the debtor says: I am insolvent, they differed on it. Some said: The statement is the debtor's statement that he is insolvent. Some said: If the debt is obligatory as a substitute for property, like a loan and the price of a sale, then the statement is of the one claiming affluence; this is reported from Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him, and the fatwa is upon it; because his ability was established by the substitute, and his statement regarding the removal of that ability is not accepted. If the debt is not a substitute for property, then the statement is his statement; because the guarantee there became obligatory as a substitute for what is property, the statement is the debtor's statement. This is supported by two issues: one: one of the two partners if he emancipates a jointly owned slave and claims to be insolvent, the statement is his statement; because the guarantee there became obligatory as a substitute for what is not property, and the original in humans is insolvency. The second: if a woman seeks maintenance from her husband and the husband claims insolvency, then the statement is the husband's statement. Some said: Every property that becomes obligatory by a contract, the debtor's claim of insolvency is not accepted, even if it is not a substitute for property. End. So you have known that the fatwa is that he is not imprisoned except in what is a substitute for property; so he is not imprisoned for the dower and suretyship according to the mufta bihi, and it is contrary to what the author chose following the author of al-Hidayah. And it is said in al-Bahr: al-Tarsusi mentioned in Anfa' al-Wasa'il: It is the relied-upon madhhab. So the ifta has differed regarding what he undertook by contract and was not a substitute for property, and the practice is according to what is in the matn; because if there is a conflict between the matn and the fatawa, what is in the matn is relied upon, and similarly what is in the commentaries is preferred over what is in the fatawa. End. So it is not said that what is in the matn contradicts the fatwa of Qadhi Khan. [1] His saying: and suretyship; an exception is made in al-Shurnubalaliyya: the surety of an original [debtor], like if he stood surety for his father and mother, then he is absolutely not imprisoned due to the consequence of imprisoning the father with him. It is in Radd al-Muhtar. [2] His saying: what is meant is the prompt dower; because custom has established the delivery of the prompt [dower], so his undertaking of marriage is evidence of his ability to deliver the prompt [dower]; so he is not imprisoned for the deferred [dower]. If you say: the deferred dower also he undertook by contract, so what is the difference? I say: because it is known that he is not demanded immediately, it does not indicate his ability to pay it, unlike the prompt by condition or custom. And it is said in al-Manh: In al-Asl: he is not believed regarding the dower without distinction between its deferred and prompt. It is in al-Bazzaziyya. End. [3] His saying: and for the maintenance of his wife; i.e., if the judge imposes on a man the maintenance of his wife, and by agreement an amount, and he does not spend on her, and she complains to the judge, he imprisons him; because in refraining from spending on them he has become unjust. And his child: i.e., he is imprisoned for the maintenance of his child; because it is for his livelihood. We say only: if the judge imposes...; because the maintenance of a wife does not become a debt upon the husband except by judicial decree or consent. If a period passes before decree or consent, it is dropped from him. Similarly, the maintenance of a poor minor. As for the maintenance of other relatives, it drops with the passage of time, even after decree or consent, unless it was borrowed by order of a judge; then it does not drop with time. It is in Radd al-Muhtar and others. [4] His saying: not for his debt; i.e., the judge does not imprison the father for the debt of his son, and likewise he is not imprisoned.
Source pages p. 440

Commentary
This passage from Umdat al-Ri'ayah is a commentary on a matn (likely al-Hidayah or al-Wiqayah) discussing the Hanafi ruling on imprisoning a debtor. The author begins by stating that if a debtor appears affluent, his claim of inability to pay is not accepted, and he is imprisoned. This is because possession of wealth is established as a fact, and its removal is only possible; the established fact is not abandoned due to mere possibility. Then, the passage reports a dispute from Qadhi Khan's Fatawa: three opinions are presented. The first opinion says the debtor's claim of insolvency is accepted. The second (attributed to Abu Hanifa and the basis of fatwa) distinguishes: if the debt is a substitute for property (e.g., loan, sale price), the claimant's claim of the debtor's affluence is believed, because the debtor's capacity was established at the time of the transaction; but if the debt is not a substitute for property (e.g., mahr, kafalah), the debtor's claim of insolvency is believed. This second view is supported by two illustrative cases: (1) a partner who frees a jointly owned slave and claims insolvency—his claim is accepted because the guarantee (of the slave's value) is for something not property; (2) a husband claiming inability to pay maintenance—his claim is accepted. A third opinion says no claim of insolvency is accepted for any contractual obligation. The passage then clarifies that the relied-upon fatwa is that imprisonment only occurs when the debt is a substitute for property; thus, a debtor is not imprisoned for mahr (dower) or kafalah (suretyship) according to the mufta bihi. However, the author of the matn (following al-Hidayah) chose a different view. There is conflict between matn and later fatawa, and the principle is that what is in the matn and its commentaries is preferred. The commentary then explains key points: (1) Exception for kafalah of an original debtor (like a parent) to avoid imprisoning the surety with the principal; (2) The distinction between prompt mahr (mu'ajjal) and deferred (mu'ajjal): the prompt mahr indicates ability, so imprisonment applies; but if the mahr is deferred, ability is not indicated, so no imprisonment. Some sources say no distinction. (3) Imprisonment for maintenance of wife and child: once the judge fixes an amount and the husband refuses, he is imprisoned because he is being unjust. But note: maintenance only becomes a debt after court order or consent; otherwise it lapses. (4) A father is not imprisoned for his son's debt. The passage emphasizes the Hanafi legal reasoning, weighing capacity and probability, and shows the diversity of opinion within the school.

Key Terms
بدلاً عن مال (badalan 'an mal): A debt that is a substitute for property, such as price of a sale or loan amount.
مهر معجل (mahr mu'ajjal): Prompt dower (mahr) that is payable immediately upon marriage, as opposed to deferred dower.
كفالة (kafalah): Suretyship; a contract guaranteeing the debt of another person.
عسرة (usrah): Insolvency or inability to pay a debt.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi (relied-upon fatwa) in this passage, for which type of debt is a debtor imprisoned?
A) Any debt arising from a contract
B) Only debts that are a substitute for property (badalan 'an mal)
C) Only debts of maintenance (nafaqah) and dower (mahr)
D) Only debts that are not a substitute for property

Key Takeaway
Imprisonment for debt in Hanafi fiqh applies only to debts that are substitutes for property; for other debts, the debtor's claim of insolvency is accepted and he is not imprisoned.

Imprisonment for Debt: The Default Presumption of Poverty
Stating the general rule for imprisonment of debtors

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule on imprisonment for debt and apply the default presumption of poverty to determine when a debtor may be imprisoned.

Core Idea
A debtor is not imprisoned for non-payment unless the creditor proves wealth — poverty is the default legal presumption.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah explains a key principle in Hanafi qada: the default state (asl) of a human is poverty (usrah). Therefore, when a debtor claims inability to pay, his word is accepted with an oath, shifting the burden of proof to the creditor. We'll examine the rule, its exceptions, and the rationale based on the original state.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule
Find the statement 'In other than these mentioned things, he is not imprisoned.' Understand that this applies to all debts not specifically excepted (like diyat, arsh, etc.).
2
Understand the default presumption
The text says a human is born poor; the original state (asl) is poverty. Therefore, the debtor's claim of poverty is accepted unless contradicted.
3
Recognize the exception
The rule changes 'unless the claimant proves by evidence that he has property.' The creditor must present bayyinah to overturn the default.
4
Apply to the examples
The passage lists examples like arsh al-jinayat, compensation for usurped property, and deferred mahr. For each, the same rule applies: no imprisonment without proof of wealth.
5
Note the rationale
The rationale is that wealth is an incidental ('arid) state, while poverty is the original. The burden of proof lies on the one claiming the exception.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[١]قوله : وفي غيرها لا؛ أي في غير هذه الأشياء المذكورة لا يحبس؛ لأنَّ الآدمي يولد فقيراً لا مال له والمدَّعي يدعي أمراً عارضاً فكان القول لصاحبه مع يمينه، ما لم يكذبه الظاهر، إلا أن يثبت المدعى بالبينة أن له مالاً بخلاف ما تقدم؛ لأنَّ الظاهر يكذبه كما لا يخفى.
(۲) [٢] قوله : وأرش الجنايات؛ وبدل المغصوب إذا ثبت استهلاكه للمغصوب، ولزمه بدله من القيمة أو المثل، وبدل ما أتلفه من أمانة ونحوها، وبدل الصلح عن دم عمل، والمهر المؤجل، ونحو ذلك.
(۳) قوله : إن ادّعى؛ الخصم فقره فلا يحبس في هذه الصورة؛ لأن الأصل في الآدمي هو العسرة، والمدعي يدعي أمراً عارضاً، وهو الغناء، فلم يقبل منه إلا إذا قامت من جانب المدعي بيّنةٌ بضده، أي بضدّ الفقر وهو القدرة على الوفاء، ولو قامت بينة بمالكية عقار أو متاع، فإنه يحبس.
[1] His statement: 'And in others, no' – meaning in other than these mentioned things, he is not imprisoned; because a human is born poor, without property, and the claimant claims an incidental matter, so the statement is with the defendant with his oath, unless the apparent fact contradicts him, unless the claimant proves by evidence that he has property, unlike the previous cases because the apparent fact contradicts him, as is not hidden. (2) [2] His statement: 'And Arsh al-Jinayat' – and compensation for the usurped property if its consumption by the usurper is proven, and its substitute of value or like is due, and substitute for what he destroyed of a trust and similar, and the substitute of a settlement for blood money for an intentional act, and the deferred dower, and the like. (3) His statement: 'If he claims' – the opponent claims his poverty, so he is not imprisoned in this case; because the original state of a human is hardship, and the claimant claims a incidental matter, which is wealth, so it is not accepted unless a evidence is presented by the claimant to the contrary, i.e., contrary to poverty, which is ability to pay. And if evidence is presented of ownership of real estate or goods, then he is imprisoned.
Source pages p. 442

Commentary
This passage summarizes the general principle for debts other than those specifically mentioned in the previous lessons. The default legal presumption (asl) is that a person is poor (mu'sir) because humans are born without property. Therefore, if a creditor claims a debtor is wealthy, the debtor's claim of poverty is accepted with an oath, unless the creditor brings evidence (bayyinah) to the contrary. The examples listed include: diyat (blood money), arsh al-jinayat (compensation for injuries), compensation for usurped property, destroyed trusts, settlement for intentional homicide, and deferred mahr. For all these, the debtor is not imprisoned unless the creditor proves wealth. The key principle is that the original state is poverty, so the creditor's claim of wealth is an incidental matter that requires proof.

Key Terms
عسرة (usrah): Poverty or financial hardship; the default legal presumption for a human.
بينة (bayyinah): Evidence that proves the opposite of the default presumption.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the commentary, what must the creditor provide to have a debtor imprisoned for a debt not among the exceptions?
A) An oath from the debtor
B) Evidence of the debtor's wealth or ability to pay
C) Two male witnesses to the debt
D) A written contract

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the default presumption is poverty; a debtor is not imprisoned for debt unless the creditor proves ability to pay with evidence.
